Ruling on Value in Invalid Kitaba
Explaining the valuation rule in a fasid kitaba contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply the Hanafi valuation rule for a fasid kitaba contract: the slave pays the maximum of the named amount and his actual value.

Core Idea
In a fasid kitaba, the slave pays the greater of the named amount and his own market value.

This passage clarifies a specific financial obligation in an invalid kitaba contract. When the kitaba is fasid due to a condition like perpetual service, the contract is not entirely void; instead, the slave must pay a compensation equal to the higher of two amounts: the named sum or his own market value. Understanding this rule helps distinguish between void (batil) and voidable (fasid) contracts in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contract type and defect
Recognize that this is a kitaba (contract of manumission) that is fasid due to an invalid condition, such as perpetual service. The example given is a kitaba for 1000 dirhams on condition of serving forever.
Fasid means voidable; the contract is not completely void but its conditions are defective.
2
Understand the valuation rule
The rule: the value (qimah) due from the slave is the maximum of the named amount (musamma) and the slave's actual value. If the value is less than the named amount, the slave pays the named amount; if the value is greater, he pays the value.
This applies only when the value and named amount are of the same type (jins wahid).
3
Apply the rule to the example
Using the example: named amount = 1000, slave's value = 800 (less). The slave pays 1000. If the value were 1200, he would pay 1200.
4
Explain the underlying rationale
The rule prevents the master from being undercompensated if his slave's value is higher, and prevents the slave from benefiting from the contract's defect when his value is lower. It upholds contractual intent while correcting the invalid condition.
5
Note the source and authority
This ruling is from al-Hidayah (Zahir al-Riwayah) and is mufta bihi. It is a separate matter from earlier discussions on wine and pig.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُنْقَصُ مما سُمِّيَ وزيدَ عليه. هذه مسألة مبتدأة لا تعلق لها بمسألة الخمر والخنزير، ومعناها: أن القيمة في الكتابة الفاسدة إذا كانت من جنس المسمى، فإن كانت ناقصة عن المسمى لا تنقص عن المسمى، وإن كانت زائدة زيدت عليه. ووضع المسألة في «المبسوط» فيما إذا كاتب عبده بألف على أن يخدمه أبداً، فالكتابة فاسدة، فتجب القيمة، فإن كانت ناقصة عن الألف لا تنقص عنه، وإن كانت زائدة زيدت عليه.
And it is not reduced from what was named and increased upon it. This is a separate matter, not related to the matter of wine and pig. Its meaning: that the value in an invalid kitaba if it is of the same type as the named thing, then if it is less than the named thing, it is not reduced from the named thing, and if it is excessive, it is increased upon it. The placement of the issue in 'al-Mabsut' is in the case where he contracted a kitaba with his slave for one thousand on the condition that he serve him forever, then the kitaba is invalid, so the value is obligatory. If it is less than the one thousand, it is not reduced from it, and if it is excessive, it is increased upon it.
Source pages p. 10

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about the determination of value (qimah) in an invalid kitaba contract (kitaba fasida). The author states that the value due from the slave must not be less than the named amount (musamma) in the contract, and if the actual value is higher, it must be increased to the higher amount. This is a separate issue from the earlier discussion of wine and pig. The example given in al-Mabsut: a master contracts kitaba with his slave for 1000 dirhams on condition of perpetual service. This contract is fasid (void due to the invalid condition). In such a case, the slave must pay the value (qimah) of his own person. If that value is less than 1000, the slave must still pay the named amount (1000) because it is not reduced from the named amount; if his value is higher, he pays the higher value. Thus the slave pays the maximum of the two. This protects the master's right while ensuring the slave's eventual freedom is not completely invalidated. The ruling is taken from al-Hidayah and is mufta bihi.

Key Terms
قيمة (qimah): The monetary value of the slave's person, determined at the time of the contract or at the time the kitaba is fasid.
الكتابة الفاسدة (al-kitaba al-fasida): A contract of kitaba that is invalid due to a condition or defect, such as requiring perpetual service.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a fasid kitaba where the named amount is 1000 and the slave's actual value is 1200, what must the slave pay?
A) 1000
B) 1100
C) 1200
D) Nothing, the contract is void

Key Takeaway
In a fasid kitaba contract, the slave must pay the greater of the named amount or his actual value.

Kitaba of a Disbeliever with a Disbelieving Slave for Wine
Explaining the validity of a disbeliever's kitaba for wine and the effect of the slave's conversion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on kitaba for wine between disbelievers and explain the effect of the slave's conversion on the obligation.

Core Idea
Wine is property for disbelievers, so kitaba for wine is valid; if the slave becomes Muslim, his obligation shifts to value.

In this passage, al-Hidayah addresses a unique scenario: a dhimmi master contracts kitaba with his dhimmi slave for a specified amount of wine. Although wine is impure and prohibited for Muslims, it is considered property (mal) for disbelievers. The ruling explores what happens if the slave embraces Islam before the contract is fully executed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and consideration
The act: a disbeliever (dhimmi) contracts kitaba with his disbelieving slave for a specified amount of wine. Determine the legal status of wine in this context.
Wine is mal for non-Muslims, akin to vinegar for Muslims.
2
Determine validity of the contract
State the ruling: the kitaba is valid (sahih). Identify the proof: wine is property for them, and kitaba is a valid contract.
The Hanafi school relies on the principle that non-Muslims can transact in what they consider property.
3
Analyze effect of slave's conversion
If the slave becomes Muslim, he cannot deliver wine. The ruling shifts the obligation to the value (qimah) of the wine. Freedom is still contingent upon the master's receipt of the wine (or value).
This is by istihsan, analogizing to kitaba for service where value can substitute.
4
Distinguish from sale
Compare with a sale of wine between dhimmis where one converts: the sale becomes invalid according to some. In kitaba, value can substitute, so the contract remains valid.
The difference lies in the nature of the contract: kitaba allows substitution by value; sale does not.
5
Consider the exception of a Muslim slave
If the slave is Muslim at the time of the contract, kitaba for wine is invalid because a Muslim cannot undertake wine. However, if he pays it, he is freed by istihsan.
This shows the Hanafi flexibility in applying istihsan to achieve the aim of manumission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي كافر كاتب عبداً مثله بخمر مقدّرة صح. وأي أسلم لسيده قيمتها، وعتق بقبض الخمر. لأن عتقه متعلق بقبضها، لكن مع ذلك يجب القيمة. وقيل: إنها مال في حقهم، بمنزلة الخل في حقنا. وقيل: قيمة الخمر؛ لأن المسلم ممنوع عن تمليك الخمر وتملكها، وفي التسليم ذلك، إذ الخمر غير متعين، فيعجز عن تسليم البدل، فيجب عليه قيمته. وهذا بخلاف ما إذا تبايع الذميان خمراً ثم أسلم أحدهما حيث يفسد البيع على ما قاله البعض؛ لأن القيمة تصلح بدلاً في الكتابة في الجملة، فإنه لو كاتب على عبد الخدمة وأتى بالقيمة، يجبر على القبول، فجاز أن يبقى العقد على القيمة، أما البيع لا ينعقد صحيحاً على القيمة، فافترقا. وعتق بقبض الخمر؛ لأن في الكتابة معنى المعاوضة، فإذا وصل أحد العوضين إلى المولى سلم العوض الآخر للعبد، وذلك بالعتق. بخلاف ما إذا كان العبد مسلماً حيث لم تجز الكتابة؛ لأن المسلم ليس من أهل التزام الخمر، ولو أدّاها عتق كما مر.
And regarding a disbeliever who contracted kitaba with a slave like him for a specified amount of wine: it is valid. And if [the slave] becomes Muslim, then upon his master is its value, and he becomes free upon receipt of the wine. Because his freedom is dependent on receipt of it, but nonetheless the value is obligatory. And it is said: it is property in their regard, like vinegar in our regard. And it is said: the value of the wine, because the Muslim is prohibited from giving and owning wine, and in delivery of it, since wine is non-specific, he is unable to deliver the substitute, so he must pay its value. And this is different from the case where two dhimmis trade wine, then one becomes Muslim, where the sale becomes invalid according to some; because value can serve as a substitute in kitaba in general, for if he contracted kitaba for the slave's service and brought the value, he is compelled to accept, so it is permissible for the contract to remain on value. As for sale, it cannot be validly contracted on value, so they differ. And he becomes free upon receipt of the wine because in kitaba there is the meaning of exchange; when one of the counter-values reaches the master, he hands over the other counter-value to the slave, which is freedom. This is different from the case where the slave is Muslim, where kitaba is not valid, because a Muslim is not competent to undertake wine; but if he pays it, he is freed as mentioned.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage rules on a specific case: a disbeliever (dhimmi) contracts kitaba with his disbelieving slave for a specified quantity of wine (e.g., 100 pounds). This contract is valid because wine is considered property (mal) for them, just as vinegar is for Muslims. If the slave later becomes Muslim, he must pay the value (qimah) of the wine to his master, because a Muslim cannot own or deliver wine. However, freedom is still contingent upon the master actually receiving the wine (or its equivalent), and the slave becomes free upon the master's receipt of the wine (or the value). The value is obligatory because the Muslim slave cannot deliver wine. This is distinguished from a sale of wine between dhimmis where one becomes Muslim: that sale becomes invalid (according to some) because sale cannot be transformed into a value obligation. In kitaba, however, value is accepted as a substitute due to istihsan (e.g., if kitaba is for service, the slave may pay value). Also, if the slave is Muslim from the start, the kitaba is invalid because a Muslim cannot commit to wine; but if he nonetheless pays the wine, he is freed (by istihsan).

Key Terms
خمر (khamr): Wine, considered property (mal) for disbelievers in Hanafi fiqh, though impure and prohibited for Muslims.
قيمة (qimah): Monetary value of the wine, to be paid by the Muslim slave since he cannot deliver wine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a dhimmi contracts kitaba with his dhimmi slave for 100 pounds of wine, and the slave later becomes Muslim, what is the slave obligated to pay?
A) 100 pounds of wine
B) The value of the wine in money
C) Nothing, the contract is void
D) He must be manumitted without payment

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, wine is property for disbelievers, so kitaba for wine is valid; upon the slave's conversion, the obligation to pay value substitutes for delivery of wine.

Dispositions of the Mukatab: Validity and Invalid Conditions
Super-commentary clarifying the effect of invalid conditions in kitaba

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mukatab's sale, purchase, and travel, and apply the distinction between conditions that invalidate kitaba and those that do not.

Core Idea
A mukatab's sale, purchase, and travel remain valid even if the master conditions otherwise, because only conditions in the core contract consideration corrupt kitaba.

This passage from a super-commentary on al-Wiqaya clarifies the scope of a mukatab's autonomy. The key question: what happens when a master stipulates a condition that contradicts the purpose of kitaba, such as prohibiting travel? The Hanafi school resolves this through istihsan, drawing an analogy to emancipation. Understanding which conditions invalidate the contract and which do not is essential for correctly applying the law of kitaba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mukatab's valid dispositions
From the opening sentence, note that the mukatab's sale, purchase, and travel are ruled valid (sahih). This includes situations where the master conditions the opposite.
The ruling covers all ordinary commercial acts needed for the mukatab to earn money for his freedom.
2
Understand the concept of 'condition contradicting the contract'
The text gives an example: if the master stipulates 'do not travel', this condition contradicts the requirement of the contract (muqtada al-aqd), which is autonomous control (malikiyyat al-yad). Such conditions are invalid, but do they nullify the kitaba?
The contract's requirement is that the mukatab can freely pursue earning; travel may be necessary.
3
Apply istihsan to preserve the contract
The Hanafi response uses istihsan: despite the invalid condition, the mukatab may still travel, and the kitaba remains intact. The reason is that the condition does not affect the core consideration ('iwad) or its equivalent.
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas that might otherwise nullify the contract.
4
Distinguish between types of conditions
Not all invalid conditions have the same effect. A condition that inserts an unknown service into the kitaba (e.g., 'serve me for an unknown period') invalidates it because it corrupts the consideration. A condition like 'do not travel' does not relate to the consideration and is thus non-invalidating.
This distinction is critical for determining the validity of the kitaba.
5
Evaluate the opposing view of Zufar and al-Shafi'i
Zufar (within Hanafi) and al-Shafi'i held that any invalid condition corrupts the kitaba, analogizing it to a sale contract. The Hanafi response: kitaba is dual-natured—it resembles both sale and emancipation. Conditions on the slave's side are treated as part of emancipation, which is not invalidated by a flaw.
The dual resemblance (shabah) is a key usul concept used to differentiate rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب تصرف المكاتب
صح بيعه وشراؤه وسفره ، وإن شرط ضده) ، فإِنَّهُ إِن شرط أن لا يسافر فله السفر استحساناً ؛ لأنه شرط مخالف لمقتضى العقد، وهو مالكية اليد، ولا تفسد الكتابة بهذا الشرط، فإنَّ الكتابة تُشبه البيع ، ومع ذلك هي إعتاق بالنظر إلى العبد، فقلنا : كل شرط مفسد يكون في أحد البدلين، كما إذا شرط خدمة مجهولة تفسدها ، وكلُّ شرط لا يكون كذلك لا يفسدها عملاً بالشبهين. (وإنكاح امتِه ، وكتابة عبده ؛ لأنهما يفيدان المال)، وعند زفر والشافعي (1) أجاب عنه بقوله : ولا تفسد الكتابة ؛ يعني أنّ الشرط الباطل إنما يبطل الكتابة إذا تمكن في صلب العقد، كما إذا قال : كاتبتك على أن تخدمني مدة أو زماناً ، وشرط عدم السفر ليس كذلك ؛ لأنه لا شرط في بدل الكتابة، ولا فيما يقابله، فلا يفسد به الكتابة، ثم أشار إلى تفصيل المقام بقوله : فإنَّ الكتابة... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الكتابة تشبه البيع من حيث المعاوضة وعدم صحتها بلا بدل واحتمالهما الفسخ قبل الأداء، ويشبه النكاح من حيث أنهما معاوضة مال بغير مال. وقوله : ومع ذلك هي إعتاق بالنظر إلى العبد ؛ إشارة إلى ما ذكره الأكمل بقوله : أو نقول : إن الكتابة في جانب العبد إعتاق ؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق إزالة الملك لا إلى أحد ، والكتابة كذلك ؛ لأنه لا يحصل للمكاتب شيء، وإنّما يسقط عنه ملك مولاه، وكلُّ شرط يختص بجانب العبد فهو داخل في الإعتاق ؛ لدخوله في الكتابة، وهذا الشرط يختص بجانب العبد، فهو داخل في الإعتاق، والإعتاقُ لا يبطل بالشرط الفاسد. انتهى.
كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [١] قوله : وكلّ شرط ؛ لا يكون كذلك كاشتراط أن لا يبيع نسيئة، أو أن لا يخرج من البلد، أو أن لا يبيع ولا يشتري عن فلان، وغير ذلك.
[٢] قوله : لأنهما يفيدان المال ؛ فإنّه بالإنكاح يتملك المهر فدخل تحت عقد الكتابة، وكذا بكتابة عبده يتملك بدل الكتابة، فدخل تحت العقد.
(۱) ينظر: «النكت» (ص٧٤٨)، وغيرها. (۲) «ذخيرة العقبی» (ص ٥٢٥ - ٥٢٦). (۳) ينظر: «الذخيرة»(ص٥٢٦).
Chapter on the dispositions of the mukatab. His sale and purchase and travel are valid, even if he conditions the opposite. So if he conditions that he should not travel, he has the right to travel by istihsan, because it is a condition that contradicts the requirement of the contract, which is ownership of control. And the kitaba is not corrupted by this condition. Indeed, kitaba resembles sale, yet it is emancipation with respect to the slave. So we say: every condition that corrupts is in one of the two considerations, like when one conditions unknown service that corrupts it, and every condition that is not like that does not corrupt it, acting upon the two resemblances. (And marrying off his slave-girl and writing kitaba for his slave; because they bring wealth.) And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i... He answered about it with his saying: And the kitaba is not corrupted; meaning that an invalid condition only invalidates the kitaba if it is inserted into the core of the contract, as if he says: I have written kitaba for you on condition that you serve me for a period or time, and the condition of not traveling is not like that, because it is neither a condition on the consideration of the kitaba nor on what is in exchange for it, so the kitaba is not corrupted by it. Then he pointed to the detail of the matter with his saying: Indeed, kitaba resembles sale from the perspective of exchange and not being valid without consideration and the possibility of rescission before payment, and it resembles marriage from the perspective that both are exchange of property for non-property. And his saying: Yet it is emancipation with respect to the slave, is an indication of what al-Akmal mentioned with his saying: Or we say: Indeed, kitaba with respect to the slave is emancipation, because emancipation is removal of ownership without transfer to anyone, and kitaba is like that, because the mukatab does not acquire anything, rather the ownership of his master is removed from him, and every condition that pertains to the side of the slave enters into emancipation, because it enters into the kitaba, and this condition pertains to the side of the slave, so it enters into emancipation, and emancipation is not invalidated by an invalid condition. End. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [1] His saying: 'every condition that is not like that' includes like stipulating not to sell on credit, not to leave the town, not to sell or buy from so-and-so, and others. [2] His saying: 'because they bring wealth', because by marrying (his slave-girl) he obtains the dower, so it enters under the contract of kitaba, and similarly by writing kitaba for his slave he obtains the kitaba payment, so it enters under the contract.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a super-commentary (hashiya) on the chapter of the dispositions of the mukatab from a sharh of al-Wiqaya. It begins by stating the ruling: the sale, purchase, and travel of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted kitaba with his master) are valid, even if the master conditions the opposite. For example, if the master stipulates that the mukatab may not travel, the mukatab may still legally travel, based on istihsan (juristic preference). The reasoning is that such a condition contradicts the requirement of the kitaba contract, which is that the mukatab has autonomous control over his person and earnings. Since the contract aims at enabling the mukatab to earn money to buy his freedom, travel may be necessary for that purpose. The contract resembles both sale (due to the monetary exchange) and emancipation (with respect to the slave's side). Consequently, an invalid condition that does not affect the core of the contract—i.e., the consideration (badal) or its equivalent—does not invalidate the kitaba contract itself. The text contrasts this with a condition of unknown service, which would invalidate the kitaba because it relates to the consideration. The passage then addresses a dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who apparently held that an invalid condition could corrupt the kitaba. The commentator explains that only conditions inserted into the core of the contract (e.g., 'I make kitaba with you on condition that you serve me for an unknown period') invalidate it, whereas a condition restricting travel does not fall into that category. The passage further explains the dual nature of kitaba: it is a sale-like exchange from one perspective (it requires a consideration and can be rescinded before payment) and an emancipation from another (it removes the master's ownership without transferring ownership to the slave). Because of this resemblance to emancipation, conditions that pertain only to the slave's side are treated as part of the emancipation, and invalid conditions in emancipation do not nullify the act. The text then states that the mukatab also has the right to marry off his slave-girl and to write kitaba for his own slave, because these actions generate wealth that contributes to his ability to pay the kitaba. The commentary concludes with examples of conditions that are not invalidating, such as stipulations not to sell on credit, not to leave the city, or not to deal with a specific person.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitaba with his master, agreeing to pay a sum in exchange for eventual freedom.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract between a master and slave whereby the slave earns money to purchase his freedom.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departing from strict analogy (qiyas) for a more suitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a master stipulates that the mukatab may not travel, what is the ruling?
A) The kitaba becomes invalid.
B) The condition is valid and the mukatab must comply.
C) The condition is invalid but the kitaba remains valid; the mukatab may travel.
D) The kitaba is valid only if the mukatab agrees to the condition.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between conditions that corrupt kitaba (those affecting consideration) and those that do not (those affecting the slave's side), using istihsan and the dual resemblance to sale and emancipation.

Effect of Invalid Conditions in the Kitabah Contract
Explaining which conditions invalidate the mukatab contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on conditions in the kitabah contract and apply the distinction between conditions affecting the badal and those affecting the slave's side.

Core Idea
Only conditions in the consideration (badal) invalidate kitabah; conditions on the slave's side are void but do not break the contract.

The kitabah contract is a unique hybrid: it exchanges money for eventual freedom, resembling both sale and manumission. This dual nature determines which conditions are fatal. In this lesson, you will learn how Hanafi jurists derive the rule that invalidating conditions must concern the monetary consideration, while other conditions—like a travel ban—leave the contract intact.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The kitabah contract is valid (sahih) unless a condition is placed on the badal (the payment). Conditions on the slave's side, like not traveling, do not invalidate.
The text gives examples: stipulating unknown service invalidates; stipulating not to sell on credit does not.
2
Locate the dalil
The ruling is based on qiyas (analogy). Kitabah resembles sale in requiring a known consideration, so conditions in the badal are treated like in sale—they invalidate. It also resembles manumission from the slave's perspective, where conditions on the slave are irrelevant to the essence of freeing.
This is a jaliy (clear) qiyas because the resemblance is explicitly articulated.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The operative cause (illa) is the dual nature: from the master's side it is a sale, from the slave's side it is manumission. Therefore, conditions that affect the monetary consideration (like unknown service) corrupt the sale aspect; conditions that only affect the slave (like travel restrictions) fall under manumission, which tolerates corrupt conditions.
Al-Akmal states: manumission is not invalidated by a corrupt condition.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Zufar and al-Shafi'i agree with the outcome but for different reasons. They hold that only conditions embedded in the core of the contract (like 'serve me for a period') invalidate, not peripheral conditions like not traveling. The Hanafi school uses the dual resemblance as its proof.
The text explicitly contrasts the Hanafi reasoning with that of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is relied upon (mufta bihi) because it comprehensively covers both exchange and manumission aspects. Conditions not in the badal are considered mere recommendations, not contract-breakers.
The authority weight is zahir al-riwayah, as it appears in the core Hanafi works.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه
وإنكاح أمتِه ، وكتابة عبده
(۱)
تفسد الكتابة بهذا الشرط، فإنَّ الكتابة تُشبه البيع ، ومع ذلك هي إعتاق بالنظر إلى العبد، فقلنا : كل شرط مفسد يكون في أحد البدلين، كما إذا شرط خدمة مجهولة تفسدها ، وكلُّ شرط لا يكون كذلك لا يفسدها عملاً بالشبهين. (وإنكاح امتِه ، وكتابة عبده ؛ لأنهما يفيدان المال"، وعند زفر والشافعي (1) أجاب عنه بقوله : ولا تفسد الكتابة ؛ يعني أنّ الشرط الباطل إنما يبطل الكتابة إذا تمكن في صلب العقد، كما إذا قال : كاتبتك على أن تخدمني مدة أو زماناً ، وشرط عدم السفر ليس كذلك ؛ لأنه لا شرط في بدل الكتابة، ولا فيما يقابله، فلا يفسد به الكتابة، ثم أشار إلى تفصيل المقام بقوله : فإنّ الكتابة... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الكتابة تشبه البيع من حيث المعاوضة وعدم صحتها بلا بدل واحتمالهما الفسخ قبل الأداء، ويشبه النكاح من حيث أنهما معاوضة مال بغير مال.
وقوله : ومع ذلك هي إعتاق بالنظر إلى العبد ؛ إشارة إلى ما ذكره الأكمل بقوله : أو نقول : إن الكتابة في جانب العبد إعتاق ؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق إزالة الملك لا إلى أحد ، والكتابة كذلك ؛ لأنه لا يحصل للمكاتب شيء، وإنّما يسقط عنه ملك مولاه، وكلُّ شرط يختص بجانب العبد فهو داخل في الإعتاق ؛ لدخوله في الكتابة، وهذا الشرط يختص بجانب العبد، فهو داخل في الإعتاق، والإعتاقُ لا يبطل بالشرط الفاسد. انتهى.
كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [١] قوله : وكلّ شرط ؛ لا يكون كذلك كاشتراط أن لا يبيع نسيئة، أو أن لا يخرج من البلد، أو أن لا يبيع ولا يشتري عن فلان، وغير ذلك.
[٢] قوله : لأنهما يفيدان المال ؛ فإنّه بالإنكاح يتملك المهـر فـدخـل تحـت عـقـد الكتابة، وكذا بكتابة عبده يتملك بدل الكتابة، فدخل تحت العقد.
(۱) ينظر: «النكت» (ص٧٤٨)، وغيرها. (۲) «ذخيرة العقبی» (ص ٥٢٥ - ٥٢٦). (۳) ينظر: «الذخيرة»(ص٥٢٦).
The book of the Mukatab: his transactions, his incapacity, and his rulings. (He may) marry his female slave and write a kitabah for his slave. (1) The kitabah is invalidated by this condition, for the kitabah resembles sale, and at the same time it is manumission with respect to the slave. So we say: every invalidating condition is in one of two considerations, just as if he stipulates unknown service, it invalidates it. And every condition that is not like that does not invalidate it, acting on both resemblances. (And he may marry his female slave and write a kitabah for his slave) because they yield money. And according to Zufar and al-Shafi'i, (1) (the author) answered on his behalf by saying: The kitabah is not invalidated; meaning that the invalid condition only invalidates the kitabah if it is embedded in the core of the contract, as if he said: I make a kitabah with you on condition that you serve me for a period or time, but the condition of not traveling is not like that, because it is not a condition in the consideration of the kitabah nor in its equivalent, so the kitabah is not invalidated by it. Then he pointed to the detail of the matter by saying: For the kitabah... etc., meaning that the kitabah resembles sale in terms of exchange and its lack of validity without consideration and both being subject to cancellation before payment, and it resembles marriage in that both are an exchange of property for non-property. And his statement: And at the same time it is manumission with respect to the slave is an indication of what al-Akmal mentioned by saying: Or we say: The kitabah on the slave's side is manumission, because manumission is the removal of ownership without giving to anyone, and the kitabah is likewise, because nothing is acquired for the mukatab, rather the ownership of his master falls away from him. And every condition specific to the slave's side is included in the manumission because it enters into the kitabah, and this condition is specific to the slave's side, so it is included in the manumission, and manumission is not invalidated by a corrupt condition. End. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi [1] His statement: And every condition that is not like that, such as stipulating that he not sell on credit, or that he not leave the city, or that he not buy or sell from so-and-so, and others. [2] His statement: because they yield money; for by the marriage he possesses the dower, so it enters under the contract of kitabah, and likewise by writing a kitabah for his slave he possesses the consideration of the kitabah, so it enters under the contract. (1) See: al-Nukat, p. 748, and others. (2) Dhakirat al-Uqba, pp. 525-526. (3) See: al-Dhakhira, p. 526.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage from the book of the Mukatab discusses the effect of conditions in the kitabah contract. The author explains that the kitabah contract is similar to sale in that it requires a consideration, but also resembles manumission from the slave's perspective. Therefore, conditions that affect the consideration (such as unknown service) invalidate the contract, while conditions that only affect the slave's side (like not traveling) do not invalidate it. The passage also addresses the permissibility of the mukatab marrying his female slave or writing a kitabah for his own slave because these acts bring money. It then contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, who also hold that the condition of not traveling does not invalidate the kitabah, but on different reasoning. The Hanafi response emphasizes that conditions not in the consideration do not invalidate the contract. The commentary further elaborates on the analogy to sale and manumission, citing al-Akmal, and provides examples of conditions that do not invalidate.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitabah): A contract between a master and his slave whereby the slave becomes free upon payment of a specified sum.
البدل (al-badal): The consideration (payment) in the kitabah contract.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition stipulated in the contract; may affect validity depending on its nature.
الإعتاق (al-i'taq): Manumission; the act of freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following conditions invalidates a kitabah contract?
A) Stipulating that the slave must not travel to a specific city
B) Stipulating that the slave must not sell on credit
C) Stipulating that the slave must perform an unknown service
D) Stipulating that the slave must not buy from a certain person

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a condition in a kitabah contract invalidates only if it concerns the consideration (badal); otherwise it is void but harmless, due to the contract's dual nature as both a sale and a manumission.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Mukatab's Legal Capacity: Kitabah, Wala', and Transactional Restrictions
Explains mukatab's kitabah, wala', and transactional restrictions with istihsan reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on a mukatab's kitabah contract with his own slave using istihsan, and state the restrictions on his transactions including marriage.

Core Idea
A mukatab's kitabah with his slave is valid by istihsan, but he cannot marry or give gifts without permission.

This passage from the commentary on Sharh al-Wiqayah clarifies the legal capacity of a mukatab (a slave under a contract of emancipation). The key issues are: can a mukatab himself contract kitabah with his own slave? And what transactions is he allowed to perform while under the kitabah? The Hanafi school answers these using the principle of istihsan, balancing the interests of the mukatab, the master, and the second slave. We will walk through the reasoning for the validity of the kitabah, the rules of clientage (wala'), and the specific prohibitions on marriage and gifts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
The passage opens with the ruling that a mukatab cannot contract kitabah with his own slave by qiyas, but then overrides it with istihsan. Your first step is to identify this pivot: why does qiyas invalidate it, and what consideration in istihsan reverses that?
Qiyas says: kitabah leads to emancipation, but a mukatab cannot cause emancipation because he is not fully free. Istihsan says: it is a wealth-acquisition contract, and emancipation is attributed to the master.
2
Trace the wala' distribution
The passage explains who gets the wala' (clientage) of the second mukatab: if the second pays after the first becomes free, the first mukatab gets it; if before, the master gets it. Understand the reasoning: the direct causer of emancipation gets wala', unless he lacks eligibility.
Consider the case where both pay simultaneously: wala' goes to the master—why? Because the first's eligibility is incomplete.
3
List the prohibited transactions
The passage lists actions the mukatab cannot do without the master's permission: marriage, gift-giving (except small charity), suretyship, lending, and emancipating his own slave. Identify the common thread: they are gratuitous or impose financial burdens on the master.
Even transactions with compensation (like selling a slave for money) are prohibited if they are initially gratuitous (like a gift for compensation).
4
Analyze the marriage dispute
There is a difference of opinion between Shaykh al-Islam (Abu Hanifa) and Zufar regarding the mukatabah's marriage without permission. Abu Hanifa forbids it; Zufar allows it as a form of acquisition. Follow the Hanafi response: the mahr belongs to the master, not the mukatabah, so it does not help her pay the kitabah.
If she marries without permission and becomes free before annulment, the marriage becomes valid automatically.
5
Summarize the underlying legal principle
All restrictions stem from the mukatab's limited legal capacity: he owns his earnings but not capital, and his transactions are tested by whether they help him earn the kitabah payment or burden the master. Istihsan allows kitabah as a beneficial wealth-acquisition contract despite the analogy that would invalidate it.
This reflects the Hanafi commitment to flexibility (istihsan) over rigid qiyas when social and economic benefit demands it.

Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. And his clientage belongs to him if he pays after his emancipation, and to his master if he pays before it. Do not marry him except with his permission, nor give a gift from him. The contract of kitabah is not valid, and that is by qiyas, because it leads to emancipation, and he is not qualified for it. The basis of istihsan: it is a means of acquiring wealth, and his emancipation is attributed to the master. And his clientage belongs to him if he pays his emancipation, and to his master if he pays before it. That is: the first mukatab has the clientage of the second if the second pays after the emancipation of the first, and to his master if he pays before it. Do not marry him except with his permission, nor give a gift from him. [0] After [1] He said: The basis of istihsan... etc. Its clarification: each of them is a contract of wealth acquisition, so he owns it like a sale. Sometimes the contract of kitabah is more beneficial for him than a sale, because it does not remove ownership until the consideration reaches him, while a sale removes it before. For this reason, the father and the executor own it. [2] He said: If he pays after his emancipation, because the contracting party, i.e., the first mukatab, is qualified to establish clientage, and that is the principle, because he directly effects the emancipation, so clientage is established for him. [3] He said: And to his master if he pays before it, because he has a kind of ownership over him, and attributing the emancipation to him is valid in general. If it becomes impossible to attribute it to the direct contracting party, i.e., the first mukatab, due to lack of eligibility, it is attributed to him, just as when a ma'dhun slave buys something, ownership is established for the master. So too this. Thus in Al-Hidayah. [4] He said: If the second pays after the emancipation of the first... etc. As for if both pay their consideration together, then their clientage belongs to the master, giving preference to the original. If the first is unable to pay and is returned to slavery, and the second has not paid his consideration, he remains a mukatab. If he pays the consideration to the master, he is emancipated. If he is unable, he is returned to slavery like the first. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Ghurar. [0] He said: Do not marry him except with his permission, because it is not of the type of acquisition, and it involves harm to the master due to the establishment of mahr and maintenance in his neck. He only benefits from the contract of kitabah in what is a means to pay its consideration, and this is not a means to pay the consideration, so he remains under restriction. If the master gives him permission, it becomes valid due to the removal of restriction then. This applies to the mukatab. As for the mukatabah, is it permissible for her to marry without his permission? There is a difference of opinion. Shaykh al-Islam said: The mukatabah may not marry without her master's permission, because she is owned by the master. Zufar said: It is permissible, because it is a type of acquisition. We say: The mahr is due in exchange for the essence of ownership, not for usufructs, and it is the right of the master. If she becomes free before the marriage is annulled, the marriage becomes valid, because it was only invalid due to the master's right. When she becomes free, his right ceases, so it becomes valid. And she has no option, because she entered the contract willingly, and it is executed after emancipation. Thus in Al-Inayah. And we say: Just as it is not permissible for him to marry without his master's permission, so too it is not permissible for him to take a concubine, even with his permission, like the fann, mudabbir, and ma'dhun. And that is taking a slave-girl for enjoyment. This is because the basis for the permissibility of taking a concubine is ownership of the corpus, and the slave absolutely does not own anything of the financial rulings of property, so their permission benefits them not. And the commentator (may Allah have mercy on him) will indicate this meaning shortly after this with his words: because the earnings of the mukatab are suspended, so what cannot bear annulment is not attached to it. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. (1) He said: Even if for compensation, because a gift is a gratuitous donation initially, and he is not qualified for it. [2] He said: Except a small amount, meaning his charity is not valid except for a small thing. It is said: less than a dirham is small, due to people's leniency regarding it. Thus in Al-Kifayah. [3] He said: And his suretyship, by person or property, with or without the master's command, because all of it is gratuitous, and he is not qualified for it. Thus in Al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage is an expansion (sharh) of the matn of Sharh al-Wiqayah, dealing with the rulings of the mukatab (a slave who has contracted with his master for emancipation upon payment of a sum). The commentary clarifies several key points:

First, it explains that a mukatab cannot enter into a kitabah contract with his own slave—this is the ruling by qiyas, since such a contract leads to emancipation, and the mukatab is not considered legally competent to cause emancipation (because he himself is not yet free). However, the Hanafi school prefers istihsan (juristic preference) here: the kitabah contract is treated as a means of acquiring wealth (similar to a sale) and is therefore valid. The emancipation is ultimately attributed to the master, not the mukatab, so the master's rights are preserved.

Second, the passage discusses the wala' (clientage) of a second mukatab owned by a first mukatab. If the second pays after the first is emancipated, the wala' goes to the first mukatab (because he directly caused the emancipation). If the second pays before the first is emancipated, the wala' goes to the master (since the first had no complete eligibility). If both pay simultaneously, the wala' goes to the master by preference. If the first fails to pay and is returned to slavery, the second remains a mukatab and may still earn freedom.

Third, there are restrictions on the mukatab's actions: he cannot marry without his master's permission, nor give gifts (except small charitable donations), nor enter into surety, nor lend, nor emancipate his own slave (even for compensation). These are all gratuitous transactions or ones that do not directly help him earn money to pay his kitabah. Marriage imposes financial obligations (mahr and maintenance) that burden the master, so it is disallowed without permission.

Fourth, a specific sub-issue arises for the female mukatab (mukatabah): may she marry without the master's permission? Here there is an intra-Hanafi dispute. Shaykh al-Islam (likely Abu Hanifa) says no, because she is still owned; Zufar says yes, because it is a form of acquisition. The preferred Hanafi position (as stated in the text) is that she may not marry without permission, but if she does and later becomes free before the marriage is annulled, the marriage becomes valid automatically, and she has no option of annulment.

Fifth, the mukatab is also prohibited from taking a concubine (tassari), even with the master's permission, because concubinage requires ownership of the slave-girl's corpus, and the mukatab does not own the corpus of any slave—he only owns the right to earn. Thus his permission is ineffective.

The overall ruling is that the mukatab is under financial restriction (hajr) to protect the master's right to the kitabah payment. All his transactions are tested against whether they help him earn the consideration or burden the master excessively.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitabah) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying a specified sum.
ولاء (wala'): Clientage: a legal bond of inheritance and responsibility that follows emancipation of a slave.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: a method of reasoning that overrides qiyas (analogy) in favor of a more equitable outcome, often based on custom or necessity.
تسري (tassari): Taking a slave-girl as a concubine (for sexual relations).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is a mukatab prohibited from marrying without his master's permission?
A) Because marriage is not a valid contract for a slave.
B) Because marriage imposes financial obligations (mahr and nafaqah) that burden the master's right.
C) Because the mukatab does not have the intellectual capacity to consent.
D) Because marriage is an act of worship and requires purity.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's kitabah with his own slave is valid by istihsan, but his other gratuitous transactions are restricted to protect the master's right to the kitabah payment.

The Mukatab's Capacity to Sell His Slave to Himself and Marry Him Off
Commentary on the permissibility of a mukatab's sale to himself and marriage of his slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a mukatab may sell his slave to himself and marry him off, and state the underlying legal reason (ta'lil) that distinguishes these acts from ordinary trading.

Core Idea
A mukatab may emancipate his slave through sale to himself or marriage because these acts are above kitabah and involve both emancipation and disposal of property.

In this passage, the commentator explains a key ruling about a mukatab's authority over his own slave. While a mukatab is partially free, his capacity includes acts that lead to full emancipation or destruction of property, as they are considered above the kitabah contract. This lesson unpacks the ta'lil behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts
Identify the two acts explicitly mentioned: selling his own slave to himself (بيع نفس عبده منه) and marrying him off (إنكاحه). These are the actions under discussion.
2
Determine the Hukm
State the ruling: these acts are permissible (jaiz) for the mukatab. The text affirms this by explaining why they are valid.
Contrast with other parties later.
3
Analyze the Ta'lil
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil) given: these acts are 'above kitabah' (فوق الكتابة) because they involve emancipation (إعتاق) or destruction of property (إتلاف مال). For the sale to himself, it is a form of emancipation; for marriage, it is a disposal of property that also implies some emancipation.
The word 'above kitabah' indicates these are not merely trading activities but higher-level dispositions.
4
Compare to Other Parties
Note that the text explicitly excludes ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik from performing such acts. Their permission does not cover these higher dispositions.
This highlights the unique capacity of the mukatab.
5
Confirm Authority Weight
Recognize that this ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah with no intra-Hanafi dispute, so it is the established position of the school.

Source Text

ولو بمال وبيع نفس عبده منه ، وإنكاحه والأبُ والوصي في رقيق الصغير كالمكاتب وشيء من ذا لا يصح من مأذون ومضارب وشريك
ولو بمال) ؛ لأنه فوق الكتابة، وبيع نفس عبده منه، وإنكاحه)، فإنَّ ذلك إعتاق، وهذا إتلاف مال.
والأب والوصي في رقيق الصغير كالمكاتب) : أي كل تصرف يملكُهُ المكاتب في عبده يملكانه في رقيق الصغير، وما لا فلا ، فإنَّهما يملكان تصرفا يحصلُ به المالُ للصَّغير كالمكاتب يملك كسب المال، فحكمهما حكمه، فيملكان كتابة عبده لا إعتاقه على مال، وبيع عبده من نفسه.
( وشيء من ذا لا يصح من مأذون ومضارب وشريك) : أي من قوله : لا تزوّجه إلى هنا، وأما إنكاحُ أمته، وكتابة عبده فهما وإن لم يكونا جائزين للمأذون لم يُدخلهما في قوله : وشيءٌ من ذا ، بل ذكرهما في «كتاب المأذون» بقوله : ولا يزوج رقيقه ولا مكاتبه ؛ لأنَّ قوله :هاهنا : وإنكاحُ أمته عطف على البيع والشراء ، وهما جائزان للمأذون ، فتخصيص الإشارة في قوله : وشيء من ذا إلى بعض المعطوفاتِ دونَ البعض لم يكن حسناً، فجعل الإشارة إلى قوله : لا تزوجه إلى آخره. [١] قوله : ولو بمال ؛ لأنّ الإعتاق على مال إسقاط الملك عـن رقبته، وإثباتُ الدين في ذمة المفلس، وهو فوق الكتابة ؛ لأنَّ الثابت للمكاتب ثبوت حق الحرية، وللمعتق على مال حقيقتها. كذا في الجلبي) ناقلاً عن «الكفاية».
[٢] قوله : وشريك ؛ أي شريك شركة العتاق وشركة المفاوضة ، وشركة العتاق هي شركة في كل تجارة، أو في نوع من أنواع التجارة، وتصح ببعض مـال أحـد الشريكين، ومع فضل مال أحدهما، ومع تساوي ماليهما مع تفاوت الربح بينهما، أو مع تفاوت ماليهما بتساوي الربح بينهما ، ومع كون أحد المالين دراهم، والآخر دنانير، والخلط فيه ليس بشرط، وكل شريكي العتاق مطالب بثمن مشتريه ، لا بثمن مشتري الآخر، فإنّ هذه الشركة لا تتضمن الكفالة.
And if with property, and selling his own slave to himself, and marrying him off. The father and guardian regarding the minor's slaves are like the mukatab. And none of this is valid from a ma'dhun, mudarib, or sharik. 'And if with property' because it is above kitabah; selling his own slave to himself and marrying him off are indeed emancipation and destruction of property. The father and guardian regarding the minor's slaves are like the mukatab: i.e., every disposition that the mukatab possesses over his slave, they possess over the minor's slave; what he does not, they do not. For they possess a disposition that acquires wealth for the minor, just as the mukatab possesses earning wealth, so their ruling is his ruling. Thus they possess the kitabah of his slave, not his emancipation for property, nor selling his slave to himself. 'And none of this is valid from a ma'dhun, mudarib, or sharik': i.e., from his statement 'do not marry him' up to here. As for marrying off his slave girl and the kitabah of his slave, even though they are not permissible for the ma'dhun, he did not include them in his statement 'nor any of this'; rather, he mentioned them in the Book of Ma'dhun with his statement: 'He does not marry off his slaves nor his mukatab.' Because his statement here: 'and marrying off his slave girl' is co-ordinated with buying and selling, and these are permissible for the ma'dhun. So specifying the reference in 'nor any of this' to some co-ordinated items and not others is not good. Thus he made the reference to his statement 'do not marry him' to the end. [1] His statement: 'And if with property': because emancipation for property is removing ownership from his neck and establishing a debt in the liability of the insolvent, and it is above kitabah; because what is established for the mukatab is the establishment of the right of freedom, whereas for the emancipator for property it is the reality of freedom. Thus in al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Kifayah. [2] His statement: 'And sharik': i.e., partner in a partnership of manumission and partnership of mufawada. Partnership of manumission is a partnership in every trade or in a type of trade. It is valid with part of the property of one of the partners, with the excess of property of one of them, with equality of property with difference in profit, or with difference in property with equality in profit, and with one of the capitals being dirhams and the other dinars. Mixing is not a condition. And each partner of manumission is responsible for the price of what he buys, not for the price of what the other buys, for this partnership does not include surety.
Source pages p. 16

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling about the legal capacity of a mukatab (a slave who has contracted for his freedom) to perform certain acts regarding his own slave. The commentator explains that a mukatab may validly buy his own slave from himself (which is effectively a form of emancipation) and may marry his slave off. This is because these actions are considered above kitabah (the contract of manumission) – they achieve full emancipation or disposal of property. The commentary then extends the ruling to the father and guardian (al-wasi) of a minor: they have the same authority over the minor's slave as the mukatab has over his own slave, because both are seen as earning wealth for another (the minor or themselves). However, the father and guardian may not emancipate the minor's slave for a price or sell the slave to himself; they may only write a kitabah for the slave. The commentator then clarifies that a ma'dhun (a slave permitted to trade), a mudarib (silent partner), and a sharik (partner in a particular type of partnership) do not have these powers – their permission does not extend to such acts. The footnote [2] defines 'sharik' further, distinguishing between shirkat al-itaq (partnership of manumission) and shirkat al-mufawada (general partnership), highlighting that each partner is only liable for his own purchases.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitabah) with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments. He partially owns himself and can engage in certain transactions.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade. He can buy and sell but cannot perform acts that imply full ownership or emancipation.
شركة العتاق (shirkat al-itaq): A partnership in manumission, where partners pool resources for trading, and each is only liable for his own purchases. Not a general partnership (mufawada).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is it permissible for a mukatab to sell his slave to himself?
A) Because it is a sale and he has general permission to trade.
B) Because it is above kitabah and constitutes emancipation.
C) Because the master has given him permission to do so.
D) Because it is a form of charity.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's capacity extends to acts that achieve emancipation or disposal of property, which are above the kitabah contract.

Comparison: Father/Guardian vs Ma'dhun, Mudarib, and Sharik
Comparison of legal capacity over slaves among father/guardian, ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the analogy that the father and guardian of a minor are like the mukatab in their capacity over the minor's slaves, and distinguish the positions of the ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik.

Core Idea
The father and guardian may write kitabah for the minor's slave but not emancipate for property, resembling the mukatab; the ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik cannot do any such acts.

The commentary extends the ruling on the mukatab to the father and guardian of a minor, drawing a parallel based on their shared goal of acquiring wealth. However, it explicitly excludes other parties like the ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik from such authority. This lesson examines the reasoning behind this comparison and the limits of each party's capacity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Parties Compared
List the parties: father (al-ab) and guardian (al-wasi) of a minor, and the mukatab. Also note the excluded parties: ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik.
2
State the Similarity
The text says the father and guardian are 'like the mukatab' (كالمكاتب) regarding the minor's slaves. They possess every disposition the mukatab has over his own slave.
This includes writing kitabah but not emancipation for property.
3
Understand the Ta'lil of the Analogy
The reason: both the mukatab and the father/guardian seek to acquire wealth (كسْب المال). The mukatab earns for his own freedom; the father/guardian earn for the minor. Therefore, their ruling is the same.
This is a clear case of qiyas based on the illah of kasb.
4
Contrast the Excluded Parties
The ma'dhun, mudarib, and sharik are explicitly excluded. Their authorization does not cover acts of emancipation or disposal beyond trade. The footnote clarifies that even in shirkat al-itaq, partners are only liable for their own purchases.
This shows that their capacity is narrower.
5
Note the Authority Weight
This ruling is established in the Hanafi school without reported dispute from the Sahibayn.

Whom Does the Mukatab Include in His Kitabah?
Ruling on inclusion of relatives in a mukatab's kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on which relatives enter a mukatab's kitabah upon purchase, and explain the legal reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani).

Core Idea
A mukatab's kitabah includes only birth relatives because maintenance for them relies on earning capacity, not wealth.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the scope of a mukatab's kitabah when he purchases a relative. The matn presents Abu Hanifa's view that only children and parents (lineal descendants and ascendants) automatically enter his kitabah. The Sahibayn extend this to any mahram relative (such as a brother or uncle). We will unpack the reasoning behind each position and its practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
What is the question being answered? When a mukatab buys a slave who is his relative, does that relative become a mukatab like him (enter his kitabah)?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that only those with a birth relationship (child, parents) enter the kitabah. Others do not.
Based on the obligation of maintenance: for birth relatives, maintenance is obligatory based on earning capacity (kasb), which the mukatab has. For others, maintenance requires wealth (yasar), which he lacks.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that any mahram relative (blood relative prohibited in marriage) enters the kitabah, just as they would be emancipated if a free person bought them.
They analogize to the case of a free person (qiyas).
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa refutes the analogy: a free person has full ownership (milk) over the purchased slave, while a mukatab has only earning capacity (kasb), not full ownership. Therefore, the case is different.
This is a distinction based on the nature of the mukatab's property rights.
5
Determine the practical consequence
If a mukatab buys his brother, according to the Sahibayn the brother becomes a mukatab and must contribute to the kitabah payment. According to Abu Hanifa, the brother remains a slave and does not enter the kitabah.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويكاتبُ عليه بالشّراءِ ولدُهُ وأبواه، لا من لا ولاد بينهما، هذا عند أبي حنيفة له ، وعندهما : إن اشترى ذا رحم مُحَرَّمٍ كالأخ والعم يدخل في كتابته ، كما يعتق عليه ، له : إنَّ للمكاتب كسباً لا ملكاً ، فجُعِلَ الكسب كافياً للصلة في قرابة الولادة إذ القادر على الكسب مخاطب بالنفقة في الولادة لا في غيره، إذ لا بد فيه من اليسار.
And he makes a kitabah upon buying his child and his parents; not one with whom there is no birth relationship. This according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if he buys a blood relative prohibited in marriage, like a brother or uncle, he enters into his kitabah, just as they would be emancipated upon him. For him (Abu Hanifa): the mukatab has earning capacity, not ownership; so earning capacity was deemed sufficient for connection in the case of birth relationship, because one capable of earning is obligated for maintenance in birth relationship, not in others, since that requires wealth.
Source pages p. 17

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whom a mukatab includes in his own kitabah when he purchases a relative as a slave. The matn states that when a mukatab buys his child or parents, they automatically become mukatab like him (they enter his kitabah). This is because there is a birth relationship (wala') – they are direct descendants or ascendants. However, other relatives without direct birth relationship, such as siblings or uncles, are not included according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Imam Muhammad) argue that any mahram relative (blood relative forbidden in marriage) should also enter the kitabah, because if the mukatab were free, such a relative would be emancipated upon purchase. Abu Hanifa's reasoning: a mukatab does not have full ownership (milk) over property; he has only earning capacity (kasb). This kasb is sufficient to create the obligation of maintenance only for those relatives with a birth relationship (lineal descendants and ascendants) because maintenance is obligatory on a person capable of earning, regardless of his wealth. For other relatives, maintenance is only obligatory if the person has wealth (yasar), which a mukatab does not have. Therefore, the connection (sila) through kitabah does not extend to them. The commentary later notes that this difference leads to practical consequences: a child born to the mukatab during his kitabah is like him; a purchased child must pay the kitabah immediately or be returned to slavery; parents are returned to slavery without immediate payment.

Key Terms
ولاد (walaad): Birth relationship; direct lineal descent (as in parent-child). The text specifies that only those with such a relationship are included according to Abu Hanifa.
كسب (kasb): Earning capacity; the mukatab is considered to have the ability to earn but not full ownership of property (milk). This limits his obligations.
يسار (yasar): Wealth/affluence; a condition for maintenance of non-birth relatives. The mukatab lacks yasar, so no obligation for them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why does a mukatab's purchase of his brother not include him in the kitabah?
A) Because a brother is not a mahram.
B) Because the mukatab lacks full ownership (milk) and the brother requires wealth (yasar) for maintenance.
C) Because the brother must consent.
D) Because the kitabah cannot be shared.

Key Takeaway
A mukatab's kitabah only includes his lineal descendants and ascendants (child, parents) according to the Hanafi school's relied-upon position, because maintenance for them is based on earning capacity, not wealth.

Selling Umm Walad by a Mukatib: Abu Hanifa vs Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on sale of umm walad by a mukatib

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the validity of a mukatib selling his umm walad and identify the key condition (child's inclusion) that determines the ruling.

Core Idea
A mukatib can sell his umm walad only if he bought her without her child.

This passage dives into an intra-Hanafi dispute about a mukatib's sale of his umm walad. While the general rule prohibits selling an umm walad, the mukatib's special status (pending earnings) creates an exception according to Abu Hanifa. The Sahibayn disagree, arguing that the umm walad is protected regardless. We will examine the proofs and the practical outcome.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and the Dispute
The act is a mukatib selling his umm walad. The dispute: does the presence or absence of her child in the kitabah contract affect the validity?
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position
Abu Hanifa says the sale is valid if the mukatib bought her without the child; invalid if he bought her with the child (by consensus).
3
State the Sahibayn's Position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say the sale is invalid in both cases because she is an umm walad.
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's Proof
Abu Hanifa uses qiyas: the mukatib's earnings are pending (mawquf), so the sale is not irrevocable. The child's presence triggers a prohibition because the mother follows the child's ruling.
The child's inclusion in the kitabah gives the mother a right against sale.
5
Examine the Commentary
The commentary (al-Jalabi, al-Hidayah glosses) explains why the mother does not become free like the child: the kitabah contract applies only to the mukatib and his offspring, not the mother.

And the sale of his umm walad is valid if he bought her without (the child), but if he bought her with (the child) then no. (1) (And the sale of his umm walad is valid if he bought her without the child, but if he bought her with the child then no) – this is according to Abu Hanifa. According to the two (companions), her sale is not valid even if he bought her without the child, because she is his umm walad, so her sale is not permissible. And for him (Abu Hanifa) is that analogy (qiyas) permits her sale even if the child is with her, because the earning of the mukatib is pending (mawquf) so it does not attach to it what cannot be revoked. But if the child is with her, the prohibition of sale is established due to the child's followership. [1] His saying: 'And the sale of his umm walad...' etc.; meaning: if the wife of the mukatib gave birth before he owned her in any way, then he owned her: if he owned her with the child, he cannot sell her by agreement, because her child is included in the kitabah as previously, and the mother is a follower of the child in this ruling according to the hadith. And if he owned her without the child, he may do so. This is in al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'due to the child's followership...' etc.; If you say: When the reality of freedom is established for the child, the mother's right is established. Here, for the child from the right of freedom is established, so it should not be established for the mother, actually, due to the lowering of her rank from the child. I say: The kitabah has rulings; among them is the impermissibility of sale, so this ruling is established for the mother without the kitabah, actually, due to the lowering of her rank. If you say: Why does she not become a mukatibah as a follower of the child? I say: The contract was made on the mukatib and the child is his offspring, so it is made on him, unlike the mother. This is in some marginal commentaries of al-Hidayah (3).
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sale of an umm walad (a slave concubine who has borne her master a child) by a mukatib (a slave in a contract of manumission). The matn states that the sale is valid if he bought her without the child, but invalid if he bought her with the child. The commentary clarifies that this is Abu Hanifa's position. According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), the sale is invalid even if she was bought without the child, because she is an umm walad. Abu Hanifa argues by analogy (qiyas) that the sale is permissible because the mukatib's earnings are pending (mawquf) and cannot attach to what is irrevocable. However, if the child is present, the sale is prohibited because the child's freedom is established, and the mother follows the child's ruling. The commentary then addresses an objection: if the child has freedom, why doesn't the mother also become free? The response is that the kitabah has specific rulings, and the mother only inherits the prohibition of sale, not the freedom, due to her lower rank. Also, the contract was made on the mukatib and the child is his offspring, so the child is included, but not the mother.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has entered a contract of manumission (kitabah) with his master, to pay a sum for freedom.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave concubine who has borne a child to her master; her status gives her certain protections against sale.
كتابة (kitabah): The contract of manumission between a master and his slave, where the slave pays installments for freedom.

B) The mother follows the child's ruling (taba'iyyah al-walad).
C) The mukatib's earnings are not pending anymore.
D) The Sahibayn's view is that it is always invalid.

Key Takeaway
A mukatib's sale of his umm walad is valid only if she was acquired without her child; the child's presence triggers protection because the mother follows the child's ruling.

The Mukatab's Intercourse with a Claimed Slave: Why the Child Is Not Free
States the ruling and its proof based on distinguishing the free man exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mukatab's intercourse with a claimed slave and explain why the free man exception does not apply.

Core Idea
The free man's child is free by consensus, but the mukatab's child remains a slave because the inability to pay immediately blocks the analogy.

This passage addresses a specific case: a mukatab (a slave contracted to buy his freedom) has intercourse with a female slave he owns (via valid or defective purchase). Later, the slave is claimed by another owner. The child's status is questioned. The Hanafi school rules the child a slave, distinguishing this from the free man deceived scenario. We will explore the proof and reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The mukatab has intercourse with a female slave he owns, and the slave is later claimed by another. The ruling: the child is a slave.
2
Recognize the potential objection
One might analogize to the free man deceived case, where the child is free. Why not apply the same here?
3
State the Hanafi proof (ijma)
The free man case is an exception to the general qiyas (that child follows mother's status) based on the consensus (ijma) of the Companions. This exception is limited to those who can immediately pay compensation.
The consensus is qat'i in transmission and meaning.
4
Apply the distinction to the mukatab
The mukatab cannot pay compensation immediately, so the harm to the claimant would be delayed. Therefore, the general rule applies: the child is a slave.
5
Note the practical consequence
If the mukatab later pays the value, he can reclaim it from the deceiving woman (if there was fraud).

Source Text

Source pages p. 22

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of the mukatab's interactions with a female slave. If a mukatab has intercourse with a female slave he owns (through valid purchase or fasid contract), and later she is claimed by another as a slave, the child is a slave. The commentary defends this ruling against an implicit objection: why not analogize to the free man deceived case where the child is free? The answer is that the free man's case is an exception from qiyas based on consensus of the Companions, and the underlying reason is that the free man can pay compensation immediately. The mukatab cannot pay immediately, so the harm to the claimant would be delayed, and thus the case remains under the general qiyas that the child follows the mother in slavery. If the mukatab later pays the value, he can reclaim it from the woman because she deceived him. The final note clarifies that the ruling applies whether the mukatab had permission from his master or not, but the text mentions 'without permission' to emphasize the first priority case; with permission it applies a fortiori.

Key Terms
وطئ (wati'a): To have sexual intercourse; here, the mukatab's intercourse with a female slave he owns.
أمة (amah): A female slave.
شراء فاسد (shira' fasid): A defective sale; a sale that is invalid due to missing a condition but not void (batil) in every sense. According to Hanafi, fasid means voidable.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered a contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom by paying installments.
استحقاق (istihqaq): Claim; the act of a third party proving ownership of a slave and reclaiming her.

C) Because the woman was not actually a slave.
D) Because the mukatab did not have permission from his master.

Key Takeaway
The mukatab's child remains a slave because the consensus-based exception for the free man does not extend to one who cannot pay compensation immediately.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Uqr for a Mukatab Who Buys and Has Intercourse with a Claimed Slave Woman
Ruling on immediate vs deferred 'uqr for a mukatab's purchase and marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the immediacy of 'uqr for a mukatab's purchase vs marriage of a claimed slave woman, explain the reasoning based on the link to trade, and evaluate the objection.

Core Idea
Purchase 'uqr immediate; marriage 'uqr deferred because purchase is trade, marriage is not.

This lesson addresses a nuanced fiqh issue: when a mukatab (a slave with a kitaba) has intercourse with a female slave he purchased or married, and she is later claimed by a third party, does the 'uqr (compensation) become immediately due or only after he becomes free? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on whether the transaction is trade or marriage, and the passage presents both the ruling and a defense against an objection.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage considers two separate facts: the mukatab purchases a slave woman and has intercourse, or he marries a slave woman and has intercourse. In both cases the slave is later claimed by someone else.
The ruling for each scenario is different.
2
Note the ruling for each
For purchase, the 'uqr is taken immediately (fi al-hal). For marriage, the 'uqr (here the mahr) is taken only after he becomes free (hina 'itq).
Immediate means while he is still a mukatab, from his trade assets.
3
Understand the reason for the distinction
Purchase is part of trade (tijara) and the authorization to purchase implies authorization for intercourse (consensual). Marriage is not trade, so the kitaba does not cover it, hence the obligation is deferred.
The key is whether the transaction falls under the categories of trade and earning.
4
Analyze the objection and the Hanafi response
An objector argues that 'uqr is established by intercourse, not purchase, and intercourse is not trade. The Hanafi response concedes that 'uqr is directly from intercourse, but the intercourse is legally based on the purchase (providing a shubha), so the purchase is the indirect cause and is part of trade, making the obligation binding on the master.
Thus the purchase creates a legal link that makes the 'uqr immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Her 'uqr is taken immediately, like the authorized (slave). And if he marries and has intercourse, it is taken when he becomes free. ... (Commentary on the text) ... So the kitaba does not encompass it. And someone may say: 'The 'uqr is established by intercourse not by purchase, and authorization for purchase is not authorization for intercourse, and intercourse is not part of trade, so it is not established against the master.' (Response: We concede that the 'uqr is initially established by intercourse, but the intercourse is based on the purchase, for otherwise it would be intercourse without any semblance of legality, so no 'uqr would be due and hadd would be obligatory. And intercourse itself, though not trade, but purchase is trade, so it is established against the master.)
Source pages p. 23

Commentary
This passage presents a Hanafi ruling on the 'uqr (compensation) for a mukatab or ma'dhun (authorized slave) who has intercourse with a female slave he purchased or married, and then the slave is claimed (mustahaqqa). The text distinguishes between purchase and marriage: In the case of purchase, the 'uqr is taken immediately from the mukatab's kitaba funds because the purchase is part of his trade, and authorization for purchase implies authorization for intercourse (consensual). Even if the purchase is invalid or the slave is later found to be owned by another, the 'uqr is due immediately to the owner. In contrast, in marriage, the 'uqr (actually mahr) is only taken after he becomes free ('itq) because marriage is not part of trade and the kitaba does not cover it. The commentary elaborates on the definition of 'uqr and clarifies that the immediate taking is because the authorization for purchase covers intercourse. A hypothetical objector argues that 'uqr is due from intercourse, not from purchase, and since intercourse is not trade, it should not be against the master. The response is that while 'uqr is directly from intercourse, the intercourse is legally based on the purchase (which provides a semblance of legality); thus the purchase is the indirect cause, and since purchase belongs to trade, the obligation falls on the master.

Key Terms
العقر (al-'uqr): Compensation paid to the owner of a female slave for intercourse, measured by the value or mahr; for virgins it is one-tenth of her value, for non-virgins one-twentieth, but with a minimum of ten dirhams.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a written contract (kitaba) with his master to purchase his freedom upon paying specified installments.
مأذون بالتجارة (ma'dhun bi al-tijara): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade with limited liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) After he becomes free, because the kitaba does not cover such obligations.
C) It is not taken because the master did not authorize intercourse.
D) Only if the purchase was valid.

Key Takeaway
For a mukatab, 'uqr from a purchased slave woman is immediate due to its link to trade; from marriage it is deferred until 'itq.
