Kitaba of Umm Walad and Mudabbara is Valid
Ruling on validity and consequences of kitaba for umm walad and mudabbara

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the validity of kitaba for an umm walad and a mudabbara, and explain the consequences if the mukataba becomes umm walad.

Core Idea
Kitaba for umm walad and mudabbara is valid; debt cancels on freedom, but contract persists for children and earnings.

The Hanafi school permits a master to write a kitaba contract with his umm walad or mudabbara. This lesson explores the reasoning behind this ruling and the special consequences that arise if the slave becomes free through istilad or tadbir before completing payment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its subjects
The passage rules that kitaba (a contract for gradual manumission) is valid (sahih) for an umm walad (a female slave who has borne her master's child) and a mudabbara (a slave declared free upon the master's death).
2
Trace the consequences if umm walad status is attained after kitaba
If the mukataba becomes umm walad (by giving birth to the master's recognized child), she is freed at the master's death without paying the kitaba debt. The debt is cancelled, but the kitaba remains in effect for her children and earnings: any children born after are free, and her earnings belong to her.
3
Analyse the rationale behind validity
Kitaba provides immediate benefit: the slave gains control over her own person and earnings, and a path to freedom. Both umm walad and mudabbara need this benefit before the master's death, just like other slaves, so it is permissible.
4
Understand why tadbir does not preclude kitaba
Tadbir only creates a future entitlement to freedom, not actual freedom. There is no contradiction between the two, so a mudabbara can validly enter kitaba.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكتابة أم ولده فعتقت بموته مجاناً ومدبره. فتعتق بعد موت المولى، فإن مضت على الكتابة فلها أن تأخذ العقر من سيدها. (وكتابة أم ولده فعتقت بموته مجاناً ومدبره): أي صحت كتابة مدبره. قوله: فتعتق بعد موت المولى؛ أي على الاستيلاد، وسقط عنها مال الكتابة. قوله: فلها أن تأخذ العقر؛ أي مهر مثلها؛ لاختصاصها بنفسها وبمنافعها، توسلاً إلى المقصود بالكتابة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي». قوله: وكتابة أم ولده؛ أي وصح كتابة أم ولده؛ لأن الكتابة يتوسل بها إلى ملك اليد في الحال، والحرية عند أداء البدل، وحاجة أم الولد إلى استفادة هذا المعنى قبل موت المولى، كحاجة غيرها فكان جائزاً. قوله: مجاناً؛ أي بالاستيلاد، وتعلق عتقها بموت السيد، وسقط عنها بدل الكتابة؛ لأن الغرض من إيجاب البدل العتق عند الأداء، فإذا عتقت قبل الأداء لا يمكن توفير الغرض على المولى، فسقط بدل الكتابة، وبطلت الكتابة؛ لامتناع إبقائها بلا فائدة. غير أنه تسلم لها الأكساب والأولاد؛ لأن الكتابة انفسخت في حق البدل وبقيت في حق الأولاد والأكساب، حتى يعتق الأولاد ويخلص له الأكساب. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: صحت... الخ؛ لوجود المقتضى وهو الحاجة، فإن الثابت بالتدبير مجرد استحقاق الحرية لا حقيقتها، ولانتفاء المانع، وهو عدم المنافاة بين الكتابة والتدبير. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي»
And the kitaba of his umm walad is valid, so she is freed at his death without payment, and [the kitaba of] his mudabbara. She is freed after the death of the master, and if she continued on kitaba, she can take the dower from her master. (And the kitaba of his umm walad, so she is freed at his death without payment, and his mudabbara): meaning his mudabbara's kitaba is valid. His saying: she is freed after the death of the master; i.e., by istilad, and the kitaba payment is dropped from her. His saying: she can take the dower; i.e., her fair marriage payment; because she is entitled to herself and her benefits, as a means to the objective of kitaba. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. His saying: and the kitaba of his umm walad; i.e., the kitaba of his umm walad is valid; because kitaba is a means to ownership of the hand immediately and freedom upon payment of the substitute, and the umm walad's need to obtain this meaning before the master's death is like the need of others, so it is permissible. His saying: without payment; i.e., by istilad, and her freedom is tied to the master's death, and the kitaba payment is dropped; because the purpose of imposing the payment is freedom upon payment, but when she is freed before payment, the purpose cannot be fulfilled for the master, so the kitaba payment is dropped and the kitaba is void; because it is impossible to keep it without benefit. But her earnings and children are surrendered to her; because the kitaba is annulled regarding the payment but remains regarding children and earnings, so that the children become free and the earnings become hers. Thus in 'al-Hidayah'. His saying: so it is valid... etc.; due to the presence of the requisite which is need, for what is established by tadbir is merely entitlement to freedom not its reality, and due to the absence of the impediment, which is the lack of contradiction between kitaba and tadbir. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the matn of al-Hidayah with commentary. It addresses the validity of concluding a kitaba contract with an umm walad (a female slave who has borne her master a child) or a mudabbara (a slave declared free upon the master's death). The ruling states that such a kitaba is valid. The reasoning is that kitaba provides immediate benefit (the slave gains control over her own person and earnings) and eventual freedom upon payment, which is a need for these slaves just as for others. If, after the kitaba, the master has a child by her (istilad), she becomes umm walad, which frees her upon the master's death automatically. In that case, the kitaba debt is cancelled because the purpose of the debt (to earn freedom via payment) is superseded by the automatic freedom through istilad. However, the kitaba is not completely void; it is annulled only regarding the debt, but it remains effective for the children and earnings, so that any children born after are free and her earnings belong to her. The commentator further clarifies that tadbir (declaration of freedom upon death) only creates an entitlement to future freedom, not actual freedom, so it does not conflict with the kitaba. Thus both the umm walad and mudabbara can validly enter into a kitaba.

Key Terms
الكتابة (al-kitāba): A contract between a master and a slave whereby the slave earns money to purchase their freedom, paying in installments.
أم ولد (umm walad): A female slave who has given birth to her master's child, which is recognized as the master's offspring, thus she is freed upon his death.
مدبر (mudabbir): A slave whom the master has declared free upon his death (tadbir).
عقر (ʿaqr): The dower (mahr) paid to a female slave when she is married off by her master; here it refers to what the mukataba can take from her master if she is married during kitaba.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the kitaba debt if a mukataba becomes umm walad after the kitaba?
A) The debt remains and she must pay it before freedom.
B) The debt is cancelled and the kitaba is void entirely.
C) The debt is cancelled but the kitaba remains effective for children and earnings.
D) The debt is reduced by half.

Key Takeaway
Kitaba for umm walad and mudabbara is valid; if she becomes free via istilad, the debt is cancelled but the contract persists for children and earnings.

Mukataba's Payment upon Master's Insolvent Death: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Options for mukataba when master dies insolvent and the three imams' positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi views on the payment a mudabbara mukataba must make when her master dies insolvent, and explain the underlying reasoning (tajazzu' al-iʿtāq).

Core Idea
In an intra-Hanafi dispute, the divisibility of emancipation determines whether the slave has a choice or must pay a specific amount.

When a master makes his slave both a mudabbara and a mukataba, then dies insolvent, the slave's path to freedom is not straightforward. The three great Hanafi imams—Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani—disagree on what the slave must pay and whether she has a choice. This passage unpacks their arguments, rooted in a key principle: is emancipation divisible or not? Let's walk through the dispute.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A female slave is both a mudabbara (declared free upon master's death) and a mukataba (under a kitaba contract). The master dies insolvent, with no assets other than this slave. The slave's value exceeds one-third of the estate, so she is not automatically freed. She must pay to earn her freedom.
The condition of insolvency is critical; if the master had other assets, the slave might be freed from the one-third of the estate without payment.
2
State the three positions
Abu Hanifa: she has a choice between paying two-thirds of her value or the full kitaba debt. Abu Yusuf: she pays the lesser of these two amounts, with no real choice. Muhammad: she pays the lesser of two-thirds of her value or two-thirds of the kitaba debt.
The dispute has two aspects: choice (khiyar) and amount (miqdar).
3
Analyze the reasoning for choice
For Abu Hanifa, emancipation is divisible (tajazzu' al-iʿtāq). Thus one-third of her is freed by tadbir, and two-thirds remain a slave under kitaba. She has two distinct obligations leading to freedom, so she can choose which to fulfill. For Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, emancipation is indivisible; the entire slave becomes free upon payment, so the two obligations merge into one (the lesser). Hence no real choice.
This step shows how a single usul principle—divisibility of emancipation—produces different fiqh rulings.
4
Analyze the reasoning for amount
Muhammad argues: the kitaba substitute was exchanged for the entire slave's value. Since one-third is already freed by tadbir, one-third of the substitute drops. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf counter: the substitute was actually exchanged only for the two-thirds that remained a slave at the time of the kitaba, because a person would not pay money for what he is already entitled to by tadbir. Thus no portion drops.
The disagreement on amount also hinges on the nature of the kitaba substitute—whether it was matched to the whole or only to two-thirds.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under Abu Hanifa, the slave chooses the cheaper option. Under Abu Yusuf, the judge imposes the lesser of the two amounts (value-debt). Under Muhammad, the judge imposes the even smaller amount (two-thirds of value vs. two-thirds of debt). The actual numbers can differ significantly.
For example: value=100, debt=120 → Abu Hanifa: slave pays either 66.67 or 120; Abu Yusuf: 66.67; Muhammad: lesser of 66.67 or 80 = 66.67 (same here). If value=90, debt=60 → Abu Hanifa: 60 or 60? Actually 2/3 of value=60, full debt=60, same. But if value=150, debt=90 → Abu Hanifa: 100 or 90; Abu Yusuf: 90; Muhammad: lesser of 100 or 60 = 60. So Muhammad's view can produce a different result.

ويسعى في ثلثي قيمته أو كل البدل في موت سيده معسراً. هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعند أبي يوسف يسعى في الأقل منهما، وعند محمد يسعى في الأقل من ثلثي القيمة أو ثلثي البدل. أما الخيار وعدمه ففرع التجزؤ وعدمه كما مر. وأما المقدار فمحمد يقول: البدل لما كان مقابلاً بالكل فبالموت يسلّم له ثلث البدل، ومن المحال أن يجب البدل في مقابلة الثلث. وهما يقولان: البدل وقع في مقابلة الثلثين، لأن الظاهر أن الإنسان لا يلتزم المال في مقابلة ما يستحق حريته. قوله: ويسعى في ثلثي قيمته... الخ؛ أي إن مات المولى ولا مال له غيرها، فهي بالخيار بين أن تسعى في ثلثي قيمتها أو جميع مال الكتابة، وإنما قيد بقوله: معسراً؛ أي لا مال له غيرها؛ لأنه لو كان له مال غيرها وهي تخرج من ثلث المال عتقت بالتدبير وسقطت عنها المكاتبة؛ لوقوع الاستغناء بها عن أداء المال، فكان هذا بمنزلة ما لو عتق المولى مكاتبه. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله: يسعى في الأقل من ثلثي القيمة أو ثلثي البدل، فالخلاف في الخيار والمقدار، فأبو يوسف مع أبي حنيفة في المقدار، ومع محمد في نفي الخيار. أما الخيار ففرع تجزؤ الإعتاق، والإعتاق عنده لما تجزأ بقي الثلثان رقيقاً، وقد تلقتها جهتا حرية ببدلين: معجّلة بالتدبير ومؤجّلة بالكتابة فتخير، وعندهما لما عتق كلها بعضها فهي حرّة، ووجب عليها أحد المالين، فتختار الأقل لا محالة، فلا معنى للتخيير. وأما المقدار؛ فلمحمد أنه قابل البدل بالكل، قد سلم لها الثلث بالتدبير، فمن المحال أن يجب البدل بمقابلته، ألا ترى أنه لو سلم لها الكل بأن خرجت من الثلث بعتق يسقط كل البدل، فهاهنا يسقط الثلث، فصار كما إذا تأخر التدبير عن الكتابة. ولهما: أن جميع البدل مقابل بثلثي رقبتها، فلا يسقط منه شيء؛ وهذا لأن البدل وإن قوبل بالكل صورة وصيغة، لكنه مقيّد لما ذكرنا معنى وإرادة؛ لأنها...

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf vs. Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding a specific scenario: a female slave who is both a mudabbara (declared free upon master's death) and a mukataba (under a kitaba contract). The master dies insolvent, meaning he has no property other than this slave. The slave's value is assumed to be more than one-third of the estate, so she is not automatically freed by tadbir. Instead, she must pay to earn her freedom. The disagreement is on two points: (1) whether she has a choice (khiyar) between paying two-thirds of her value or the full kitaba debt, and (2) what exactly she must pay. Abu Hanifa says she can choose between paying two-thirds of her value or the full kitaba substitute. Abu Yusuf says she must pay the lesser of the two amounts, and there is no real choice because she will inevitably choose the lesser. Muhammad says she must pay the lesser of two-thirds of the value or two-thirds of the substitute. The reasoning: Abu Hanifa considers emancipation as divisible, so part of her is free by tadbir (one-third) and part remains a slave (two-thirds) subject to the kitaba; the two obligations (tadbir and kitaba) are towards different aspects of freedom, so she can choose which to fulfill. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, however, view emancipation as indivisible: she is either fully free or not. Since she is ultimately free after payment, she is considered entirely free, and the two obligations merge; she must pay the lesser amount. On the amount specifically, Muhammad argues that the kitaba substitute was in exchange for the entire slave's value, but one-third of that value has already been freed by tadbir, so one-third of the substitute should be dropped. Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf counter that the kitaba substitute was actually in exchange for only two-thirds of her value (the part that remained a slave at the time of the kitaba), since tadbir already covered one-third. Therefore, no portion of the substitute is dropped. The commentator explains that this ruling applies only when the master is insolvent; if he had other assets, the slave would be valued and if she falls within one-third, she is freed without payment. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here, but later Hanafi works follow Abu Hanifa on the choice and Abu Yusuf on the amount, or vice versa.

Key Terms
تجزؤ الإعتاق (tajazzu' al-iʿtāq): The divisibility of emancipation; whether freeing a slave can apply to a portion of the slave.
العقر (al-ʿaqr): Here refers to the kitaba substitute (mal al-kitaba) or the value (qima) that the slave pays.
معسر (muʿsir): Insolvent; having no property other than the slave in question.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) She can choose between paying two-thirds of her value or two-thirds of the kitaba debt.
D) She must pay the lesser of two-thirds of her value or the full kitaba debt.

Key Takeaway
The divisibility of emancipation (tajazzu' al-iʿtāq) is the root principle that generates three distinct Hanafi rulings on the mukataba's payment upon her master's insolvent death.

Validity of Compromise in Kitaba: Istihsan Overrides Qiyas
Ruling on compromise in kitaba with istihsan basis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school validates a compromise between a master and his mukatab on deferred payment, applying istihsan to override qiyas.

Core Idea
In kitaba, both the term and the consideration are partial wealth; exchanging them is not riba.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced fiqh issue: a master and his mukatab (a slave under a kitaba contract) agree to replace a future payment with a smaller immediate payment. By strict analogy (qiyas), this appears to be an exchange of time for money, resembling riba. However, the Hanafi school rules it valid based on istihsan (juristic preference). Understanding why requires analyzing the unique legal nature of the kitaba contract, where the term (ajal) and the consideration (badal) are each considered 'wealth from one perspective' (mal min wajh), preventing the transaction from falling into riba.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the specific transaction being ruled upon? What is the hukm (sahih/fasid etc.)? The passage states that the compromise is valid (sahih) by istihsan, even though qiyas would make it invalid.
The act is a sulh (compromise) between a master and his own mukatab, changing a deferred payment (badal mu'ajjal) to a smaller immediate payment (half nāqid).
2
Locate the qiyas and its reasoning
The author first presents the qiyas that would invalidate the transaction: because it is an exchange of the term (ajal) for money, and term is not wealth, so it resembles riba. This is analogous to the case of a free man converting a deferred debt to a smaller immediate sum, which is prohibited.
The qiyas relies on the principle that exchanging time for money is ribawi.
3
Trace the istihsan basis (wajh al-dalalah)
Istihsan is based on the unique nature of kitaba: the term (ajal) is considered 'wealth from one perspective' (mal min wajh) because the mukatab needs it to fulfil the kitaba; the consideration (badal al-kitaba) is also not full wealth because suretyship (kafala) for it is invalid. Thus, both sides are partially wealth and of different genres (jins), so the exchange is not riba.
The balancing ('fāʿtadalā') of the two partial wealths prevents the analogy to riba.
4
Note the ikhtilaf (cross-madhhab)
Other schools, notably the Shafiʿi school, would likely consider this transaction invalid because they do not accept the concept of partial wealth in this context or limit istihsan. The Hanafi school relies on the stronger reasoning (istihsan) over qiyas.
The passage itself does not name the opposing school, but the contrast with the free man case implies ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih (preferred view)
The Hanafi mufta bihi position is that the compromise is valid (sahih). The authority is Al-Hidayah, which follows Zahir al-Riwayah. The tarjih is based on the strength of the istihsan argument, which addresses the specific circumstances of the mukatab.
Later Hanafi jurists (e.g., al-Kasani, Ibn Abidin) reaffirm this ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصلحه مع مكاتبه على نصف حال من بدل مؤجل : أي صح صلحه ، والقياس أن لا يصح ) ، لأَنَّهُ اعتياض عن الأجل بالمال، ووجه الاستحسان : أن الأجل في حق المكاتب مال من وجه لا يقدر على الأداء إلا به، وبدل الكتابة ليس بمال من وجه" حتى لا تصح الكفالة به فاعتدلا .
[١]قوله : أن لا يصح؛ لأنّه اعتياض عن الأجل، وهو ليس بمال، والدين مال فكان ربا ؛ ولهذا لا يجوز مثله في الحرّ، بأن كان على الحرّ ألف مؤجّل فصالحه على خمس مئة حالة، وفي مكاتب الغير بأن كان على مكاتب الغير ألف إلى سنة فصالحه على خمسمئة. كذا في بعض حواشي «الهداية».
[٢] قوله : ليس بمال من وجه ؛ لاحتمال التعجيز.
[٣]قوله : حتى لا تصح الكفالة به؛ أي ببدل الكتابة، فلو كان مالاً من كل وجه لصحت الكفالة به. كذا في شرح العيني).
[٤] قوله : فاعتدلا ; فكان اعتياضاً عما هو مال من وجه بما هو مال من وجه ، واختلف الجنس، فلم يكن ثمة ربا. كذا في «العناية».
And his compromise with his mukatab for half of the present [payment] from a deferred consideration: meaning his compromise is valid, and by analogy it should not be valid, because it is an exchange of the term for money. And the basis of istihsan: that the term in the right of the mukatab is wealth from one perspective, as he cannot make payment except by it, and the consideration of kitaba is not wealth from one perspective, so much so that suretyship for it is not valid, so they become balanced.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a ruling_statement that the compromise between a master and his mukatab (slave under kitaba) to change a deferred payment to a smaller immediate payment is valid. The author states that by analogy (qiyas) it should be invalid because it is an exchange of a non-wealth (the term) for wealth (money), which resembles riba. However, the Hanafi school adopts the ruling based on istihsan (juristic preference). The reasoning is that the term (ajal) is considered wealth from one perspective for the mukatab because he depends on it to make payment, and the consideration (badal al-kitaba) is not full-fledged wealth because kitaba itself is not fully a financial contract (suretyship for it is invalid). Thus, the two sides are balanced, and the exchange is not riba because the two items being exchanged differ in genre (jins). The commentator elaborates that the same qiyas would apply to a free man exchanging a deferred debt for a lesser immediate amount, which is prohibited as riba, but the case of the mukatab is different due to the unique nature of kitaba.

Key Terms
صلح (sulh): A compromise or settlement between the master and mukatab to change the payment terms.
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a kitaba contract with his master to earn his freedom by paying a specified sum.
أجل (ajal): The deferred term of payment; the time allowed for the mukatab to pay.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; departing from qiyas for a stronger reason.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the compromise between a master and his mukatab on deferred payment considered valid in the Hanafi school, according to the passage?
A) Because qiyas directly permits it for any debtor.
B) Because the term is considered wealth from one perspective and the consideration is not fully wealth, so no riba occurs.
C) Because both the term and the consideration are full-fledged wealth, making the exchange a simple sale.
D) Because the master has the right to forgive part of the debt unconditionally.

Key Takeaway
A compromise reducing a deferred kitaba payment to a smaller immediate sum is valid because both the term and the consideration are partial wealth, avoiding riba.

Ruling on a Free Person Paying the Kitabah of a Slave: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Ruling with proof contrasting qiyas and istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a free person pays a slave's kitabah without stipulation and explain why istihsan overrides qiyas.

Core Idea
A free person's payment of a slave's kitabah frees the slave by istihsan, even without prior stipulation.

When a free person intervenes to pay a slave's kitabah, the Hanafi school applies istihsan to free the slave, departing from strict qiyas. This lesson unpacks the textual reasoning and the role of donation (tabarru').

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage discusses a free person (ajnabi/fuduli) who tells the master to make a kitabah contract for his slave, and then pays the agreed amount. Two sub-cases: with or without stipulation that payment triggers freedom.
The payer is not the slave's owner or agent.
2
Contrast qiyas and istihsan
If no stipulation, qiyas would require the slave's acceptance for the contract to be binding, so the slave would not be freed merely by the free person's payment. Istihsan frees the slave because the payment benefits the slave without harming him, and the payer is a donor.
The key is that the payer's act is tabarru' (donation), not a contract imposing liability.
3
Apply the ruling to the present case
In the scenario with a present and absent slave, the present slave's acceptance makes him a mukatab. For the absent slave, the contract is effective in freeing him (benefit) but suspended regarding the substitute (harm).
This illustrates the principle: the fuduli's act is effective in benefits, not harms.
4
Extract the consequence
The free person has no recourse against the slave because he acted as a donor. The slave is freed immediately upon payment.
This is a key practical outcome.

(۱) «البناية» (۸: ۸۱).  كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه ۳۱ وإن قبل العبد فهو مكاتب ، فإن كوتب حاضر وغائب، وقبل الحاضر فأي أدى قبل جبراً أو عتقاً بأداء الحرّ، أما إن شرط العتق بأدائه فظاهر، وأما إن لم يشترط فالقياس أن لا يعتق، وفي الاستحسان يعتق ؛ لأنَّهُ يتوقف على قبول العبدِ الغائبِ فيما يضرُّه وهو وجوب البدل عليه ، لا فيما ينفعُهُ وهو صحةُ أداء القابل البدل، (وإن قبل العبد فهو مكاتب ) : أي إن كاتب الحر العبد، وبلغ العبد وقبل فهو مكاتب ؛ لأنَّ الكتابة موقوف على إجازته. فإن كوتب حاضر وغائبٌ ، وقَبلَ الحاضر فأي أدى قبل جبراً أو عتقاً ) ، صورة المسألة : أن يقول : كاتِبني بألف على نفسي وعلى فلان الغائب ففعل وقبل الحاضر قوله : فيما يضره....الخ ؛ وفيما نحن فيه لا ضرر للعبد الغائب ؛ لأنّ المولى ينفرد بإيجاب العتق ، والحاجة إلى قبول المكاتب لأجل البدل، فإذا تبرع الفضولي بأداء البدل. وتصرف الفضولي نافذ في حق كلّ حكم ليس فيه ضرر، ولا ضرر عليه في عتقه عند تبرع الغير بأداء البدل عنه، فينفذ الكتابة في حق هذا الحكم، ويتوقف في حكم لزوم البدل وهو الألفُ مثلاً على العبد. كذا في «الكفاية»). قوله : وإن قبل العبد فهو مكاتب ؛ يعني أنَّ هذا العقد نافذ في حق العبد من حرمةِ البيع، ونفوذ عتقه بأداء هذا القابل، وموقوف على إجازته فيما عليه من لزوم البدل ؛ لأنه عقد جرى بين فضولي ومالك، فيتوقف على إجازة من له الإجازة ، فإذا قبله كان ذلك إجازة منه، فيصير مكاتباً منه ؛ لأنَّ الإجازة في الانتهاء كالإذن في الابتداء. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» قوله : وعلى فلان ؛ يعني به الشارح الله العبد الآخر الغائب لهذا المولى
If a free person says to the master of a slave: 'Write a kitabah for your slave for such and such,' and stipulates emancipation upon payment first, and he does so, and the free person pays, [the slave] is freed, and [the payer] does not have recourse. (And in half of its value here): meaning when the substitute is half the value here: meaning in the mentioned case, which is the death of the sick person who wrote a kitabah for his slave with a deferred substitute; he paid two-thirds of it immediately or was enslaved; meaning the slave is given a choice between paying two-thirds of the value immediately and refraining, in which case he is enslaved; because the favor occurred in the amount and in the deferment, so it is executed within one-third, not within two-thirds by agreement. If a free person said to the master of a slave: 'Write a kitabah for your slave for such and such,' and stipulates emancipation upon payment first: meaning whether he said: 'Upon me, if he pays, he is free' or did not say, and he did so and the free person paid, [the slave] is freed, and [the payer] does not have recourse: meaning the payer has no recourse against the slave because he is a donor in payment, and he is only freed. His saying: 'occurred in the amount': which is the cancellation of one thousand, and 'in the deferment' is the postponement of the other thousand. His saying: 'so it is executed within one-third': meaning his disposal in one-third of his value in cancellation and deferment is valid, but when that one-third is cancelled, the deferment also ceases, and his disposal in two-thirds of the value is not valid except in respect of cancellation, not in respect of deferment. Thus in Sharh al-Ayni. His saying: 'If he said...etc.': When he finished mentioning the rulings related to the original in kitabah, he began to mention the rulings related to the deputy in it. So if a free person: meaning a foreign intermeddler (fuduli) says to the master of a slave: 'Write a kitabah for your slave for such and such': meaning for one thousand dirhams, for example, and stipulates emancipation or does not stipulate, then the master does so and the free person pays one thousand dirhams, the slave is freed, and this free person does not have recourse against the slave for what he paid, because he is a donor, and the slave is only freed by the payment of the free person. As for if he stipulated emancipation upon payment, by saying: 'If he pays, he is free,' it is obvious. As for if he did not stipulate, then by qiyas the slave is not freed, because he did not stipulate that he would be freed upon fulfilling the condition, and the contract is suspended upon the slave's acceptance; but by istihsan he is freed, because it is suspended...etc. (Al-Binaya 8:81). [PAGE_031] The Book of al-Mukatab: His Transactions, Incapacity, and Rulings. 31. And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab. So if he is written as a mukatab [and he has] a present and an absent [slave], and the present accepts, then whichever [of them] pays, whether compelled or freed, by the payment of the free person. As for if he stipulated emancipation upon payment, it is obvious. As for if he did not stipulate, then by qiyas he is not freed, and by istihsan he is freed, because it is suspended upon the acceptance of the absent slave in what harms him, which is the obligation of the substitute, not in what benefits him, which is the validity of the payer's payment of the substitute. (And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab): meaning if the free person made a kitabah with the slave, and the slave comes of age and accepts, then he is a mukatab, because the kitabah is suspended upon his acceptance. So if he is written as a mukatab [with] a present and an absent [slave], and the present accepts, then whichever pays, whether compelled or freed. The image of the case: that he says: 'Make a kitabah for me for one thousand on myself and on so-and-so' the absent one, so he does so and the present accepts. His saying: 'in what harms him...etc.': In our case, there is no harm to the absent slave, because the master alone imposes emancipation, and the need for the mukatab's acceptance is for the substitute; so if the intermeddler volunteers to pay the substitute, and the disposal of the intermeddler is effective in respect of every ruling that involves no harm, and there is no harm upon him in his emancipation when another volunteers to pay the substitute on his behalf, so the kitabah is effective in respect of this ruling, and is suspended in the ruling of the binding of the substitute, i.e., one thousand, upon the slave. Thus in al-Kifaya. His saying: 'And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab': meaning that this contract is effective in respect of the slave concerning the prohibition of sale and the effectiveness of his emancipation by the payment of this payer, and is suspended upon his acceptance concerning what is upon him of the binding of the substitute; because it is a contract conducted between an intermeddler and an owner, so it is suspended upon the acceptance of the one who has the right to accept; so if he accepts it, that is an acceptance from him, so he becomes a mukatab from him, because acceptance at the end is like permission at the beginning. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. His saying: 'And upon so-and-so': meaning by it the commentator [means] the other absent slave of this master.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the ruling when a free person (ajnabi/fuduli) intervenes to make a kitabah contract on behalf of a slave's master. The commentator explains two scenarios: (1) if the free person stipulates that emancipation is conditional upon his payment (e.g., 'If I pay, he is free'), then if he pays, the slave is freed by clear wording; (2) if no such condition is stipulated, then by qiyas (analogical reasoning) the slave would not be freed because the contract is suspended on the slave's acceptance, but by istihsan (juristic preference) the slave is freed. This is because the free person's payment is a voluntary donation (tabarru'), and the master's act of writing the kitabah is valid. The commentator then addresses a variant where the slave is present and another is absent. The present slave's acceptance makes him a mukatab; the absent slave's acceptance is not needed for the benefit (freedom) but only for the burden (the substitute). Thus, the contract is effective in freeing the slave by the free person's payment, but the obligation of the substitute is suspended on the absent slave's acceptance. The Hanafi school relies on istihsan here, overriding strict qiyas, based on the principle that the intermeddler's disposal is effective in rulings that involve no harm to the absent party.

Key Terms
فضولي (fuduli): An intermeddler or stranger who acts on behalf of another without prior authorization.
كتابة (kitabah): A contract between a master and a slave where the slave pays a sum in installments in exchange for freedom.
عتق (itq): Emancipation; the act of freeing a slave.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that deviates from strict qiyas for a stronger evidence or public good.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; deriving a ruling for a new case based on a similar original case with the same effective cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a free person tells a master 'Write a kitabah for your slave for such and such' without stipulating emancipation upon payment, and then pays, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) The slave is not freed because payment was not stipulated as a condition.
B) The slave is freed by istihsan, and the free person has no recourse against the slave.
C) The slave is freed only if the master agrees to the free person's payment.
D) The free person can demand repayment from the slave because he paid for his freedom.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school frees the slave by istihsan when a free person pays his kitabah without stipulation, treating the payment as a donation and prioritizing benefit over strict analogy.

Kitabah with Present and Absent Slave: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Ruling on kitabah with present and absent slave, qiyas vs istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on kitabah with a present and an absent slave and distinguish between the qiyas and istihsan reasoning for the effects of payment.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, payment by either slave under kitabah frees both, based on istihsan overriding qiyas.

This lesson unpacks a nuanced scenario in the Book of Mukatab: a master contracts kitabah with two slaves, one present and one absent. Only the present accepts. What happens if either slave pays the kitabah amount? The text presents two counterposed legal methods – qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference) – and adopts the latter. Understanding this distinction reveals how Hanafi jurists prioritize benefit over strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
The master offers kitabah to two slaves jointly, one present and one absent. The present accepts; the absent is not present to accept. The key question: does payment by either slave lead to freedom for both, and is the master compelled to accept?
2
Understand the qiyas ruling
By qiyas, the contract is valid only for the present slave's share because he accepted. The absent's share is suspended pending his acceptance. If either pays, the master may not be compelled to accept if the absent pays, since the absent has no obligation.
Qiyas here is based on the principle that a fuduli (unauthorized person) cannot bind another without acceptance.
3
Understand the istihsan ruling
Istihsan holds that payment by either slave frees both, and the master is compelled to accept. Reason: the absent slave benefits from freedom without harm to himself, and the present's payment is a voluntary act that brings benefit.
The distinction: acceptance is needed for harm (obligation to pay) but not for benefit (freedom).
4
Apply the Hanafi mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the istihsan ruling: whichever slave pays the full kitabah, the master must accept and both slaves become free.
This is the operative view for fatwa, drawn from Zahir al-Riwayah and later commentaries.

Source Text

وجه الاستحسان : أنّ الحاضر أضاف العقد إلى نفسه فجعل نفسه أصلاً، والغائب تبعاً فيصح كما يصح على الأولاد" بالتبعية فأيهما أدَّى قَبلَ جبراً ، أما [E] الحاضر ؛ فلأن كل البدل عليه ، وأما الغائب ، فلأنَّه ينال شرف الحرية ، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه. (فصار كمعير الرهن صورته استعار رجل عيناً من غيره ليرهنه بدين [١]قوله : في حصة الحاضر ؛ لأنّ الحاضر ولي نفسه، ويتوقف في حصة الغائب ؛ لعدم الولاية عليه، كمن باغ عبده وعبد غيره، أو زوج أمته وأمة غيره، أو زوج بنته وبنت غيره الصغيرتين. Book of Mukatab: His transactions, incapacity, and rulings. And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab. If a master contracts kitabah with a present and an absent [slave], and the present accepts, then whichever [of the two] pays, it is accepted compulsorily, or he is freed by the payment of the free [meaning the one who pays]. As for if he stipulated freedom upon payment, it is apparent. And if he did not stipulate, then by qiyas he is not freed, but by istihsan he is freed; because it depends on the absent slave's acceptance in what harms him, which is the obligation of the substitute upon him, not in what benefits him, which is the validity of the payment by the payer. (And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab): meaning if the free man contracts kitabah with the slave, and the slave reaches [maturity] and accepts, he is a mukatab; because kitabah depends on his permission. So if he contracted kitabah with a present and an absent [slave], and the present accepted, then whichever pays, it is accepted compulsorily, or he is freed. The form of the issue: that he says: 'Contract kitabah with me for a thousand upon myself and upon so-and-so the absent', so he does and the present accepts. [1] His statement: 'in what harms him...etc'; and in our case there is no harm to the absent slave; because the master alone imposes freedom, and the need for the mukatab's acceptance is for the substitute. So if the non-owner (fuduli) volunteers to pay the substitute, and the action of the non-owner is effective regarding every ruling in which there is no harm, and there is no harm upon him in his freedom when another volunteers to pay the substitute for him, so kitabah is effective regarding this ruling, and it is suspended regarding the ruling of necessity of the substitute, which is the thousand for example, upon the slave. This is in al-Kifayah. [2] His statement: 'And if the slave accepts, he is a mukatab'; meaning that this contract is effective regarding the slave in terms of the prohibition of sale and the effectiveness of his freedom by the payment of this payer, and it is suspended upon his acceptance regarding what is upon him of the necessity of the substitute; because it is a contract that occurred between a non-owner and an owner, so it depends on the permission of the one who has the right to permit. So if he accepts it, that is a permission from him, so he becomes a mukatab from him; because permission at the end is like permission at the beginning. This is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His statement: 'and upon so-and-so'; meaning by it the commentator, the other absent slave of this master. (1) al-Kifayah (8:129). (2) Dhakirat al-'Uqba (p. 529). (3) See: Dhakirat al-'Uqba: p. 529. So it became like the one who lends a thing for mortgage. [1] So by qiyas it is valid regarding the share of the present, and regarding the share of the absent it depends on his acceptance. The face of istihsan: that the present attached the contract to himself, making himself the principal and the absent a follower, so it is valid just as it is valid upon children by followership. So whichever of the two pays, it is accepted compulsorily. As for the present, because all the substitute is upon him; and as for the absent, because he attains the honor of freedom, even if the substitute is not upon him. (So it became like the one who lends a thing for mortgage. Its form: a man borrowed an item from someone else to mortgage it for a debt. [1] His statement: 'regarding the share of the present'; because the present is the guardian of himself, and it is suspended regarding the share of the absent due to lack of guardianship over him, like one who sells his slave and the slave of another, or marries his slave girl and the slave girl of another, or marries his daughter and the daughter of another who are minors. [2] His statement: 'just as it is valid upon children'; meaning that kitabah in this manner is legislated, like when a slave girl is contracted kitabah, her children enter into her kitabah as followers, so they are freed by her payment, and there is nothing of the substitute upon them. [3] His statement: 'whichever of the two pays, it is accepted compulsorily'; meaning the master is compelled to accept. As for the present, because the substitute is upon him, so the master is compelled to accept his payment, and the absent is also freed because he entered into the kitabah of the present as a follower, like the child of a mukataba, or due to what we mentioned of the suspension and existence of the condition. And as for when the absent pays, because he attains for this payment the honor of freedom, so he is not like a stranger but is like the child of a mukataba. This is in al-Kifayah. [4] His statement: 'and as for the absent...etc'; meaning that by qiyas the master is not compelled to accept if the absent pays, because he is a volunteering stranger, since nothing of the substitute is upon him. And the face of istihsan: that he has benefit, because he attains the honor of freedom...etc. This is in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. (1) al-Kifayah (8:130-131). (2) Dhakirat al-'Uqba (p. 529).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling and its proof from the Book of Mukatab. It discusses a scenario where a master contracts kitabah with two of his slaves: one present and one absent. The present slave accepts the contract. The question is: what happens if either slave pays the kitabah (the fixed sum) to the master? The text explains that if the master stipulated that freedom occurs upon payment, then it is obvious. But if no such stipulation was made, qiyas (analogy) would dictate that the paying slave is not freed because the absent slave's acceptance is needed for the obligation of the substitute upon him. However, istihsan (juristic preference) rules that he is freed. The reasoning is that the absent slave only needs to accept what harms him (the obligation to pay), not what benefits him (the validity of the payment by another). The present slave's payment is a voluntary act by a non-owner (fuduli), but it is effective because it brings benefit (freedom) without harm. The contract is thus effective regarding freedom but suspended regarding the obligation of payment on the absent slave until he accepts. The text then compares this to the case of a person who borrows an item to mortgage it (معير الرهن), where the borrower's action is valid as a voluntary act. The ruling: whichever slave pays (present or absent), the master is compelled to accept it. If the present pays, all the substitute is upon him, and the absent is freed as a follower. If the absent pays, he attains freedom and the master must accept, even though no payment was upon him, because he gains the honor of freedom. This is based on istihsan due to the benefit of freedom, overriding the qiyas that would treat him as a stranger.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has entered into a contract of kitabah with his master to purchase his freedom in exchange for a fixed sum paid in installments.
فضولي (fuduli): A person who acts on behalf of another without prior authorization, like the present slave paying for the absent.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to a stronger underlying reason, such as benefit or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a master contracts kitabah with a present and an absent slave, and only the present accepts, what is the Hanafi mufta bihi ruling if the absent slave pays the full kitabah?
A) The absent slave is not freed because he did not accept the contract.
B) The absent slave is freed but the master is not compelled to accept the payment.
C) The absent slave is freed and the master is compelled to accept the payment.
D) The absent slave remains a slave unless he also accepts the contract.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a master contracts kitabah with a present and an absent slave, payment by either slave frees both, and the master must accept, based on istihsan prioritizing benefit over strict analogy.

Kitaba for Present and Absent Slave: Qiyas vs. Istihsan
Explaining the ruling of kitaba when one slave is absent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on kitaba when one slave is present and one absent, and explain the application of istihsan over qiyas in this case.

Core Idea
Istihsan validates the absent slave's inclusion in kitaba, overriding qiyas that requires his explicit acceptance.

When a master writes a kitaba contract for two slaves, one present and one absent, the Hanafi school faces a tension between strict analogy (qiyas) and juristic preference (istihsan). This lesson unpacks the ruling and the reasoning behind it, focusing on the interplay between principal and dependent in the contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
The master writes a kitaba for two slaves: one present (al-hadir) and one absent (al-gha'ib). The present slave acts as the principal, including the absent as a dependent.
The present slave has direct authority over his own share; the absent's acceptance is initially required.
2
State the qiyas ruling
Under strict qiyas, the kitaba is valid only for the present slave's share immediately. For the absent slave, it is suspended until he expressly accepts, because the present lacks guardianship over the absent.
This parallels cases like selling another's slave without permission.
3
Identify the istihsan ruling
By istihsan, the kitaba is valid for both slaves immediately, with the absent as a dependent. The present made himself the principal, and the absent is subordinate, analogous to children entering their mother's kitaba.
Istihsan considers the benefit of freedom for the absent.
4
Analyze the analogy
The Hanafi analogy compares the absent slave to a child who enters his mother's kitaba as a dependent. Just as the child is freed without payment obligation, the absent is freed if the present pays, and vice versa.
This analogy supports the validity of the contract without explicit acceptance.
5
Determine the practical consequence
Whichever slave pays the kitaba amount, the master is forced to accept. The present pays because the debt is on him; the absent pays for the honor of freedom. In both cases, the master cannot refuse.
The absent is not considered a stranger because he attains freedom.

الحاضر ؛ فلأن كل البدل عليه ، وأما الغائب ، فلأنَّه ينال شرف الحرية ، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه. (فصار كمعير الرهن صورته استعار رجل عيناً من غيره ليرهنه بدين [١]قوله : في حصة الحاضر ؛ لأنّ الحاضر ولي نفسه، ويتوقف في حصة الغائب ؛ لعدم الولاية عليه، كمن باغ عبده وعبد غيره، أو زوج أمته وأمة غيره، أو زوج بنته وبنت غيره الصغيرتين. [٢] قوله : كما يصح على الأولاد ؛ يعني أنّ الكتابة على هذا الوجه مشروعة، كالأمة إذا كوتبت دخل أولادها في كتابتها تبعاً ، حتى عتقوا بأدائها، وليس عليهم من البدل شيء. [٣] قوله : فأيهما أدى قبل جبراً ؛ أي يجبر المولى على القبول، أما الحاضر؛ فلأنّ البدل عليه فيجبر المولى على قبوله عند أدائه، ويعتق الغائب أيضاً لدخوله في كتابة الحاضر تبعاً، كما في ولد المكاتبة، أو لما ذكرنا من التعليق ووجود الشرط. وأما إذا أدى الغائب ؛ فلأنه ينالُ لهذا الأداء شرف الحرية، فلا يكون بمنزلة الأجنبي بل يكون بمنزلة ولد المكاتبة. كذا في «الكفاية»).

ولم يرجع على الآخر
عليه للآخر، فرهنه ، ثمَّ احتاجَ المعيرُ إلى استخلاص عينه ، فإِنْ أَذًى الدِّينَ إلى المرتهن يجبر المُرْتَهِنُ على القبول ، وإن لم يكن على معير الرهن دين، وإنما هو
على المستعير، وإن أدى الدين يرجع على المستعير به، وإن أدى بغير أمره ، لأنه مضطر إلى تخليص عينه ولا يتمكن إلا بأداء الدين.
(ولم يرجع على الآخر ؛ لأنه متبرع في حق الآخر، وإنما يرجع معير الرهن " ؛ لأنه مضطر في الأداء ؛ لأنه يخافُ تَلَفَ مَالِه في يد المرتهن
[1] قوله : وإن لم يكن على معير الرهن دين ؛ فكذا هاهنا يجبر المولى على القبول من الغائب، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه ؛ لأنه محتاج إلى استفادة الحرية. كذا في «شرح العيني».
[٢]قوله : ولم يرجع على الآخر ؛ الحاصل أنه أيهما أدى لا يرجع على صاحبه، أما الحاضر ؛ فلأنه قضى دينا عليه ولم يتعرّض له الشارح لظهوره، وأما الغائب ؛ فلأنه متبرع بالأداء غير مضطر إليه. قد يقال : معير الرهن اعتبر مضطراً إلى أداء دين المستعير؛ لاستخلاص المال، والحاجة الى استخلاص نفسه أقوى ، فالأولى أن يعتبر الغائب مضطراً إلى أداء بدل الكتابة، فالأولى أن يقال : الغائب بالأداء عامل لنفسه ؛ لرجوع منفعته إليه، وهو عتقُ رقبته فلا يرجع بما نفعه يعود إليه على الغير بخلاف معير الرهن. هكذا في «حاشية ملا الهداد».
(۳) قوله : وإنما يرجع معير الرهن جواب عما قيل : الغائب هاهنا كمعير الرهن، وهو مضطر ؛ ولهذا يرجع على المستعير بما أدّى.
تقريره : إنّ المعير كالغائب، وفي جواز الأداء من غير دين عليه لا في الاضطرار، فإنّ الاضطرار إنّما هو إذا فات له شيء حاصل ، وهاهنا ليس كذلك ، بل إنما هو
So it becomes like the one who gives a loan to be pawned. [1] Qiyas is that it is valid for the share of the present slave, and for the share of the absent slave it is suspended upon his acceptance. The basis of istihsan: the present slave added the contract to himself, making himself the principal, and the absent one subordinate, so it is valid just as it is valid upon children as subordinates. So whichever of them pays, he is forced to accept. As for the present: because the entire payment is upon him. As for the absent: because he attains the honor of freedom, even though the payment is not upon him. (Thus it becomes like the case of the one who gives a loan to be pawned: its form is that a man borrows an item from another to pawn it against a debt... [1] His statement: for the share of the present; because the present is his own guardian, and it is suspended for the share of the absent due to lack of guardianship over him, like one who sells his own slave and another's slave, or marries his own slave woman and another's slave woman, or marries his small daughter and another's small daughter. [2] His statement: just as it is valid upon children; meaning that kitaba in this manner is legislated, like when a slave woman is given kitaba, her children enter into her kitaba as subordinates, so they are emancipated upon her payment, and no payment is upon them. [3] His statement: whichever of them pays, he is forced to accept; meaning the master is forced to accept. As for the present: because the payment is upon him, so the master is forced to accept his payment, and the absent also becomes free due to his inclusion in the kitaba of the present as a subordinate, just as the child of a mukataba, or due to the ta'liq and existence of the condition. As for when the absent pays: because he attains the honor of freedom through this payment, so he is not like a stranger but rather like the child of a mukataba. So in al-Kifaya. [4] His statement: As for the absent... etc.; meaning that qiyas in this is that the master is not forced to accept if the absent pays, because he is a stranger and a volunteer, since no payment is upon him. The basis of istihsan: he has a benefit because he attains the honor of freedom... etc. So in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. Kitab al-Mukatab: His transactions, legal incapacity, and rulings. And he does not have recourse against the other. Payment for the other, so he pawns it. Then the one who gave the item for pawn needed to reclaim his item. If he pays the debt to the pledgee, the pledgee is forced to accept, even if the mughri (the one who gave the item for pawn) has no debt, but it is upon the borrower. If he pays the debt, he has recourse against the borrower for it; if he pays without his command, because he is compelled to reclaim his item and cannot achieve it except by payment. (And he does not have recourse against the other; because he is a volunteer regarding the other. Only the mughri has recourse because he is compelled in payment, because he fears loss of his property in the hands of the pledgee. [1] His statement: And even if there is no debt upon the mughri; so here too the master is forced to accept from the absent, even though the payment is not upon him, because he needs to obtain freedom. So in Sharh al-Ayni. [2] His statement: And he does not have recourse against the other; the conclusion is that whichever of them pays, he does not have recourse against his companion. As for the present: because he has paid a debt owed by him, and the commentator did not address it due to its obviousness. As for the absent: because he is a volunteer in payment, not compelled. It might be said: the mughri is considered compelled to pay the debt of the borrower to recover his property, and the need to recover oneself is stronger, so it is more appropriate to consider the absent compelled to pay the kitaba payment. So it is more appropriate to say: the absent, by payment, acts for himself because his benefit returns to him, namely the freedom of his person, so he does not have recourse for what profit comes back to him against another, unlike the mughri. So in Hashiyat Mulla al-Haddad. (3) His statement: Only the mughri has recourse is a response to what is said: the absent here is like the mughri, and he is compelled, and therefore he has recourse against the borrower for what he paid. Explanation: that the mughri is like the absent, and in the permissibility of payment without any debt upon him, but not in compulsion. For compulsion is only when something existing is lost for him, and here it is not so, but rather it is only...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a ruling from Al-Wiqaya regarding a special case of kitaba (contract of manumission by payment). The case involves two people who jointly write a kitaba contract for two slaves: one present (al-hadir) and one absent (al-gha'ib). The present slave enters the contract on behalf of both, making himself the principal and the absent a dependent. The author explains the qiyas (analogy) ruling and then the istihsan (juristic preference) that overrides it. Qiyas would say the kitaba is valid immediately for the present slave's share, but for the absent slave it is suspended until his acceptance (due to lack of guardianship). However, istihsan considers the benefit: the absent slave gains the honor of freedom without any payment obligation, so the kitaba is valid like the case of children entering their mother's kitaba as dependents. The passage then draws an analogy with the case of mu'ir al-rahn (one who lends an item to be pawned for another's debt). In that case, if the lender pays the debt to reclaim his item, he is forced to accept because he is compelled. Similarly, if the absent slave pays the kitaba amount, the master is forced to accept because the slave is compelled by his need for freedom. However, unlike the mughri lender, the absent slave does not have recourse against the present slave because he acts for his own benefit. The commentary reconciles differences and cites sources like Al-Kifaya, Hashiyat al-Jalabi, Sharh al-Ayni, and Hashiyat Mulla al-Haddad.

Key Terms
حصة الحاضر (hissat al-hadir): The share of the present slave in the kitaba contract; he is the principal because he is present and can contract for himself.
حصة الغائب (hissat al-gha'ib): The share of the absent slave; he is a dependent in the kitaba, and his acceptance is not required initially by istihsan.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas in favor of a beneficial outcome; here, it validates the kitaba despite lack of direct authority over the absent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling on kitaba with a present and an absent slave, what happens if the absent slave pays the kitaba amount?
A) The master is not forced to accept, because the absent is a stranger.
B) The master is forced to accept, because the absent attains the honor of freedom.
C) The payment is invalid because the absent has no right to pay.
D) The present slave must also pay the same amount.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan validates the absent slave's inclusion in kitaba, forcing the master to accept payment from either slave and granting freedom without the absent's explicit acceptance.

The Analogy with Mu'ir al-Rahn and the Issue of Recourse
Explaining the analogy with mu'ir al-rahn and the ruling on recourse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi analogy between the absent slave's payment of kitaba and the lender's payment in mu'ir al-rahn, and state why the absent does not have recourse against the present.

Core Idea
The absent slave, like the mu'ir al-rahn, is compelled by the need for freedom, but he does not have recourse because his benefit (freedom) outweighs the payment.

The analogy with mu'ir al-rahn deepens the legal reasoning for forcing the master to accept the absent slave's payment. However, a crucial distinction arises regarding recourse: the mu'ir al-rahn can reclaim his payment from the debtor, but the absent slave cannot reclaim from the present. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Recall the scenario from Lesson 1
The absent slave pays the kitaba amount. The master is forced to accept by istihsan. Now we examine the analogy with mu'ir al-rahn.
The present slave is not obligated to reimburse the absent.
2
Understand the qiyas objection
Under qiyas, the absent is a stranger (ajnabi) making a voluntary payment. Thus the master would not be forced to accept, and the absent would have no claim.
This objection is overridden by istihsan.
3
Introduce the analogy with mu'ir al-rahn
Mu'ir al-rahn is a person who lends an item to someone to pawn it for a debt. If the lender pays the debt to reclaim his item, he is compelled to pay, and the pledgee must accept. Similarly, the absent slave is compelled to pay for his freedom.
Both are compelled by a necessity: the lender to save his property, the slave to gain freedom.
4
Analyze the compulsion (idtarar)
The Hanafi school considers the absent slave's payment as compelled (mudtar) because he needs to attain freedom. Freedom is a stronger need than reclaiming property, so the analogy is even more fitting.
This strengthens the argument for forcing the master to accept.
5
Distinguish recourse
The mu'ir al-rahn has recourse against the borrower because he paid someone else's debt to save his own property. The absent slave, however, acts for his own benefit (his freedom), so he does not have recourse against the present slave. The benefit returns to him alone.
The present slave is not liable for the absent's payment because the absent's freedom is his own gain.

Source Text

(ولم يرجع على الآخر ؛ لأنه متبرع في حق الآخر، وإنما يرجع معير الرهن " ؛ لأنه مضطر في الأداء ؛ لأنه يخافُ تَلَفَ مَالِه في يد المرتهن
[1] قوله : وإن لم يكن على معير الرهن دين ؛ فكذا هاهنا يجبر المولى على القبول من الغائب، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه ؛ لأنه محتاج إلى استفادة الحرية. كذا في «شرح العيني».
[٢]قوله : ولم يرجع على الآخر ؛ الحاصل أنه أيهما أدى لا يرجع على صاحبه، أما الحاضر ؛ فلأنه قضى دينا عليه ولم يتعرّض له الشارح لظهوره، وأما الغائب ؛ فلأنه متبرع بالأداء غير مضطر إليه. قد يقال : معير الرهن اعتبر مضطراً إلى أداء دين المستعير؛ لاستخلاص المال، والحاجة الى استخلاص نفسه أقوى ، فالأولى أن يعتبر الغائب مضطراً إلى أداء بدل الكتابة، فالأولى أن يقال : الغائب بالأداء عامل لنفسه ؛ لرجوع منفعته إليه، وهو عتقُ رقبته فلا يرجع بما نفعه يعود إليه على الغير بخلاف معير الرهن. هكذا في «حاشية ملا الهداد».
(۳) قوله : وإنما يرجع معير الرهن جواب عما قيل : الغائب هاهنا كمعير الرهن، وهو مضطر ؛ ولهذا يرجع على المستعير بما أدّى.
تقريره : إنّ المعير كالغائب، وفي جواز الأداء من غير دين عليه لا في الاضطرار، فإنّ الاضطرار إنّما هو إذا فات له شيء حاصل ، وهاهنا ليس كذلك ، بل إنما هو
So it becomes like the one who gives a loan to be pawned. [1] Qiyas is that it is valid for the share of the present slave, and for the share of the absent slave it is suspended upon his acceptance. The basis of istihsan: the present slave added the contract to himself, making himself the principal, and the absent one subordinate, so it is valid just as it is valid upon children as subordinates. So whichever of them pays, he is forced to accept. As for the present: because the entire payment is upon him. As for the absent: because he attains the honor of freedom, even though the payment is not upon him. (Thus it becomes like the case of the one who gives a loan to be pawned: its form is that a man borrows an item from another to pawn it against a debt... [1] His statement: for the share of the present; because the present is his own guardian, and it is suspended for the share of the absent due to lack of guardianship over him, like one who sells his own slave and another's slave, or marries his own slave woman and another's slave woman, or marries his small daughter and another's small daughter. [2] His statement: just as it is valid upon children; meaning that kitaba in this manner is legislated, like when a slave woman is given kitaba, her children enter into her kitaba as subordinates, so they are emancipated upon her payment, and no payment is upon them. [3] His statement: whichever of them pays, he is forced to accept; meaning the master is forced to accept. As for the present: because the payment is upon him, so the master is forced to accept his payment, and the absent also becomes free due to his inclusion in the kitaba of the present as a subordinate, just as the child of a mukataba, or due to the ta'liq and existence of the condition. As for when the absent pays: because he attains the honor of freedom through this payment, so he is not like a stranger but rather like the child of a mukataba. So in al-Kifaya. [4] His statement: As for the absent... etc.; meaning that qiyas in this is that the master is not forced to accept if the absent pays, because he is a stranger and a volunteer, since no payment is upon him. The basis of istihsan: he has a benefit because he attains the honor of freedom... etc. So in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. Kitab al-Mukatab: His transactions, legal incapacity, and rulings. And he does not have recourse against the other. Payment for the other, so he pawns it. Then the one who gave the item for pawn needed to reclaim his item. If he pays the debt to the pledgee, the pledgee is forced to accept, even if the mughri (the one who gave the item for pawn) has no debt, but it is upon the borrower. If he pays the debt, he has recourse against the borrower for it; if he pays without his command, because he is compelled to reclaim his item and cannot achieve it except by payment. (And he does not have recourse against the other; because he is a volunteer regarding the other. Only the mughri has recourse because he is compelled in payment, because he fears loss of his property in the hands of the pledgee. [1] His statement: And even if there is no debt upon the mughri; so here too the master is forced to accept from the absent, even though the payment is not upon him, because he needs to obtain freedom. So in Sharh al-Ayni. [2] His statement: And he does not have recourse against the other; the conclusion is that whichever of them pays, he does not have recourse against his companion. As for the present: because he has paid a debt owed by him, and the commentator did not address it due to its obviousness. As for the absent: because he is a volunteer in payment, not compelled. It might be said: the mughri is considered compelled to pay the debt of the borrower to recover his property, and the need to recover oneself is stronger, so it is more appropriate to consider the absent compelled to pay the kitaba payment. So it is more appropriate to say: the absent, by payment, acts for himself because his benefit returns to him, namely the freedom of his person, so he does not have recourse for what profit comes back to him against another, unlike the mughri. So in Hashiyat Mulla al-Haddad. (3) His statement: Only the mughri has recourse is a response to what is said: the absent here is like the mughri, and he is compelled, and therefore he has recourse against the borrower for what he paid. Explanation: that the mughri is like the absent, and in the permissibility of payment without any debt upon him, but not in compulsion. For compulsion is only when something existing is lost for him, and here it is not so, but rather it is only...
Source pages p. 32

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding on a ruling from Al-Wiqaya regarding a special case of kitaba (contract of manumission by payment). The case involves two people who jointly write a kitaba contract for two slaves: one present (al-hadir) and one absent (al-gha'ib). The present slave enters the contract on behalf of both, making himself the principal and the absent a dependent. The author explains the qiyas (analogy) ruling and then the istihsan (juristic preference) that overrides it. Qiyas would say the kitaba is valid immediately for the present slave's share, but for the absent slave it is suspended until his acceptance (due to lack of guardianship). However, istihsan considers the benefit: the absent slave gains the honor of freedom without any payment obligation, so the kitaba is valid like the case of children entering their mother's kitaba as dependents. The passage then draws an analogy with the case of mu'ir al-rahn (one who lends an item to be pawned for another's debt). In that case, if the lender pays the debt to reclaim his item, he is forced to accept because he is compelled. Similarly, if the absent slave pays the kitaba amount, the master is forced to accept because the slave is compelled by his need for freedom. However, unlike the mughri lender, the absent slave does not have recourse against the present slave because he acts for his own benefit. The commentary reconciles differences and cites sources like Al-Kifaya, Hashiyat al-Jalabi, Sharh al-Ayni, and Hashiyat Mulla al-Haddad.

Key Terms
معير الرهن (mu'ir al-rahn): A person who lends an item to another to be pawned as collateral for a debt. In the analogy, he pays the debt to reclaim his property.
الاضطرار (al-idtarar): Compulsion or necessity that permits an otherwise impermissible act. Here, it justifies the master's forced acceptance.
الرجوع (al-ruju'): Recourse or the right to claim reimbursement from another. The absent slave does not have this right.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling, why does the absent slave not have recourse against the present slave after paying the kitaba?
A) Because the absent is a stranger and his payment is invalid.
B) Because the absent's payment brings him the personal benefit of freedom.
C) Because the present slave also paid his share.
D) Because the master refunded the absent's payment.

Key Takeaway
The absent slave, though compelled to pay like the mu'ir al-rahn, does not have recourse because his payment results in his own freedom, a personal benefit.

Recovery When Paying Another's Debt: Compulsion vs Volunteering
Ruling on when payment of another's debt is considered donation vs compelled

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that recovery of payment made for another's debt depends on whether the payer is compelled to prevent loss of an existing asset.

Core Idea
A payer recovers only if compelled to prevent loss of an existing asset, not when gaining a new benefit.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi distinction: when you pay someone else's debt, do you have the right to recover from them? The answer hinges on whether you were compelled (mudtarr) or acted voluntarily (mutabarri'). The source passage examines two scenarios: a lender of collateral (mu'ir) who pays the borrower's mortgage debt to reclaim his property, and an absent slave (gha'ib) who pays the kitaba debt. In the first case, recovery is allowed; in the second, it is not. Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the rules of tabarru' and idtirar in daily transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and identify the two cases: (1) the lender of collateral (mu'ir) pays the debt of the borrower (musta'ir) to the mortgagee (murtahin); (2) the absent slave (gha'ib) pays the kitaba debt. Note the ruling in each: mu'ir recovers, gha'ib does not.
The third party (mu'ir or gha'ib) is not the original debtor.
2
State the apparent analogy
Both parties pay a debt that is not originally theirs. At first glance, both appear to be volunteers. However, the Hanafi ruling treats them differently. Identify the objection raised in the text: 'the absent slave is like the lender of collateral, so he should also recover.'
This is the qiyas khafi that is refuted.
3
Discover the underlying cause (illa)
The distinguishing factor is compulsion (idtirar). The mu'ir is compelled because his property (the collateral) is at risk of being lost. The gha'ib, however, is not losing anything; he is seeking to gain freedom. Find where the text says the mu'ir fears loss of his property (ماله) while the gha'ib acts for his own benefit (عتق رقبته).
Compulsion exists only when something already possessed is at stake.
4
Apply the principle to a new case
Suppose a person's car is used as collateral for a friend's loan. The friend defaults, and the owner pays the loan to avoid losing the car. He can recover from the friend. Now suppose a person pays a friend's debt voluntarily to improve his friend's credit. He cannot recover. Why? Because in the first case he is compelled (loss of car), in the second he is a volunteer.
The ruling depends on the presence of idtirar.
5
Formulate the general rule
Based on the two scenarios, derive the rule: 'Whoever pays another's debt in order to prevent loss of his own property may recover; whoever pays to acquire a new benefit may not.' The Hanafi school uses istihsan to prefer this distinction over the literal analogy.
This rule appears in chapters of kitaba and rahn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المكاتب تصرفاته وعجزه وأحكامه

ولم يرجع على الآخر
عليه للآخر، فرهنه، ثمَّ احتاجَ المعيرُ إلى استخلاص عينه، فإِنْ أَذًى الدِّينَ إلى المرتهن يجبر المُرْتَهِنُ على القبول، وإن لم يكن على معير الرهن دين، وإنما هو على المستعير، وإن أدى الدين يرجع على المستعير به، وإن أدى بغير أمره، لأنه مضطر إلى تخليص عينه ولا يتمكن إلا بأداء الدين.
(ولم يرجع على الآخر؛ لأنه متبرع في حق الآخر، وإنما يرجع معير الرهن؛ لأنه مضطر في الأداء؛ لأنه يخافُ تَلَفَ مَالِه في يد المرتهن)

[1] قوله: وإن لم يكن على معير الرهن دين؛ فكذا هاهنا يجبر المولى على القبول من الغائب، وإن لم يكن البدل عليه؛ لأنه محتاج إلى استفادة الحرية. كذا في «شرح العيني».

[2] قوله: ولم يرجع على الآخر؛ الحاصل أنه أيهما أدى لا يرجع على صاحبه، أما الحاضر؛ فلأنه قضى دينا عليه ولم يتعرّض له الشارح لظهوره، وأما الغائب؛ فلأنه متبرع بالأداء غير مضطر إليه. قد يقال: معير الرهن اعتبر مضطراً إلى أداء دين المستعير؛ لاستخلاص المال، والحاجة إلى استخلاص نفسه أقوى، فالأولى أن يعتبر الغائب مضطراً إلى أداء بدل الكتابة، فالأولى أن يقال: الغائب بالأداء عامل لنفسه؛ لرجوع منفعته إليه، وهو عتقُ رقبته فلا يرجع بما نفعه يعود إليه على الغير بخلاف معير الرهن. هكذا في «حاشية ملا الهداد».

[3] قوله: وإنما يرجع معير الرهن جواب عما قيل: الغائب هاهنا كمعير الرهن، وهو مضطر؛ ولهذا يرجع على المستعير بما أدّى. تقريره: إنّ المعير كالغائب، وفي جواز الأداء من غير دين عليه لا في الاضطرار، فإنّ الاضطرار إنّما هو إذا فات له شيء حاصل، وهاهنا ليس كذلك، بل إنما هو
Book of the Mukatab (slave under contract of manumission): His transactions, incapacity, and rulings. And he does not recover from the other. Upon him for the other, he mortgaged it. Then the lender needed to reclaim his property. So if he pays the debt to the mortgagee, the mortgagee is compelled to accept. And if there is no debt on the lender of the collateral, but rather on the borrower, and he pays the debt, he recovers from the borrower, even if he paid without his order, because he is compelled to reclaim his property and cannot do so except by paying the debt. (And he does not recover from the other because he is a volunteer regarding the other. The lender of the collateral recovers because he is compelled in payment, as he fears the loss of his property in the hands of the mortgagee.) [1] He said: 'And if there is no debt on the lender of the collateral' – likewise here, the master is compelled to accept from the absent slave, even if the debt is not upon him, because he needs to obtain freedom. As in Sharh al-Ayni. [2] He said: 'And he does not recover from the other' – the result is that whichever one pays does not recover from his companion. As for the present, because he paid a debt upon himself, and the commentator did not mention it due to its obviousness. As for the absent, because he is a volunteer in payment, not compelled. It could be said: The lender of the collateral is considered compelled to pay the debt of the borrower to reclaim the property, and the need to reclaim oneself is stronger; so it is more appropriate to consider the absent slave compelled to pay the kitaba debt. So it is more appropriate to say: The absent slave, by paying, acts for his own benefit, as the benefit returns to him, which is the freedom of his neck, so he does not recover what benefits him from another, unlike the lender of the collateral. Thus in Hashiya of Mulla al-Hidad. [3] He said: 'And the lender of the collateral recovers' is a response to what was said: The absent here is like the lender of the collateral, and he is compelled; therefore he recovers from the borrower what he paid. Its conclusion: The lender is like the absent in the permissibility of payment without debt upon him, not in compulsion. For compulsion only occurs when something already possessed is lost, and here it is not the case, rather it is...
Source pages p. 33

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-with-proof from the Book of Mukatab, addressing whether a person who pays another's debt can recover from that other. The matn states: 'And he does not recover from the other.' The commentator explains this in the context of a borrowed item (ariyah) being mortgaged by the borrower (musta'ir). The lender (mu'ir) needs to reclaim his property, so he pays the debt to the mortgagee (murtahin) even without the borrower's order, and he can recover from the borrower because he is compelled (mudtarr) – he fears losing his property. However, if there are two slaves under kitaba, a present one and an absent one, and the absent one pays the kitaba debt, he does not recover from the present one because he is a volunteer (mutabarri'), not compelled. The commentator distinguishes: the lender is compelled because he is losing something he already possesses, but the absent slave is gaining his freedom, not preventing a loss. Therefore the absent slave acts for his own benefit, so no recovery. The hashia of Mulla al-Hidad adds that the absent slave is acting for himself because the benefit (freedom) returns to him, so he cannot claim reimbursement. The third footnote resolves an objection: the absent slave is not compelled because he is not losing any property; he is merely seeking freedom. This is a classic Hanafi application of istihsan (juristic preference) based on the concept of necessity (darurah) and the distinction between regaining possession and acquiring new benefit.

Key Terms
المعير (al-mu'ir): The lender of a tangible item (ariyah) who allows another to use it without compensation.
المستعير (al-musta'ir): The borrower of the item (musta'ir) who uses it and may mortgage it to a third party.
المرتهن (al-murtahin): The mortgagee, the creditor who holds the collateral (rahn) as security for a debt.
الغائب (al-gha'ib): The absent slave (mukatab) who is not present when the kitaba contract is concluded or when payment is made.
الحاضر (al-hadir): The present slave who is physically present and accepts the kitaba contract.
متبرع (mutabarri'): A person who performs an act of payment or service voluntarily without being legally obliged, considered a donor.
مضطر (mudtarr): A person who is compelled by necessity to act, such as paying a debt to prevent loss of property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why does the absent slave not recover from the present slave when he pays the kitaba debt?
A) Because the present slave is not liable for the absent slave's debt.
B) Because the absent slave is a volunteer (mutabarri') acting for his own benefit (freedom).
C) Because the master (mawla) must accept the payment from the absent slave.
D) Because the absent slave is compelled to pay to avoid losing his property.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, recovery for paying another's debt requires compulsion (idtirar) to prevent loss of one's own property; voluntary payment for one's own benefit is a donation without right to reimbursement.
