Kitabah of a Jointly Owned Slave with Authorization
Ruling and commentary on authorization in kitabah of joint slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when one partner authorizes the other to write a kitabah on his share and receive part of the payment, and explain why the partial payment belongs exclusively to the authorized partner.

Core Idea
In a jointly owned slave, partial payment received by the authorized partner belongs entirely to him.

This passage presents a specific scenario in the chapter on kitabah (indenture) of jointly owned slaves. When one co-owner authorizes the other to contract kitabah on his share and to collect the payment, and the authorized partner only receives part of the payment before the slave defaults, who gets that money? The Hanafi ruling provides a clear answer based on the principle of divisibility of kitabah and the legal effect of authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the authorization
There is a slave owned jointly by two partners. One partner authorizes the other to write a kitabah on his (the authorized partner's) share for 1000 dirhams and to receive the payment.
The authorization is twofold: to write the kitabah and to receive the payment.
2
Determine what happened
The authorized partner writes the kitabah and receives only part of the 1000 dirhams. The slave becomes unable (aajiz) to pay the remainder.
The condition 'part of it' is crucial – if all payment was received, the slave would be fully freed and no issue of inability arises.
3
Apply the ruling
According to Abu Hanifah, the partial payment belongs entirely to the authorized partner who received it. The authorizing partner has no claim on it.
The basis is that kitabah is divisible (mutajazzi'ah); the contract only affects the share of the partner who writes it.
4
Understand the legal effects of authorization
Authorization (idhn) has two benefits: (1) it prevents the authorizing partner from revoking the kitabah, and (2) it makes the receiver a donor (mutabarri') – the received money is his alone.
Al-Zayla'i explains that the authorization for receipt cuts off the authorizing partner's right to that money.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب كتابة العبد المشترك
أحد شريكي عبد أذن للآخر بكتابة حصته بألف وقبضه ففعل وقبض بعضه، فذا له إن عجز. الضمير في حصته يرجع إلى الآخر، هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأصله: أن الكتابة متجزئة فيكون مقتصراً على نصيبه، وفائدة الإذن. قوله: باب كتابة العبد المشترك; أخره; لأن الأصل عدم الاشتراك، والاثنين بعد الواحد. كذا في رد المحتار.
قوله: أحد شريكي عبد... الخ; أي إذا كان العبد بين شريكين أذن أحدهما لصاحبه أن يكاتب حصته بألف درهم، ويقبض بدل الكتابة، فكاتب الشريك المأذون له، نفذ في حصته فقط إذا قبض بعضه; أي بعض الألف فعجز، فالمقبوض كله للقابض; لإذنه له بالقبض، فيكون متبرعاً. كذا في الدر المختار.
قوله: أذن للآخر; قال الزيلعي: فائدة الإذن بالكتابة أن لا يكون له حق الفسخ كما لم يأذن، وفائدة إذنه بالقبض أن ينقطع حقه فيما قبض. كذا في رد المحتار.
قوله: وقبض بعضه; وإنما خص البعض; لأن مدار المسألة على العجز، وذا لا يتصور بعد قبض الكل; لأنه لو قبض الكل عتق حظ القابض كله، فامتنع العجز. كذا في حاشية شرح جلبي.
قوله: وفائدة الإذن... الخ; جواب عن سؤال مقدر تقريره.
Chapter of the kitabah of a jointly owned slave. One of two partners of a slave authorized the other to write a kitabah for his share for one thousand and to receive it, so he did and received part of it, then it is for him if the slave becomes unable. The pronoun 'his share' refers to the other partner; this is according to Abu Hanifah. Its basis is that kitabah is divisible, so it is confined to his share. And the benefit of the authorization... He said: The chapter of kitabah of the jointly owned slave; he delayed it because the original state is no co-ownership, and two comes after one. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. He said: 'One of two partners of a slave...' meaning if the slave is between two partners, one authorized his companion to write a kitabah for his share for one thousand dirhams and to receive the kitabah payment, so the authorized partner wrote the kitabah; it takes effect only in his share if he received part of it, i.e., part of the thousand, and the slave became unable, then all that was received is for the receiver because the authorization allowed him to receive, so he is a donor. Thus in al-Durr al-Mukhtar. He said: 'Authorized the other'; al-Zayla'i said: The benefit of the authorization for the kitabah is that he does not have the right of revocation as if he had not authorized, and the benefit of his authorization for receipt is that his right is cut off regarding what was received. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. He said: 'And he received part of it'; he specifically mentioned part because the pivot of the issue is inability, and this is not conceivable after receipt of all because if he received all, the share of the receiver would be fully freed, so inability is prevented. Thus in Hashiyat Sharh Jilbi. He said: 'And the benefit of the authorization...' is a response to an implied question...
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on the kitabah of a jointly owned slave. The matn states: If one of two partners authorizes the other to write a kitabah for his share for 1000 dirhams and to receive the payment, and the authorized partner does so and receives part of the payment, then if the slave becomes unable to pay the rest, that partial payment belongs to the authorized partner. The commentary explains that the pronoun 'his share' refers to the share of the authorized partner, not the authorizing partner. This is Abu Hanifah's position, based on the principle that kitabah is divisible, so the contract only affects the share of the partner who writes it. The authorization has two benefits: (1) it prevents the authorizing partner from revoking the kitabah, and (2) it allows the authorized partner to keep the money received, making him a donor (mutabarri'). The condition that only part of the payment is received is critical because if the full amount were received, the slave's share would be fully freed and there would be no question of inability.

Key Terms
عبد مشترك (abd mushtarak): A slave owned by two or more partners.
كتابة متجزئة (kitabah mutajazzi'ah): The concept that a kitabah contract can be divided among shares.
إذن (idhn): Authorization given by one partner to the other to perform a kitabah and receive payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if one partner authorizes the other to write a kitabah for his share and receive payment, and the authorized partner receives part of the payment before the slave becomes unable to pay the rest, what happens to the amount received?
A) It is returned to the slave.
B) It is shared equally between the partners.
C) It belongs entirely to the authorized partner.
D) It belongs entirely to the authorizing partner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when one partner authorizes the other to write kitabah on his share and receive payment, any partial payment belongs exclusively to the authorized partner due to the legal effect of authorization.

Ruling when One Master Fathers a Child from a Joint Mukatab Slave
Explains the ruling when one master fathers a child from a joint mukatab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the financial obligations of a master who fathers a child from a jointly owned mukatab slave-woman and explain the effect of her subsequent inability to pay the kitaba.

Core Idea
Fathering a child from a joint mukatab triggers immediate compensation and retroactively vests full ownership upon inability.

This passage examines the rulings when one co-owner of a mukatab slave-woman fathers a child from her. The Hanafi school holds that the child is free and belongs to the father, but he must compensate his partner. The analysis clarifies how the slave-woman's eventual inability to fulfill the kitaba confirms that full ownership had already transferred to the father at the time of istilad, invalidating any contradictory claims like tadbir.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is istilad (fathering a child) from a jointly owned mukatab. The ruling is that the child belongs to the father, and he must pay half the uqr (bridal payment) and half the current value of the slave to his partner.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a rational inference: upon the slave's inability to pay the kitaba, it becomes clear that the father owned the partner's share at the time of istilad. This retroactive confirmation justifies the compensation and invalidates any contradictory tadbir.
This is a form of istidlal (rational proof) based on ownership analysis.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The key logical link: the slave's inability reveals that the father's ownership over the whole slave was established at the moment of istilad. Therefore, the compensation to the partner is essentially payment for the share that had transferred to the father.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
There is consensus on the validity of istilad from a mukatab, but the details of compensation and the effect of freeing before inability may differ among schools or imams. The text adopts Abu Hanifa's view on the freeing scenario.
The passage mentions that before inability, the partner cannot claim guarantee from the freeer – this is Abu Hanifa's position.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The text relies on Abu Hanifa's ruling that the freeer's guarantee only arises after the slave becomes unable. This is the mufta bihi position, as the commentary does not mention an alternative from the Sahibayn.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text

والولد له ، وضَمِنَ لشريكه نصف عقرها ، ونصف قيمتها، فإن حررها أحدهما غنيّاً فعجزتْ ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتها لشريكه، ورجع به عليها؛ لأنه تبين بالعجز أنه يملك نصيب الشريك وقت الاستيلاد، فالتدبير وقع في غير ملكه بخلافِ النسب؛ لأنه يعتمد الغرور. (فإن حررها): أي المكاتبة المشتركة، أحدهما غنيّاً فعجزت ضَمِنَ نصف قيمتها لشريكه، ورجع به عليها) في قول أبي حنيفة له، ثم إذا عجزت بعد ذلك جعلت الكتابة كأن لم تكن، وتبيَّن أنّ الجارية كلّها أم ولد للأوّل؛ لأنه زالَ المانع من الانتقال. كذا في «الكفاية». قوله: والولد له؛ أي للأول؛ لأنه صحت دعوته لقيام المصحح وهو الملك في المكاتبة؛ لأن استيلاده كان عند قيام الكتابة، ويصح استيلاد المكاتبة بالإجماع؛ لأن الاختلاف مع بقاء الكتابة، وهاهنا ما بقيت لأنه لما استولدها الأول ملك نصف شريكه، ولم يبق ملك للمدبّر فيها، فلا يصح تدبيره. كذا في «الكفاية) و«العناية». قوله: ونصف قيمتها؛ لأنه تملك نصفها بالاستيلاد، وهو تملك بالقيمة. قوله: فإن حررها؛ صورته: إن كاتب الجارية المشتركة ثمّ أعتقها أحدهما وهو موسر، ثم عجزت يضمن المعتق لشريكه نصف قيمتها، ويرجع بذلك عليها. قوله: فعجزت؛ وأما قبل العجز ليس له أن يضمن المعتق عند الأعظم له؛ لأنَّ الإعتاق لما كان يتجزؤ عنده كان أثره أن يجعل نصيب غير المعتق كالمكاتب، فلا يتغيّر بالإعتاق نصيب صاحبه؛ لأنها مكاتبة قبل ذلك.
And the child belongs to him, and he guarantees to his partner half of her bridal payment (uqr) and half of her value. If one of them frees her while wealthy and she becomes unable (to pay), he guarantees half of her value to his partner and recovers it from her. Because it becomes clear upon her inability that he owned the partner's share at the time of istilad, so the tadbir occurred in non-ownership, unlike lineage, because lineage relies on deception. (If he frees her): meaning the joint mukatab, one of them frees her while wealthy and she becomes unable, he guarantees half her value to his partner and recovers it from her, in the view of Abu Hanifa. Then if she becomes unable after that, the kitaba is considered as if it never existed, and it becomes clear that the entire slave-girl is an umm walad for the first master, because the obstacle to transfer has been removed. As in al-Kifayah. His statement: 'And the child belongs to him' means to the first master, because his claim is validated due to the presence of the correct basis, which is ownership in the mukatab, since his istilad occurred while the kitaba was in effect. Istilad of a mukatab is valid by consensus, because the disagreement (about its validity) is while the kitaba remains, and here it did not remain because when the first master performed istilad, he owned half of his partner, and no ownership remained for the mudabbir in her, so his tadbir is invalid. As in al-Kifayah and al-Inayah. His statement: 'And half of her value' because he owns half of her by istilad, and that is ownership by value. His statement: 'Then if he frees her' its form: if he writes a kitaba for the joint slave-girl, then one of them frees her while wealthy, then she becomes unable, the freeer guarantees to his partner half her value and recovers it from her. His statement: 'she becomes unable' and before inability, he is not allowed to claim guarantee from the freeer according to the great one (Abu Hanifa), because since freeing is divisible in his view, its effect is to make the share of the non-freeer like a mukatab, so the freeer's share does not change his partner's share, because she was a mukatab before that.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning a jointly owned mukatab slave-woman when one master fathers a child (istilad) from her. The ruling states that the child belongs to the father-master, and he must compensate his partner by paying half the uqr (the bridal payment for a slave-woman) and half the current value of the slave-woman. The proof given is that upon the slave-woman's inability to pay the kitaba (contract of manumission), it becomes clear that the father already owned the partner's share at the time of istilad. Therefore, any tadbir (postponed emancipation) made by the other master was invalid regarding that share, because the mudabbir had no ownership at the time. The commentary then elaborates that the child goes to the first master because his claim of paternity is valid due to his ownership in the mukatab at the time of conception. Istilad of a mukatab is valid by scholarly consensus. The passage also discusses a scenario where one master frees the slave-woman while wealthy, and she later becomes unable to pay. In that case, the freeing master guarantees half her value to his partner and can recover that amount from her. This is the view of Abu Hanifa. Before her inability, the partner cannot claim guarantee from the freeer, because the freeing action is divisible in Abu Hanifa's view: it leaves the partner's share as if it is still a mukatab, so the freeer's action does not affect his partner's share.

Key Terms
استيلاد (istilad): Fathering a child from a female slave by her master, which establishes her as umm walad.
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A female slave who has made a contract of manumission (kitaba) with her master to purchase her freedom in installments.
عقر (uqr): The compensatory payment for sexual intercourse with a slave-woman, typically her value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does a master who fathers a child from a jointly owned mukatab owe to his partner?
A) Only the value of the child
B) Half of the uqr and half of the slave's value
C) Full value of the slave
D) Nothing, because the child is free

Key Takeaway
The master who fathers a child from a jointly owned mukatab must compensate his partner with half the uqr and half the slave's value; later inability retroactively confirms full ownership and voids the kitaba.

Options of a Joint Owner After Tadbir or I'taq by Co-Owner
States the ruling on a jointly owned slave when one master makes tadbir and the other frees

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the options available to a joint owner under Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn when the other owner has made tadbir or freed the slave, and explain how the principle of divisibility (tajazzu') affects the ruling.

Core Idea
Tadbir and emancipation are divisible: each co-owner's action affects only his share, giving the silent partner multiple options.

When two people jointly own a slave, they each have rights over their share. What happens if one owner makes tadbir (postponed emancipation) and the other immediately frees the slave? Does the silent owner lose all rights? This passage reveals a sharp intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, over the divisibility of emancipation. Understanding this dispute clarifies how the Hanafi school balances joint ownership with individual ownership rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage presents two sequences: (1) one owner makes tadbir first, then the other frees; (2) one frees first, then the other makes tadbir. Note which owner is 'silent' in each case.
The silent owner is the one who did not perform the second action; his options are at stake.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position for scenario 1
According to Abu Hanifa, the silent owner (the one who made tadbir) has three options: free the slave, make the slave work (istis'a'), or claim guarantee (tadmin) from the freeing master. He can choose any.
Freeing is always an option because the silent owner can complete the emancipation.
3
State the Sahibayn's position for scenario 1
The Sahibayn restrict the silent owner: he can only claim guarantee if the freeer is wealthy, or make the slave work if the freeer is poor. He cannot free because emancipation is indivisible.
They consider the whole slave emancipated by the second owner's action, so the silent owner's share is also freed, leaving no room to free again.
4
Compare scenario 2
When the freeing occurs first, then tadbir, Abu Hanifa gives the silent owner (the one who freed) two options: free or istis'a', but NOT tadmin. The Sahibayn again restrict to guarantee (if wealthy) or istis'a'.
The difference in scenario 2: the freeer acted first, so the one who made tadbir later cannot claim guarantee from the freeer because the freeer's action preceded.
5
Identify the underlying principle
The dispute hinges on whether tadbir and i'taq are divisible (yatajazzau). Abu Hanifa says yes; the Sahibayn say no. This determines whether the silent owner retains independent control over his share.
This is a talil (reasoning) argument: the ruling is based on the nature of the act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد لرجلين دبره أحدهما، ثُمَّ حرّره الآخر مليئاً أو عكساً أعتق المدبر، واستسعى فيهما أو ضمن شريكه في الأولى فقط: هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما لا يرجع، وهذا مبني على أن الساكت إذا ضَمَّنَ المعتق يرجع به عليها عند أبي حنيفة له لا عندهما. (عبد لرجلين) دبرَهُ أحدهما، ثُمَّ حرّره الآخر مليئاً أو عكساً: أي حرره أحدهما، ثُمَّ دبَّرَهُ الآخر، أعتق المدبر، واستسعى فيهما: أي في المسألتين، (أو ضمن شريكه في الأولى فقط، اعلم أنَّ في المسألة الأُولى إذا دبَّرَهُ الأَوَّل، فللثاني الإعتاق، أو التضمين، أو الاستسعاء عند أبي حنيفة، فإذا أعتق الثاني لم يبقَ له ولاية التضمين والاستسعاء وعندهما: لما كان لا يتجزء بعتق الكلِّ فله أن يضمنه قيمته نصيبه مكاتباً إن كان موسراً، ويستسعي العبد إن كان معسراً؛ لأنه ضمان إعتاق، فيختلف باليسار والإعسار. كذا في «الهداية». قوله: هذا عند أبي حنيفة له؛ وعندهما لا يرجع؛ لأنها لما عجزت وردّت في الرق تصير كأنها لم تنزل فيه على الخلاف في الرجوع، وفي الخيارات عند أبي حنيفة الساكت مخيّر بين الخيارات الثلاث، إن شاءَ،أعتق، وإن شاء استسعى العبد، وإن شاء ضمن شريكه قيمة نصيبه وعندهما ليس له إلا الضمان مع اليسار، والسعاية مع العسار. قوله: عبد لرجلين.. الخ؛ أي إن كان العبد بين رجلين دبره أحدهما ثم أعتقه الآخر وهو موسر، فإن شاءَ الذي دبَرَه ضَمِّنَ المعتق نصف قيمته مدبّراً، أو استسعى العبد أو أعتق، وإن أعتقه أحدهما ثمَّ دبَّره الآخر، فإن شاء الذي دبره استسعى العبد، أو أعتق ولم يكن له أن يُضَمِّنَ المعتق، كما في المسألة الأولى، وهذا عند أبي حنيفة له بناء على أن التدبير يتجزء عنده. قوله: لم تبق له ولاية التضمين .. الخ؛ لأنّ أثر التضمين أن يملك الضامن
A slave belonging to two men, one of them made tadbir for him, then the other freed him while wealthy, or vice versa (i.e., one freed then the other made tadbir). The mudabbir is freed, and he is given the option of istis'a' in both cases, or he may claim guarantee from his partner in the first case only. This is according to Abu Hanifa. But according to them (the two Sahibayn), he does not recourse. This is based on the principle that the silent one, if he claims guarantee from the freeer, he recourses from her (the slave) according to Abu Hanifa, not according to them. (A slave belonging to two men...) one of them made tadbir for him, then the other freed him while wealthy, or vice versa: meaning one freed him then the other made tadbir. The mudabbir is freed, and he is given istis'a' in both cases (i.e., in both scenarios), or he may claim guarantee from his partner in the first case only. Know that in the first case, if the first made tadbir, then the second has the option of freeing, claiming guarantee, or istis'a' according to Abu Hanifa. If the second frees, he no longer has the authority of guarantee and istis'a'. According to them (the two Sahibayn), since freeing is not divisible, he may claim from him the value of his share as a mukatab if he is wealthy, and make the slave work if he is poor, because it is a guarantee of freeing, so it varies with wealth and poverty. As in al-Hidayah. His statement: 'This is according to Abu Hanifa; according to them he does not recourse' because when she becomes unable and returns to slavery, it is as if the claim of recourse did not occur, according to the difference of opinion on recourse. In the options, according to Abu Hanifa, the silent one is given a choice among three options: he may free, or make the slave work (istis'a'), or claim guarantee from his partner the value of his share. According to them, he only has guarantee if the freeer is wealthy, and istis'a' if the freeer is poor. His statement: 'A slave belonging to two men...' meaning if a slave is between two men, one made tadbir, then the other freed him while wealthy, then the one who made tadbir may, if he wishes, claim from the freeer half his value as a mudabbir, or make the slave work, or free him. And if one freed him then the other made tadbir, then the one who made tadbir may, if he wishes, make the slave work, or free him, and he cannot claim guarantee from the freeer, as in the first case. This is according to Abu Hanifa, based on the principle that tadbir is divisible in his view. His statement: 'he no longer has the authority of guarantee...' because the effect of guarantee is that the guarantor owns.
Source pages p. 43

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn) regarding the options available to a joint owner when the other owner has made tadbir (postponed emancipation) or freed the slave. The case involves a slave jointly owned by two men. In the first scenario, one makes tadbir, then the other frees the slave while wealthy. According to Abu Hanifa, the silent owner (the one who did not act) has three options: he can free the slave himself (which would complete the emancipation), or he can make the slave work to pay his share (istis'a'), or he can claim a guarantee (tadmin) from the freeing master for half the value of the slave as a mudabbir. Once the second owner chooses to free, he loses the other options. The Sahibayn disagree: they say that since emancipation is not divisible, the silent owner can only claim the value from the freeer if he is wealthy, or make the slave work if he is poor, but he cannot free the slave because that would be redundant. In the second scenario, where one frees first and then the other makes tadbir, the mudabbir (the one who made tadbir) has options according to Abu Hanifa: he can either free or make the slave work, but he cannot claim guarantee from the freeer. The Sahibayn again restrict the options. The basis of the dispute is whether tadbir and emancipation are divisible (yatajazzau). Abu Hanifa considers them divisible, so the joint owner's actions affect only his share, leaving the other's share intact. The Sahibayn consider them indivisible, so the action of one affects the whole slave, limiting the other's options.

Key Terms
تدبير (tadbir): A master's declaration that his slave will be free upon the master's death, making the slave a mudabbir.
إعتاق (i'taq): The act of immediate manumission of a slave.
استسعاء (istis'a'): Making the slave work to pay off his value, typically after partial emancipation.
تضمين (tadmin): Claiming a guarantee of value from the co-owner who freed or made tadbir.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, how many options does the silent owner have if the first owner made tadbir and the second freed the slave while wealthy?
A) One option: claim guarantee only
B) Two options: claim guarantee or istis'a'
C) Three options: free, istis'a', or claim guarantee
D) No options: the slave is free

Key Takeaway
The silent owner's options depend on the order of acts and the divisibility of emancipation: Abu Hanifa gives full flexibility, while the Sahibayn restrict based on the freeer's wealth.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Coercion (Ikrah) and Its Effect on Contracts: Fasid or Mawquf?
Defines coercion and rules on validity of coerced contracts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced contracts, distinguish between fasid and mawquf, explain the proof from Quran 4:29, and apply the condition of consent to determine the validity of a coerced transaction.

Core Idea
Coerced consent makes a contract fasid, not batil – the buyer owns upon possession.

Coercion negates the mutual consent required for a valid contract, but in Hanafi fiqh, the contract is not void (batil) – it is voidable (fasid). This means the contract is concluded but defective, and the coerced party has the right to annul or ratify it after the coercion ends. In this lesson, we explore the definition of coercion, the legal ruling on coerced sales, the proof from the Quran, and a key intra-Hanafi disagreement with Imam Zufar.

Guided Path
1
Define coercion and identify its types
First, understand what constitutes ikrah according to the Hanafi school: it varies by person, but essentially involves a threat of harm that overcomes the will without making the person completely incapacitated (ghayr mulji'). The passage distinguishes between severe beating, prolonged imprisonment, and harsh speech for the noble.
Coercion must negate consent (rida) but not render the person completely involuntary.
2
Identify the ruling on coerced contracts
State the Hanafi position: a contract made under coercion is fasid (voidable), not batil. This means it is concluded (mun'aqid) but execution (nafadh) is suspended. The coerced party has the option to annul (faskh) or ratify (imda').
Contrast with batil contracts where the rukn is missing.
3
Locate the dalil and trace its legal implication
The proof is Quran 4:29: 'except that it be trade by mutual consent.' The verse makes mutual consent a condition for a valid trade. Its absence due to coercion removes the condition, making the contract fasid, not batil, because the rukn (offer and acceptance) is still present.
The absence of condition causes fasad, not butlan.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Zufar differs: he holds the coerced sale is mawquf (suspended), meaning ownership does not transfer until the coerced party ratifies. Understand his reasoning and the majority's response: the rukn is sound, so the contract is concluded; defect only affects execution.
The practical consequence: under the majority view, the buyer owns upon possession; under Zufar's, no ownership until ratification.
5
Apply the ruling to a specific case
Consider coercion by threatening to imprison a person's family member. The default ruling is that this is not considered coercion (since it is not overwhelming), but by istihsan, it is treated as coercion. Understand how istihsan overrides qiyas in Hanafi methodology.
The relied-upon view is that imprisoning any mahram relative is coercion by istihsan.

Book of Coercion / Its definition, conditions, and rulings. And the coerced person refraining from what he is coerced to do before it accepts it for his right, or for the right of another, or for the right of the Shari'ah. [1] Know that this differs according to people, for the lowly may not be deterred by beating or imprisonment. So mild beating is not coercion in their case, rather severe beating, and likewise imprisonment unless it is prolonged imprisonment that causes distress. The noble are deterred by speech that contains harshness, so such is coercion for them. ... And the coerced person refraining from what he is coerced to do accepts it for his right: like selling his property, or destroying it, or freeing his slave; or for the right of another: like destroying the property of another; or for the right of the Shari'ah: like drinking wine and adultery. [If he is coerced by killing, or severe beating, or imprisonment until he sold, or bought, or confessed, or leased, he may annul or ratify.] For these contracts require consent, so coercion that negates consent, and it is not overwhelming (mulji'), prevents their execution but they are concluded, and he has the option to annul or ratify. [1] His saying: 'al-mubarrah' - with ra and ha without dots - the severe painful. Thus understood from al-Sihah. [2] His saying: 'So such ...' etc.; so Muhammad said: There is no fixed measure in that, rather it is according to what is seen from the situation of one who is afflicted with it, because fixing measures is not by opinion. From Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [3] His saying: 'for his right'; i.e., either his refraining from what he is coerced to do is because it is purely his right, like being coerced to destroy his property, even with compensation like its sale. From Radd al-Muhtar. [4] His saying: 'or severe beating'; fatal not with one or two lashes except on the testicles and eye, because harm to life is feared. [0] His saying: 'or imprisonment'; said in Radd al-Muhtar: i.e., imprisonment of himself. Al-Zayla'i said: And the buyer owns it if he takes possession, so his freeing is valid and he is liable for its value. And the buyer owns it if he takes possession, so his freeing is valid and he is liable for its value. Because the coerced sale according to us is a fasid sale; because the rukn of sale issued from its qualified person in its place, and the fasad is due to the loss of the description, which is consent. And an item sold in a fasid sale is owned by possession. So if he takes possession and frees, or performs an act that cannot be undone, it is valid, contrary to Zufar, for it is with him a mawquf sale. And coercion by imprisoning the parents or children is not considered coercion, because it is not overwhelming (mulji') and does not negate consent, unlike imprisonment of himself. But in al-Shurnubalaliyya from al-Mabsut: It is by qiyas, and by istihsan imprisonment of the father is coercion. And al-Turi mentioned that the relied-upon view is that there is no difference between imprisonment of parents and child in the face of istihsan. Al-Quhistani added 'or others of mahram relatives' and attributed it to al-Mabsut. (1) His saying: 'because the rukn of sale' i.e., offer and acceptance issued from its qualified person, the rational adult, in its place i.e., the valuable property. (2) His saying: 'due to the loss of the description' which is the condition from His saying: 'except that it be trade by mutual consent', and the effect of the absence of the condition on the fasad of the contract and nothing else: like the absence of equality in the chapter of riba. (3) Quoting from al-Akmaliyya. So from Hashiyat al-Jalabi [3] His saying: 'that cannot be undone' i.e., cannot be revoked, like tadbir and istilad. [4] His saying: 'is valid' i.e., is permissible, and he is liable for the value like other fasid sales. If it is said: If it were like other fasid sales, it would not become valid by ratification. And it is answered: By the owner's ratification, the corrupting factor (coercion and lack of consent) is removed, so it becomes valid, unlike other fasid sales where the corrupting factor remains.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage from the Book of Coercion (Kitab al-Ikrah) defines the nature of coercion and its effect on contracts. The author first explains that what constitutes coercion varies according to the person's social status and endurance. The ruling focuses on a coerced person who initially refrains from performing the coercive act but eventually complies. The compliance may be for his own right (e.g., selling his property), for the right of another (e.g., destroying another's property), or for the right of the Shari'ah (e.g., drinking wine). The key legal rule is that if a person is coerced by a threat of death, severe beating, or imprisonment into performing a contract (sale, purchase, acknowledgment, lease), that contract is concluded (mun'aqid) but its execution is suspended. The coerced party has the option to annul (faskh) or ratify (imda') after the coercion is removed. This is because such contracts require mutual consent (rida), and coercion that negates consent but is not overwhelming (ghayr mulji') prevents the contract from being binding but does not render it void (batil). The commentator elaborates that a coerced sale is considered fasid (voidable) in the Hanafi school, not batil. The buyer obtains ownership of the goods upon taking possession (qabd), even if the sale is later annulled. For example, if the buyer takes possession of a slave and then frees him, the freeing is valid, and the buyer must pay the value to the seller. This ruling contrasts with Imam Zufar's view, who considers the coerced sale mawquf (suspended), meaning it does not transfer ownership until the coerced party ratifies it. The passage further discusses whether imprisoning a person's parents or children constitutes coercion. The general ruling is that it does not, because such imprisonment is not overwhelming and does not negate consent in the same way as imprisoning the person himself. However, the commentator notes a difference: by strict analogy (qiyas), imprisoning the father is not considered coercion, but by juristic preference (istihsan), it is considered coercion. The relied-upon view in the school is that there is no difference between imprisoning parents, children, or any mahram relative—all are considered coercion by istihsan. The Quranic proof cited is the verse 'except that it be trade by mutual consent' (Surah al-Nisa 4:29), which indicates that consent is a condition for the validity of a contract. Its absence causes the contract to be fasid, not batil, because the rukn (offer and acceptance) is still present. The commentator also clarifies that a fasid sale can become valid if the owner ratifies it later, which distinguishes it from other fasid sales where the defect (mufsid) cannot be removed after the fact.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that compels a person to act against his will, negating consent but not necessarily overwhelming (ghayr mulji').
رضا (rida): Mutual consent, a condition for the validity of contracts; its absence due to coercion renders the contract fasid.
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is concluded (mun'aqid) but defective due to missing a condition; ownership transfers upon possession and the contract can be ratified.
بيع موقوف (bay' mawquf): A suspended sale that does not transfer ownership until ratification by the competent party; Zufar's view.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on a sale conducted under coercion (ikrah ghayr mulji')?
A) The sale is batil and has no effect.
B) The sale is fasid and the buyer does not own until ratification.
C) The sale is fasid and the buyer owns upon possession.
D) The sale is mawquf (suspended) until ratification.

Key Takeaway
Coerced contracts are fasid (voidable), not batil; the buyer owns upon possession, and the coerced party retains the right to annul or ratify.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

The Sale Under Duress in Hanafi Fiqh: Corrupt but Effective by Possession
Explaining the ruling on sale under duress with proof and intra-school differences

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a sale under duress is classified as fasid in the Hanafi school and state the legal effect of possession on subsequent acts like freeing a slave.

Core Idea
A sale under duress is corrupt (fasid), not void; possession transfers ownership, making subsequent irrevocable acts valid.

This lesson delves into the Hanafi ruling on sales conducted under duress. While the contract's pillars exist, the absence of mutual consent renders it corrupt (fasid). However, unlike void contracts, possession transfers ownership, allowing the buyer to validly perform acts like freeing a slave. We'll explore the proof from the Quran and the school's nuanced position on what constitutes coercion, including the difference between external pressure and imprisonment of relatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification of the sale under duress
The passage states it is a corrupt sale (bay' fasid). Note the distinction: fasid means the pillars are present but a condition (consent) is missing; batil means a pillar is missing.
In Hanafi law, fasid is a middle category; ownership can still transfer by possession.
2
Locate the proof from the Quran
The verse 'except that it be trade by mutual consent' (Quran 4:29) is cited. Consent is a condition; its absence corrupts the contract.
The verse is qat'i al-thubut but zanni al-dalala regarding this application.
3
Trace the effect of possession (qabd)
In a fasid sale, ownership transfers by possession, not by contract. Therefore, if the buyer takes possession and then frees a slave (an act that cannot be undone), it is valid and binding.
This is the majority Hanafi view, contrary to Zufr who considers the sale suspended.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi disagreement on what constitutes duress
Imprisoning parents or children is not considered legal duress in qiyas (analogy), but istihsan (equity) treats imprisoning the father as duress. The relied-upon position extends this to all close kin.
This shows how istihsan can override qiyas in Hanafi usul.
5
Understand the effect of subsequent ratification
If the coerced seller later ratifies the sale, it becomes valid because the defect (coercion) is removed. This is unlike other fasid sales where the defect cannot be cured.
The commentary addresses this objection explicitly.

Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling that a buyer under duress who takes possession owns the item, so his freeing of a slave is valid, but he must pay the value. The passage type is an expansion probe, analyzing the nature of a sale under duress in the Hanafi school. It begins by affirming that such a sale is corrupt (fasid) because although the pillars (offer and acceptance) are present, the required description of mutual consent (rida) is absent. The proof is the Quranic verse 'except that it be trade by mutual consent.' In Hanafi fiqh, a corrupt sale transfers ownership by possession (qabd). Therefore, if the buyer under duress takes possession and then performs an act that cannot be reversed—such as freeing a slave or setting up a tadbir (slave to be freed after owner's death) or istilad (granting mother-of-child status)—these acts are valid and binding. This contradicts the view of Zufr, who considers such a sale suspended (mawquf), not corrupt. The commentary then addresses the ruling on what constitutes coercion in this context: imprisoning one's parents or children is generally not considered legal coercion (ikrah) because it is not compelling (mulji') and does not negate consent, unlike imprisoning the individual himself. However, according to al-Mabsut via istihsan, imprisoning the father is deemed coercion, and the relied-upon position is that there is no difference between imprisoning parents and children or other close unmarriageable kin in this ruling, as added by al-Quhustani. Finally, the passage addresses a possible objection: if this corrupt sale were like other corrupt sales, subsequent ratification would not make it valid. The answer is that ratification by the owner removes the defect (coercion and lack of consent), so the sale becomes valid, unlike other corrupt sales where the defect remains. This demonstrates the nuance of Hanafi contract law.

Key Terms
بيع فاسد (bay' fasid): A sale that is valid in its pillars (offer and acceptance) but defective due to a missing condition (like consent); ownership transfers by possession, not by contract alone.
إكراه (ikrah): Compelling someone to act against his will; only when the threat is immediate and severe (mulji') does it negate consent in Hanafi law.
قبض (qabd): Physical possession of the sold item; in a fasid sale, ownership transfers by qabd, not by the contract alone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Ownership does not transfer until the duress is removed.
B) Ownership transfers, and the buyer may validly perform irreversible acts like freeing a slave.
C) The sale becomes batil and possession is irrelevant.
D) The buyer must return the item immediately to the original owner.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a sale under duress is corrupt (fasid), not void; possession transfers ownership and the buyer's subsequent irrevocable acts are valid, but the seller's ratification can cure the defect.

The Four Hanafi Views on Bai' al-Wafa'
Presenting and analyzing different scholarly views on Bai' al-Wafa'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four Hanafi positions on Bai' al-Wafa' and explain the practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf) of each view.

Core Idea
Bai' al-Wafa' is not a simple sale; its ruling depends on whether it is seen as valid, corrupt, pledge, or void.

Bai' al-Wafa' is a widely used transaction in Muslim societies, especially for securing debts, yet its legal nature has been debated by Hanafi scholars. In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, we see four distinct positions within the school, each with its own reasoning and practical consequences. Understanding these differences is crucial for correctly applying the ruling in modern finance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the transaction's form
First, grasp the form of Bai' al-Wafa': a seller transfers an asset to a buyer, often for a debt, with the condition that when the debt is repaid, the asset returns.
2
List the four Hanafi positions
Read the passage carefully and list each position: 1) ja'iz (valid, limited to benefit), 2) fasid (corrupt, like duress), 3) rahn (pledge), 4) batil (void, like jest).
The scholars of Samarkand and the author of Al-Hidayah choose the first.
3
Analyze the reasoning for each position
For each view, note the key principle or analogy: custom (urf) and need for ja'iz; lack of genuine consent for fasid; intention and purpose for rahn; jest (hazl) for batil.
The passage uses 'urf, qiyas, and maqasid as tools.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Work out the practical differences: under ja'iz the buyer can only use the asset; under fasid subsequent sales are voidable; under rahn the buyer is a pledgee; under batil either party can cancel.
These differences affect the validity of later transactions.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The passage states that the author of Al-Hidayah chose the Samarkand view (ja'iz). This is the operative position for fatwa in later Hanafi practice.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن صاحب «الهداية ) أورد [قد] هاهنا مسألة بيع الوفاء بقوله : قال المصنف : ومن جعل البيع الجائز المعتاد بيعاً فاسداً، جعله كبيع المكره حتى ينقض بيع المشتري من غيره إلى آخره. (۲) قال الأكمل ) : أرادَ بالبيع الجائز المعتادِ بيع الوفاء، وصورته : أن يقول البائع للمشتري : بعت منك هذا العين بما لك علي من الدين، أنّي متى قضيتُ الدين فهو لي، أو يقول : بعت منك هذا العين بكذا، على أنّي إذا دفعت إليك ثمنك تدفع العين إلي، قد اختلف الناس فيه، ومشايخ سَمَرْقَند جعلوه جائزاً مفيداً لبعض الأحكام، وهو الانتفاع به دون البيع والهبة على ما هو المعتاد بين الناس للحاجة. واختاره صاحب «الهداية» (۳) ، وأشار إليه بقوله : البيع الجائز المعتاد من المشايخ من جعله بيعاً فاسداً، وجعله كالبيع المكره عليه، حتى ينقض به بيع المشتري من غيره ؛ لأنَّ الفساد لفواتِ الرضاء، كما في البيع المكره عليه. ومنهم من جعله رهناً لقصد المتعاقدين؛ لأنهما وإن سميا بيعاً، لكن غرضهما الرهن، والعبرة للمقاصد والمعاني، فلا يملكه المرتهن، ولا يباح له الانتفاع به إلا بإذن مالكه، وهو ضامن لما أكل من ثمره ، واستهلك من عليه ، والدين ساقط لهلاكه في يده إذا كان وفاء بالدين، ولا ضمانَ عليه في الزيادة إذا هلك بغير صنعه ، وللبائع استرداده إذا قضى دينه ، ولا فرق عندنا بينه وبين الرهن. ومنهم : من جعله بيعاً باطلاً اعتباراً بالهازل ؛ لأنهما تكلّما بلفظ البيع، وليس قصدهما البيع، فكان لكلِّ منهما أن يفسخ بغير رضاء صاحبه، ولو أجاز أحدهما لم يجز على صاحبه. و معنى قوله : هو المعتاد ؛ أنهم في عرفهم لا يفهمون لزوم البيع بهذا الوجه، بل يجوزونه إلى أن يردّ البائع الثمن إلى المشتري، وبقي للمشتري رد المبيع على البائع من
And know that the author of Al-Hidayah introduced here the issue of Bai' al-Wafa' by saying: The author said: Whoever considers the commonly practiced permissible sale as a corrupt sale makes it like a sale under duress, so that the buyer's sale to another is annulled, etc. (2) Al-Akmal said: He meant by the commonly practiced permissible sale: Bai' al-Wafa'. Its form is that the seller says to the buyer: I sold you this asset for the debt you have against me; whenever I pay off the debt, it is mine. Or he says: I sold you this asset for such-and-such, on condition that when I pay you your price, you return the asset to me. People have differed on this. The scholars of Samarkand considered it permissible, giving effect to some rulings, which is the right to benefit (from the asset) but not to sell or gift, as is customary among people due to need. The author of Al-Hidayah chose this and pointed to it by saying: The permissible, commonly practiced sale according to the scholars: some make it a corrupt sale, making it like a sale under duress so that the buyer's sale to another is annulled, because the corruption is due to lack of consent, as in a sale under duress. Among them are those who make it a pledge (rahn) due to the intention of the contracting parties; because although they call it a sale, their purpose is a pledge, and consideration is given to intentions and meanings. So the mortgagee does not own it, nor is it permissible for him to benefit from it except with the owner's permission, and he is liable for what he consumes of its fruit or destroys, and the debt falls upon its destruction in his hand if it was a wafa (repayment) of the debt, and there is no liability on him for the increase if it perishes without his act, and the seller may reclaim it when he pays off his debt. There is no difference in our view between this and a pledge. Among them are those who consider it an absolutely void (batil) sale by analogy with the case of jest; because they spoke the words of sale but did not intend sale, so each of them may rescind without the consent of the other, and if one ratifies it, it does not become binding on the other. And the meaning of his statement 'the commonly practiced' is that in their custom they do not understand the binding nature of a sale in this manner; rather, they allow it until the seller returns the price to the buyer, and the buyer retains the right to return the sold item to the seller.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute analysis of Bai' al-Wafa' (literally 'sale of fulfillment'), a contract used as a security device where a seller transfers an asset to a buyer with the right to repurchase upon repaying the debt. The passage type is a critical refinement, evaluating different scholarly positions within the Hanafi school. It begins with the author of Al-Hidayah referencing a statement: those who consider the commonly practiced permissible sale as corrupt analogize it to sale under duress, so that if the buyer subsequently sells to a third party, that sale is annulled. Al-Akmal explains that this refers to Bai' al-Wafa'. The form is: the seller sells an asset for the debt owed to the buyer, with the condition that when the debt is repaid, the asset returns to the seller; or sells for a price with the condition that when the buyer returns the price, the seller returns the asset. The passage then outlines three opinions among Hanafi scholars: 1) The scholars of Samarkand consider it a valid (ja'iz) contract that gives the buyer the right to benefit (use) but not to sell or gift, based on customary need. The author of Al-Hidayah adopts this view. 2) Some scholars consider it a corrupt (fasid) sale, like sale under duress, because there is no genuine consent to a final sale; thus, if the buyer sells to another, that sale is voidable (the original seller can annul it). The corruption is due to lack of full consent. 3) Some consider it a pledge (rahn) because despite the label of sale, the parties intend a security interest. According to this view, the buyer (now a mortgagee) does not own the asset, cannot benefit without permission, and is liable for consumption; the debt is discharged if the asset perishes in his hand (as it repays the debt), but he is not liable for excess value if it perishes without his fault. The seller may reclaim upon debt repayment. The passage notes that in the Hanafi school, there is no substantive difference between this and a regular pledge. 4) Some consider it an absolutely void (batil) sale by analogy with the case of jest (hazl), because the parties uttered sale words without intending a sale; thus either party may rescind without consent, and ratification by one does not bind the other. The passage concludes by explaining the meaning of 'commonly practiced': customarily, people do not understand this transaction as a binding sale, but rather as a transaction subject to the seller's repurchase. This reflects the Hanafi method of considering 'urf (custom) in contracting.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bai' al-wafa'): A sale where the seller transfers an asset to the buyer (often for a debt) with the condition that when the seller repays the debt, the buyer returns the asset; used as a security device.
هازل (hazil): One who jokes; the analogy of hazl means that if someone speaks the words of a contract without intending it, the contract is void.
معتاد (mu'tad): Customary or commonly practiced; used here to describe Bai' al-Wafa' as a transaction that is widely accepted in commercial practice.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which Hanafi view of Bai' al-Wafa' did the author of Al-Hidayah choose?
A) The view that it is a corrupt sale (fasid).
B) The view that it is a valid sale limited to benefit (ja'iz).
C) The view that it is a pledge (rahn).
D) The view that it is absolutely void (batil).

Key Takeaway
Bai' al-Wafa' is a valid but limited contract in the mufta bihi Hanafi view, where the buyer only gains the right to benefit, not full ownership.

Definition and Three Views on Bay' al-Wafa'
Defines Bay' al-Wafa' and presents three scholarly positions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define Bay' al-Wafa', identify its customary form, and state the three debated Hanafi views on its validity.

Core Idea
Bay' al-Wafa' is debated: is it a valid customary sale, a corrupt sale, a mortgage, or void? The preferred view: permissible customary sale.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we examine a unique transaction called Bay' al-Wafa'—a sale with a repurchase condition. Hanafi scholars disagreed on its legal nature. We will unpack the definition, the Samarkand scholars' view accepted by Al-Marghinani, and three competing positions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the transaction
Recognize the structure: seller transfers ownership of an asset in exchange for a debt or price, with the condition that when the seller repays, the asset returns.
This is the basic form of Bay' al-Wafa'.
2
Identify the preferred view
Note that the Samarkand scholars considered it a permissible customary sale with limited effects (only usufruct, not further sale/gift), and Al-Marghinani chose this view.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Examine the first opposing view: corrupt sale (fasid)
Those who see it as fasid liken it to a coerced sale due to defective consent; the buyer's sale to a third party is subject to rescission.
Analogy with bay' al-mukrah.
4
Examine the second opposing view: mortgage (rahn)
Those who see it as rahn emphasize the parties' true intention of security; the buyer does not own the item and is a guarantor.
Consideration of intent over literal words.
5
Examine the third opposing view: absolutely void (batil)
Those who see it as batil analogize it to a jester's contract (hazl) since the words of sale do not match intent; each party may rescind.
Because the transaction lacks real intention to sell.

واعلم أن صاحب «الهداية» أورد [قد] هاهنا مسألة بيع الوفاء بقوله: قال المصنف: ومن جعل البيع الجائز المعتاد بيعاً فاسداً، جعله كبيع المكره حتى ينقض بيع المشتري من غيره إلى آخره. قال الأكمل: أراد بالبيع الجائز المعتاد بيع الوفاء، وصورته: أن يقول البائع للمشتري: بعت منك هذا العين بما لك علي من الدين، أني متى قضيت الدين فهو لي، أو يقول: بعت منك هذا العين بكذا، على أني إذا دفعت إليك ثمنك تدفع العين إلي. قد اختلف الناس فيه، ومشايخ سمرقند جعلوه جائزاً مفيداً لبعض الأحكام، وهو الانتفاع به دون البيع والهبة على ما هو المعتاد بين الناس للحاجة. واختاره صاحب «الهداية»، وأشار إليه بقوله: البيع الجائز المعتاد من المشايخ من جعله بيعاً فاسداً، وجعله كالبيع المكره عليه، حتى ينقض به بيع المشتري من غيره؛ لأن الفساد لفوات الرضاء، كما في البيع المكره عليه. ومنهم من جعله رهناً لقصد المتعاقدين؛ لأنهما وإن سميا بيعاً، لكن غرضهما الرهن، والعبرة للمقاصد والمعاني، فلا يملكه المرتهن، ولا يباح له الانتفاع به إلا بإذن مالكه، وهو ضامن لما أكل من ثمره، واستهلك من عليه، والدين ساقط لهلاكه في يده إذا كان وفاء بالدين، ولا ضمان عليه في الزيادة إذا هلك بغير صنعه، وللبائع استرداده إذا قضى دينه، ولا فرق عندنا بينه وبين الرهن. ومنهم من جعله بيعاً باطلاً اعتباراً بالهازل؛ لأنهما تكلما بلفظ البيع، وليس قصدهما البيع، فكان لكل منهما أن يفسخ بغير رضاء صاحبه، ولو أجاز أحدهما لم يجز على صاحبه. ومعنى قوله: هو المعتاد؛ أنهم في عرفهم لا يفهمون لزوم البيع بهذا الوجه، بل يجوزونه إلى أن يرد البائع الثمن إلى المشتري، وبقي للمشتري رد المبيع على البائع من
Know that the author of Al-Hidayah presented here the issue of Bay' al-Wafa' saying: The author said: And whoever considers the permissible, customary sale a corrupt sale, he makes it like the sale of a coerced person, such that the buyer's sale to another is invalidated, etc. Al-Akmal said: He meant by the permissible, customary sale Bay' al-Wafa'. Its form is that the seller says to the buyer: I sold you this item for the debt you owe me, whenever I repay the debt it becomes mine, or he says: I sold you this item for such and such, on condition that when I pay you the price you return the item to me. People have differed regarding it. The scholars of Samarkand considered it permissible, beneficial for some rulings, namely benefiting from it without selling or gifting, according to what is customary among people out of need. And the author of Al-Hidayah chose it, and indicated by his saying: The permissible, customary sale from the scholars who consider it a corrupt sale, and make it like a coerced sale, such that the buyer's sale to another is invalidated; because the corruption is due to lack of consent, as in a coerced sale. And among them are those who consider it a mortgage (rahn) due to the intention of the contracting parties; because even though they call it a sale, their purpose is mortgage, and consideration is given to intentions and meanings. So the mortgagee does not own it, nor is it permissible for him to benefit from it except with the owner's permission, and he is guarantor for what he consumes of its fruit and for what he wastes, and the debt is canceled upon its destruction in his hand if it is a repayment of the debt, and there is no guarantee on him for the excess if it is destroyed without his act, and the seller may reclaim it when he pays his debt, and there is no difference in our view between it and a mortgage. And among them are those who consider it an absolutely void sale, by analogy with the jester; because they spoke with the words of sale while their intention was not sale, so each of them may rescind without the other's consent, and if one of them ratifies, it does not bind the other. And the meaning of his saying 'customary' is that in their custom they do not understand the binding of the sale in this manner, rather they permit it until the seller returns the price to the buyer, and the buyer retains the right to return the item to the seller.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal classification of Bay' al-Wafa' (a sale with the right of redemption). It begins by defining the transaction: the seller transfers ownership of an asset to the buyer in lieu of a debt or for a price, with the condition that if the seller repays the amount, the asset is returned. The scholars of Samarkand considered it a valid transaction with limited effects (only usufruct, not further sale or gift). The author of Al-Hidayah (Al-Marghinani) preferred this view but notes that some scholars deem it a corrupt (fasid) sale because consent is defective, like a coerced sale. Others consider it a mortgage (rahn) because the real intent is security, thus the buyer does not own the item and cannot use it without permission, and the debt is discharged if the item is destroyed. Yet others hold it absolutely void (batil) by analogy with a jester's contract (hazl), since the parties utter sale but do not intend it, so each has a right to rescind. The passage concludes by clarifying that 'customary' means people do not consider the sale binding until the price is returned. The commentary flows from definition to opinion enumeration to explanation of the term 'mu'tad'.

Key Terms
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa'): A sale with a condition that the seller can repurchase the item upon returning the price or debt.
البيع الجائز المعتاد (al-bay' al-ja'iz al-mu'tad): The sale that is considered permissible and customary according to the scholars of Samarkand, specifically Bay' al-Wafa'.
رهن (rahn): A mortgage or pledge, where the item is held as security for a debt, not transferred in ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in Al-Hidayah, what is the ruling on Bay' al-Wafa'?
A) It is a corrupt (fasid) sale.
B) It is a customary permissible sale (ja'iz mu'tad).
C) It is a mortgage (rahn).
D) It is an absolutely void (batil) sale.

Key Takeaway
Bay' al-Wafa' is a valid customary transaction in the Hanafi school, but its precise classification is subject to legitimate ikhtilaf; the relied-upon position is that it is a permissible customary sale.

Coerced Delivery in Bay' al-Wafa': Does It Effect Ownership?
Examines the ruling on coerced delivery and rebuts omission in Al-Hidayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on coerced delivery in Bay' al-Wafa' and explain why it is considered a corrupt sale according to some scholars.

Core Idea
Coerced delivery in Bay' al-Wafa' does not transfer ownership; the item must be returned.

This lesson examines a nuanced issue in Hanafi sales law: when a seller is forced to deliver the sold item under duress, does ownership transfer? The passage from Hashiyat al-Jalabi clarifies that if the coercion is on the delivery itself (not just the contract), the delivery is ineffective and the item must be returned. It also presents Al-Bayan's view that the entire Bay' al-Wafa' arrangement is a corrupt sale due to the prohibited condition, yet still effects ownership after possession. We will walk through the ruling, the dispute, and the evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the delivery of the sold item under coercion. The ruling is that it does not effect ownership (la yufid al-milk). If the seller receives the price under coercion or delivers under coercion, he must return the item if it remains.
This is the base ruling from the text.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the hadith: the Prophet (ص) prohibited a sale with a condition ('بيع وشرط'). This indicates that any sale containing a condition not inherent to the contract is invalid (fasid).
The hadith is cited by Al-Bayan to support the corrupt nature of Bay' al-Wafa'.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The prohibition (al-nahy) implies the sale is invalid (fasid) because it includes a condition (the promise to redeem) that is not a requirement of the contract. The condition makes it resemble a sale with a right of option, which suspends ownership until the condition is fulfilled.
The text analogizes Bay' al-Wafa' to a sale with khiyar (option).
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Al-Hidayah states that if one sells and delivers under coercion, ownership is established. Al-Bayan argues it is a corrupt sale that nevertheless effects ownership after possession. The passage rebuts the claim that Al-Hidayah omitted the ruling by pointing out its earlier statement.
The critic says Al-Hidayah omitted the ruling, but the rebuttal shows it was mentioned.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The author of Al-Bayan states that the most correct view (al-asahh 'indi) is that it is a corrupt sale. This is considered the mufta bihi position by later scholars, as reflected in the commentary.
Authority weight: fatawa mutakhkhirin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قبض ثمنه، أو سلم طوعاً نفذ، وإن قبضه مكرها لا، ورده إن بقي. لم يذكر في «الهداية» حكم التسليم مكرها، لكن ذكر في أصول الفقه: أن الإكراه إذا كان على البيع والتسليم يكون التسليم مقتصراً على الفاعل، ولم يجعل غير امتناع، ولا يكون ذلك إلا إذا لم يخرج عن ملكه ببيع أو هبة؛ ولهذا سموه ببيع الوفاء؛ لأنه وفاء بما عهد من رد المبيع. هذا زبدة ما في «العناية». وقال صاحب «البيان»: والأصح عندي أنه بيع فاسد يوجب الملك بعد القبض وحكمه حكم سائر البيوع الفاسدة؛ لأنه بيع بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وقد نهى النبي عن بيع وشرط. انتهى كلامه. كذا في حاشية الجلبي. قوله: لا يفيد الملك؛ كالبيع بشرط الخيار. قلنا: البيع بالخيار إنما لا يفيده؛ لأنه جعل العقد في حق حكمه كالمعلق بشرط، والمعلق بالشرط معدوم قبل وجود الشرط. كذا في حاشية الجلبي ناقلاً عن «العناية». قوله: أو سلم؛ أي المبيع طوعاً، وهو قيد للقبض والتسليم معاً، والتسليم طوعاً إنما يتصور إذا أكره على البيع، لا على الدفع. قوله: لم يذكر في «الهداية»؛ يعني كما لم يذكر في «الوقاية» أيضاً، قال أخي: يرد على ظاهره أن صاحب الهداية قال قبيل هذا: ثم إذا باع مكرها وسلّم مكرهاً يثبت به الملك، وهل هذا إلا ذكر حكم التسليم مكرهاً، فليتأمل. انتهى. قوله: لكن ذكر في أصول الفقه؛ في «التوضيح»: والأفعال: منها ما لا يحتمل كون الفاعل آلة للحامل، ومنها ما يحتمله:
If he receives its price, or delivers voluntarily, it is effective. But if he receives it under coercion, it is not, and he returns it if it remains. Al-Hidayah did not mention the ruling on coerced delivery, but it was mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh: that if coercion is upon the sale and the delivery, the delivery is attributed solely to the doer, and is not considered anything but [an act of] non-prohibition, and that only happens if it does not leave his ownership by sale or gift; for this reason they called it Bay' al-Wafa'; because it is a fulfillment of what was pledged regarding the return of the sold item. This is the summary of what is in Al-‘Inayah. And the author of Al-Bayan said: The most correct in my view is that it is a corrupt sale that effects ownership after possession, and its ruling is the ruling of all other corrupt sales; because it is a sale with a condition that is not required by the contract, and the Prophet prohibited a sale with a condition. End of his words. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. His statement: 'It does not effect ownership' like the sale with a condition of option. We say: The sale with an option does not effect ownership because it makes the contract, with respect to its ruling, like one suspended on a condition, and what is suspended on a condition is non-existent before the condition exists. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from Al-‘Inayah. His statement: 'or delivered' i.e. the sold item voluntarily, and this is a qualifier for both receipt and delivery together, and voluntary delivery is only conceivable if he was coerced into the sale, not into the handing over. His statement: 'It was not mentioned in Al-Hidayah' meaning just as it was not mentioned in Al-Wiqayah also. My brother said: It appears objectionable that the author of Al-Hidayah said just before this: 'Then if he sold under coercion and delivered under coercion, ownership is established by it.' Is this not a mention of the ruling on coerced delivery? So let it be considered. End. His statement: 'But it was mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh' in Al-Tawdih: And actions: among them are those that do not admit of the doer being a mere instrument for the coercer, and among them are those that admit it.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of Al-Hidayah's ruling on coerced delivery in Bay' al-Wafa'. It addresses three sub-points: (1) If the seller receives the price voluntarily, or delivers the sold item voluntarily (even if coerced into the sale contract), the sale is effective. (2) If the seller receives the price under coercion, or is forced to deliver, it is not effective, and he must return the item. (3) The commentator notes that Al-Hidayah allegedly omitted the ruling on coerced delivery, but a rebuttal points out that Al-Hidayah earlier stated that if one sells and delivers under coercion, ownership is established. The text then cites Usul al-Fiqh principles distinguishing actions that can or cannot be attributed to the coercer. The author of Al-Bayan argues that Bay' al-Wafa' is a corrupt sale because it includes a condition not inherent to the contract, citing the hadith prohibiting sale with a condition. The commentary also addresses a parallel to a sale with a condition of option (khiyar), explaining that such a sale does not effect ownership until the condition is fulfilled. The overall lesson clarifies the nuanced ruling on coerced delivery and supports it with a hadith and usul principles.

Key Terms
الإكراه على التسليم (al-ikrah 'ala al-taslim): Coercion to physically deliver the sold item, as opposed to coercion to enter into the sale contract.
بيع بشرط (bay' bi-shart): A sale conditional on a stipulation not normally part of the contract, like the right of redemption.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is effective and ownership transfers.
B) The delivery is invalid and the item must be returned.
C) The sale becomes a corrupt (fasid) sale.
D) The seller must compensate the buyer.

Key Takeaway
Coerced delivery in Bay' al-Wafa' does not transfer ownership; the item must be returned, and the sale is considered corrupt by later Hanafi authorities.
