Ruling on Coerced Sale and Delivery
Explains the effect of coercion on sale and delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a coerced sale and delivery, and explain the rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī) that supports it, distinguishing the Hanafi view from the opposing view.

Core Idea
A coerced sale is valid but ownership is suspended until voluntary receipt or delivery.

In this passage, the commentator expands on a nuanced ruling from the matn of al-Wiqayah regarding the effect of coercion on a sale. The core issue is whether a seller who is forced to sell and then forced to deliver the item actually transfers ownership. The Hanafi school distinguishes between coercion on the contract and coercion on the delivery, a distinction rooted in the nature of human action under duress. We will explore the ruling, the rational evidence from usul al-fiqh, and the objection raised by the author of al-Bayan.

Guided Path
1
State the ruling from the matn
The matn states: if the coerced seller receives the price or delivers voluntarily, it is effective; if he receives under coercion, it is not. Returning the item before ratification does not confer ownership.
Focus on the two triggers for effectiveness: voluntary receipt of price or voluntary delivery.
2
Identify the unresolved issue
Al-Hidayah did not explicitly rule on delivery under coercion; the commentator resolves this using Usul al-Fiqh.
This shows how usul principles are applied to derive rulings.
3
Understand the usul principle
Actions under coercion are attributed to the actor unless the actor can be seen as a tool of the coercer. For delivery, the actor is the primary agent, so the sale is effective (though ownership is suspended).
This is the core rational proof (dalīl 'aqlī).
4
Analyze the counter-argument
Al-Bayan argues the sale is fāsid because it contains a condition (the promise to return) that is not inherent, citing the hadith prohibiting conditional sales.
Identify the hadith and the reasoning.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi defense
The commentator defends by analogy to a sale with option (khiyar al-shart), where ownership is also suspended. This preserves the validity of the contract while acknowledging the condition.
This analogy shows how qiyas works in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حسن الدراية لأواخر شرح الوقاية
فإن قبض ثمنه ، أو سَلَّمَ طوعاً نَفَذ ، وإن قبضه مُكْرَها لا ، وردَّهُ إن بقي قبل الإجازة لا يفيد الملك.

(فإن قبض ثمنه ، أو سَلَّمَ طوعاً نَفَذ، وإن قبضه مُكْرَها لا ، وردَّهُ إن بقي)، لم يذكر في «الهداية» حُكْمَ التسليم مُكْرَها ، لكن ذُكِرَ في أصول الفقه : أن الإكراه إذا كان على البيع والتسليم يكون التسليم مقتصراً على الفاعل، ولم يجعل غير امتناع، ولا يكون ذلك إلا إذا لم يخرج عن ملكه ببيع أو هبة ؛ ولهذا سموه بـ بيع الوفاء ؛ لأنه وفاء بما عهد من ردّ المبيع، هذا زيدة ما في «العناية).

وقال صاحب «البيان»: والأصح عندي أنه بيع فاسد يوجب الملك بعد القبض وحكمه حكم سائر البيوع الفاسدة ؛ لأنه بيع بشرط لا يقتضيه العقد، وقد نهى النبي عن بيع وشرط.

قوله : لا يفيد الملك ؛ كالبيع بشرط الخيار ، قلنا : البيع بالخيار إنما لا يفيده ؛ لأنه جعل العقد في حق حكمه كالمعلق بشرط، والمعلق بالشرط معدوم قبل وجود الشرط.

قوله : أو سلّم ; أي المبيع طوعاً، وهو قيد للقبض والتسليم معاً، والتسليم طوعاً إنما يتصوّر إذا أكره على البيع، لا على الدفع.

قوله : لم يذكر في «الهداية» ; يعني كما لم يذكر في «الوقاية» أيضاً، قال أخي : يرد على ظاهره أنّ صاحب الهداية قال قبيل هذا : ثم إذا باع مكرها وسلّم مكرهاً يثبت به الملك ، وهل هذا إلا ذكر حكم التسليم مكرهاً، فليتأمل.

قوله : لكن ذكر في أصول الفقه ; في «التوضيح ) : والأفعال : منها : ما لا يحتمل كون الفاعل آلة للحامل، ومنها : ما يحتمله :
الفاعل آلة للحامل في التسليم ؛ لأنه حمله على تسليم المبيع، ولو جُعِلَ آلة له يصير تسليم المغصوب ، فإذا كان التسليم مقتصراً على الفاعل، ينبغي أن ينفذ، ويجب القيمة.
في الأول : يقتصر على الفاعل، مثل الأكل والشرب حتى لا يرجع إلى الحامل شيء من أحكامها من حيث أنهما أكل وشرب، كما إذا أكره صائم صائما على الإفطار، فإنّه يبطل صوم الفاعل لا الحامل.
وأما ما يتعلق بذلك من حيث أنه إتلاف، كما إذا أكرهه على أكل مال الغير فقد اختلفت المرويات في أنّ الضمان على الفاعل، أو على الحامل، وكذا في الزنا لو أكرهه عليه ، كان العقرُ على الزاني، لكن لو تلفت الجارية ينبغي بذلك أن يكون الضمان على الحامل : أي على المكره. والثاني : وهو ما يحتمل كون الفاعل آلة للحامل قسمان؛ لأنه : إما أن يلزم من جعله آلة للمكره – بالكسر – تبديل محل الجناية أو لا. أما القسم الأوّلُ : فيقتصر على الفاعل ولا يتعلق بالحامل، إذ لو نسب إلى الحامل، وجعلَ الفاعل بمنزلة الآلة عاد على موضعه بالنقض ; لأنّ تبديل محلّ الجناية يستلزم مخالفة الحامل ; لأنه إنما حمله بالإكراه على الجناية في ذلك المحل، ومخالفة الحامل يستلزم بطلان الإكراه ; لأنه عبارة عن حمل الغير على ما يريده الحامل ويرضاه، على خلاف رضاء الفاعل ، وهو فعل معين في محل معين، فإذا فعل غيره كان طائعاً بالضرورة لا مكرهاً. والثاني : ما يكون تبديل محل الجناية مستلزماً لتبديل ذات الفعل، كما إذا أكرهه الغير على بيع الشيء وتسليمه، فيقتصر التسليم على الفاعل، إذ لو نسب إلى الحامل وجعل الفاعل آلة لزم التبديل في محل التسليم بأن يصير مغصوباً ; لأنَّ التسليم من جهة الحامل يكون تصرفاً في ملك الغير على سبيل الاستيلاء، فيصير البيع والتسليم غصباً.
وأما إذا نسب التسليم إلى الفاعل وجعل تتميماً للعقد ، حتى أن المشتري يملك المبيع ملكاً فاسداً لانعقاد البيع وعدم نفاذه ، فلا يلزم ذلك.
Husn al-Dirayah for the last parts of Sharh al-Wiqayah: If he receives its price, or delivers voluntarily, it is effective. If he receives it under coercion, it is not. And he returns it if it remains before ratification; it does not confer ownership. (Then if he receives its price or delivers voluntarily it is effective, and if he receives it under coercion it is not, and he returns it if it remains) – Al-Hidayah did not mention the ruling of delivery under coercion, but it was mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh: that when coercion is on sale and delivery, the delivery is limited to the actor, and is not considered as non-prevention, and that is only if he does not remove it from his ownership by sale or gift; therefore they called it bay' al-wafa (sale of fulfillment) because it fulfills the promise of returning the sold item; this is the summary from Al-Inayah. And the author of Al-Bayan said: The soundest in my view is that it is a corrupt sale (fāsid) that confers ownership after receipt, and its ruling is the same as all other corrupt sales, because it is a sale with a condition that the contract does not require, and the Prophet prohibited a sale with a condition. His statement: 'it does not confer ownership' – like a sale with option of stipulation; we say: the sale with option does not confer ownership because the contract is made, with respect to its ruling, like suspended upon a condition, and what is suspended upon a condition is non-existent before the condition exists. His statement: 'or delivers' – i.e., the sold item voluntarily, and it is a qualification for both receipt and delivery. Delivery voluntarily is only conceivable if he was coerced on the sale, not on the handing over. His statement: 'Al-Hidayah did not mention' – meaning just as Al-Wiqayah did not mention it either; my brother said: An objection arises on its apparent meaning: the author of Al-Hidayah said shortly before this: 'Then if he sells under coercion and delivers under coercion, ownership is established.' Is this not a mention of the ruling of delivery under coercion? So let it be considered. His statement: 'but it was mentioned in Usul al-Fiqh' – in Al-Tawdhih: Actions are of two types: those that cannot be considered that the actor is a tool for the inciter, and those that can be so considered. The actor is a tool for the inciter in delivery, because he carries him to deliver the sold item; if he is made a tool, it becomes delivery of a usurped item. So if the delivery is limited to the actor, it should be effective, and the value is obligatory. In the first type: it is limited to the actor, like eating and drinking, so that nothing of their rulings returns to the inciter in that they are eating and drinking, just as if a fasting person coerces another to break his fast: it invalidates the fast of the actor, not the inciter. As for what relates to that as being destruction, such as if he coerces him to eat the property of another, the reports differ on whether the guarantee is on the actor or the inciter. Similarly in fornication: if he coerces him to do it, the dower is on the fornicator, but if the slave-girl perishes, then the guarantee should be on the inciter, i.e., the coercer. The second type – which can consider the actor as a tool for the inciter – is of two kinds: either making him a tool for the inciter necessitates changing the locus of the offense or not. As for the first kind: it is limited to the actor and does not relate to the inciter, because if it were attributed to the inciter and the actor were made the tool, it would revert to its place with nullification, because changing the locus of the offense necessitates violating the inciter's intent; since he only coerced him to commit the offense in that specific locus, and violating the inciter's intent nullifies the coercion because it is the act of carrying someone to do what the inciter wants and is pleased with, contrary to the actor's satisfaction. It is a specific action in a specific locus; if he does something else, he is necessarily obedient, not coerced. The second kind: where changing the locus of the offense necessitates changing the action itself, such as if one coerces another to sell a thing and deliver it; then delivery is limited to the actor, because if it were attributed to the inciter and the actor made a tool, a change in the locus of delivery would become necessary, such that it becomes usurped. This is because delivery from the inciter would be a disposition in the property of another by way of seizure, making the sale and delivery usurpation. But if delivery is attributed to the actor and is made a completion of the contract, such that the buyer owns the sold item with a corrupt ownership due to the contract being valid but not effective, then that does not occur.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an expansion and commentary on a ruling from the matn (foundation text) regarding a coerced sale. The ruling states: If a person coerced into a sale receives the price or delivers the sold item voluntarily, the transaction is effective (nafadh). However, if he receives it under coercion, it is not effective. Moreover, if he returns the item before ratification (ijazah), it does not confer ownership upon the buyer. The commentator then notes that Al-Hidayah did not explicitly mention the ruling of delivery under coercion, but Usul al-Fiqh clarifies it: when coercion is applied to both the sale and the delivery, the delivery is attributed solely to the coerced person (fā'il) and is not considered a non-prevention (i.e., not attributed to the coercer). This is similar to bay' al-wafa (sale of fulfillment), where the seller retains ownership. The author of Al-Bayan disagrees, arguing that such a sale is fasad (corrupt) and becomes valid upon receipt, citing the hadith prohibiting a sale with a condition. The commentator defends the original ruling by analogy to a sale with an option (khiyar al-shart), which also does not transfer ownership immediately. The passage then delves into a detailed discussion from Al-Tawdhih on the nature of actions under coercion: some actions (like eating, drinking, or destroying property) are attributed solely to the actor, while others (like delivery in a sale) can be seen as the actor being a tool of the coercer. The commentator concludes that in the case of a coerced sale, delivery is attributed to the actor (so it is effective as a completion of the contract, giving the buyer a corrupt ownership) and not to the coercer (which would make it usurpation). This preserves the validity of the contract in a limited sense. The lesson type is an expansion probe (sharh), based on the matn of al-Wiqayah, with references to al-Hidayah and other works.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion; forcing someone to do an act against their will, specifically in the context of a sale.
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery; handing over the sold item from seller to buyer.
بيع بشرط (bay' bi-shart): Sale with a condition that is not inherent in the contract, considered corrupt (fāsid) by some.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling if a person coerced into a sale delivers the item voluntarily?
A) The delivery is ineffective and ownership does not transfer.
B) The delivery is effective and the buyer owns the item.
C) The sale is void and the item must be returned.
D) The seller retains ownership until the price is paid.

Key Takeaway
A coerced sale is valid but ownership is suspended until voluntary receipt or delivery; the attribution of actions under coercion determines the effectiveness.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Classification of the Coerced Person as Instrument in Coercion
Defines and refines the instrument analogy in coercion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between acts where the coerced person is treated as an instrument (ālah) and those where he is not, and apply the Hanafi ruling on guarantee (ḍamān) and contract validity accordingly.

Core Idea
A coerced person may be a mere instrument; attribution and liability depend on whether instrument status changes the locus or essence of the act.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi legal classification of the coerced person (al-fāʿil) in coercion cases. The central question is: when should the coerced be treated as a tool (ālah) of the coercer, and when is he the true legal agent? The answer determines who bears liability for the act and whether a coerced contract is effective or defective. We will follow the refined analysis from the Sharh al-Wiqayah tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary categories
Acts where the coerced is NOT an instrument (e.g., eating/drinking) vs. acts where he CAN be considered an instrument. For non-instrument acts, rulings apply only to the coerced (e.g., his fast breaks).
The text uses the example of a fasting person compelling another to break fast.
2
Subdivide the instrument category
Under the second category, two cases: (a) treating the coerced as an instrument would change the locus of the offense, so the act stays on the coerced; (b) treating him as an instrument would change the essence of the act, so the act is attributed to the coerced but with different legal consequences.
Example for (a): coercing someone to commit an offense in a specific place, but he does it elsewhere – then he acts willingly. Example for (b): coerced sale – delivery attributed to coerced seller completes the contract.
3
Apply to coerced sale
In coerced sale, the delivery is attributed to the coerced seller, making the contract concluded but not effective (fāsid). The buyer gets defective ownership, and the seller can demand the price or return.
The coercer is not treated as the deliverer, otherwise the sale would be usurpation.
4
Analyze destruction and guarantee
For acts of destruction (e.g., coerced eating of another's property) and coerced adultery, the transmitted reports differ on whether guarantee (ḍamān) lies on the coerced or the coercer. The text suggests that in perishing of the subject, the coercer may be liable.
The dower in coerced adultery is on the adulterer, but if the slave-girl perishes, the coercer guarantees.
5
Examine the buyer's guarantee when sold item perishes
If only the seller is coerced, the buyer (voluntary) must guarantee the value of the item that perishes in his hand. The buyer can choose to hold either the coercer or the seller (as original owner) liable.
The buyer's ownership from the defective contract makes him liable; recourse depends on whom he sues.

الفاعل آلة للحامل في التسليم ؛ لأنه حمله على تسليم المبيع، ولو جُعِلَ آلة له يصير تسليم المغصوب ، فإذا كان التسليم مقتصراً على الفاعل، ينبغي أن ينفذ، ويجب القيمة.
في الأول : يقتصر على الفاعل، مثل الأكل والشرب حتى لا يرجع إلى الحامل شيء من أحكامها من حيث أنهما أكل وشرب، كما إذا أكره صائم صائما على الإفطار، فإنّه يبطل صوم الفاعل لا الحامل.
وأما ما يتعلق بذلك من حيث أنه إتلاف، كما إذا أكرهه على أكل مال الغير فقد اختلفت المرويات في أنّ الضمان على الفاعل، أو على الحامل، وكذا في الزنا لو أكرهه عليه ، كان العقرُ على الزاني، لكن لو تلفت الجارية ينبغي بذلك أن يكون الضمان على الحامل : أي على المكره. والثاني : وهو ما يحتمل كون الفاعل آلة للحامل قسمان؛ لأنه : إما أن يلزم من جعله آلة للمكره - بالكسر – تبديل محل الجناية أو لا. أما القسم الأوّلُ : فيقتصر على الفاعل ولا يتعلق بالحامل، إذ لو نسب إلى الحامل، وجعلَ الفاعل بمنزلة الآلة عاد على موضعه بالنقض ؛ لأنّ تبديل محلّ الجناية يستلزم مخالفة الحامل ؛ لأنه إنما حمله بالإكراه على الجناية في ذلك المحل، ومخالفة الحامل يستلزم بطلان الإكراه ؛ لأنه عبارة عن حمل الغير على ما يريده الحامل ويرضاه، على خلاف رضاء الفاعل ، وهو فعل معين في محل معين، فإذا فعل غيره كان طائعاً بالضرورة لا مكرهاً. والثاني : ما يكون تبديل محل الجناية مستلزماً لتبديل ذات الفعل، كما إذا أكرهه الغير على بيع الشيء وتسليمه، فيقتصر التسليم على الفاعل، إذ لو نسب إلى الحامل وجعـل الـفـاعـل الـة لـزم التبديل في محل التسليم بأن يصير مغصوباً ؛ لأنَّ التسليم من جهة الحامل يكون تصرفاً في ملك الغير على سبيل الاستيلاء، فيصير البيع والتسليم غصباً.
وأما إذا نسب التسليم إلى الفاعل وجعل تتميماً للعقد ، حتى أن المشتري يملك المبيع ملكاً فاسداً لانعقاد البيع وعدم نفاذه ، فلا يلزم ذلك. انتهى

(فلو أُكْرِهَ البائع لا المشتري ، وهَلَكَ المبيع في يده) : أي في يد المشتري ، (ضمن قيمته للبائع ، وله أن يضمن أيا شاء" ، فإن ضمن المكره رجع على [١]قوله : فإن قلت : أول : حاصلُ السؤالِ إنّ المفهوم من أصول الفقه : إنّ الفعل الذي [لا] يجعل المكره فيه آلةً للمكره يقتصر على المباشر فينفذ، ويجب القيمة وقبض الثمن من هذا القبيل، مع أنّ النفاذ ووجوب القيمة منتفيان فيه. وحاصل الجواب : إنهما مختصان بما يستلزم مما جعل الفاعل آلة تبديل محل الجناية مثلاً، والقبض ليس كذلك كما لا يخفى. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٢] قوله : ضمن قيمته ؛ لأنه مضمون عليه بحكم عقد فاسد، وهو البيع بالإكراه، وما هو كذلك فهو مضمون بالقيمة، فضمن المشتري قيمته للبائع وهو كالبيع .

«الكفاية»(۳).
(۱) «ذخيرة العقبی»(ص٥٣٨). (۲) ينظر: «ذخيرة العقبی» (ص ٥٣٨
(۳) (الكفاية» (۸: ۱۷۱ - ۱۷۲).
Book of Coercion / its definition, conditions, and rulings. The actor is an instrument for the one who causes in delivery; because he compelled him to deliver the sold item, and if he is made an instrument for him, the delivery of the usurped property results. So if the delivery is restricted to the actor, it should be effective, and the value is obligatory. In the first case: it is restricted to the actor, like eating and drinking, so that nothing of its rulings returns to the one who causes in terms that they are eating and drinking, just as if a fasting person compels another fasting person to break the fast, then the fast of the actor is invalidated, not the one who causes. As for what pertains to that in terms of destruction, like if he compels him to eat the property of another, the transmitted narrations differed on whether the guarantee is on the actor or on the one who causes. Similarly, in adultery if he compels him to it, the dower is on the adulterer, but if the slave-girl perishes, the guarantee should accordingly be on the one who causes: i.e., the coercer. The second: which is what admits the possibility of the actor being an instrument for the one who causes, is of two types; because: either it necessitates from making him an instrument for the coercer (with kasra) a change in the locus of the offense or not. As for the first type: it is restricted to the actor and does not attach to the one who causes, for if it were attributed to the one who causes, and the actor were made like an instrument, it would contradict its place because changing the locus of the offense entails opposing the one who causes; because he only compelled him by coercion to the offense in that locus, and opposing the one who causes entails the nullification of the coercion; because it is an expression of compelling another to what the one who causes wants and is pleased with, contrary to the pleasure of the actor, and it is a specific act in a specific place, so if he does something else, he is necessarily willing, not coerced. The second: what makes changing the locus of the offense entail changing the essence of the act, like if someone else compels him to sell a thing and deliver it, then the delivery is restricted to the actor, for if it were attributed to the one who causes and the actor were made an instrument, it would necessitate a change in the locus of delivery such that it becomes usurped; because the delivery from the perspective of the one who causes is a disposition in the property of another by way of seizure, so the sale and delivery become usurpation. As for if delivery is attributed to the actor and made a completion of the contract, such that the buyer owns the sold item with defective ownership due to the contract being concluded but not effective, then that does not necessitate. End. (1) See: Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 538). Husn al-Dirayah li-Awakhir Sharh al-Wiqayah. So if the seller is coerced, not the buyer, and the sold item perishes in his hand, he guarantees its value to the seller, and he may guarantee whichever he wishes. If he makes the coercer liable, he (the coercer) has recourse to... If you say: it is problematic regarding the receipt of the price, for the actor cannot be an instrument in it, and yet it is not effective in it. I say: it does not necessitate here from making him an instrument a change in the act that he was coerced upon, unlike delivery. [2] (If the seller is coerced, not the buyer, and the sold item perishes in his hand): i.e., in the hand of the buyer, (he guarantees its value to the seller, and he may guarantee whichever he wishes), if he makes the coercer liable, he has recourse to... [1] His saying: If you say: first: the gist of the question is that the understanding from the principles of fiqh is that the act in which the coerced person is not made an instrument for the coercer is restricted to the direct doer, so it is effective, and the value is obligatory, and the receipt of the price is of this category, yet effectiveness and obligation of value are negated in it. The gist of the answer: they are specific to what necessitates, of making the actor an instrument, a change in the locus of the offense for example, and receipt is not like that, as is not hidden. Thus in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: he guarantees its value; because it is guaranteed by virtue of a defective contract, which is the sale under coercion, and what is like that is guaranteed by value, so the buyer guarantees its value to the seller, and it is like the guarantee. [3] His saying: whichever he wishes; i.e., the buyer, and the coercer is like a usurper, and the usurper's usurper, and the owner may choose to hold liable whichever of them he wishes. If he holds the coercer liable, he has recourse to the buyer for its value because he stands in the place of the seller: i.e., by paying the guarantee; because the guaranteed thing becomes the property of the guarantor at the time of cause. And if he holds the buyer liable, every purchase after his purchase is effective: i.e., the purchase of this guarantor buyer if hands alternate by sales; because he owned it by guarantee, so it appears that he sold his own property, and a purchase before the purchase of this guarantor buyer is not effective; because the buyer's ownership is based on the time of his receipt. Thus in al-Kifayah (8:171-172). (1) Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 538). (2) See: Dhakhirat al-Uqba (p. 538). (3) al-Kifayah (8:171-172).

Commentary
This passage from 'Imdad al-Ri'ayah' is a critical refinement on the chapter of coercion (al-Ikrāh). It opens by establishing a key principle: when a coercer compels someone to deliver a sold item, the coerced person (al-fāʿil) is treated as an instrument (ālah) of the coercer (al-ḥāmil/al-mukrih) for the purpose of delivery. This means the delivery itself is effective (yanfudh) and the value (al-qīmah) must be paid. The passage then classifies coerced acts into two broad categories. First, acts where the coerced person is NOT an instrument—these are acts like eating and drinking, where the ruling (e.g., nullification of fast) applies only to the coerced person, not the coercer. For example, if a fasting person compels another to break the fast, only the coerced person's fast is invalidated. However, when the act involves destruction (itlāf), such as compelling someone to eat another's property, the transmitted reports (al-marwiyyāt) differ on whether the guarantee (ḍamān) falls on the coerced or the coercer. Similarly, in coerced adultery, the dower is on the adulterer, but if the slave-girl perishes, the guarantee should be on the coercer. The second category comprises acts where the coerced CAN be considered an instrument. This is further divided into two sub-categories. The first sub-category: where treating the coerced as an instrument would change the locus (maḥall) of the offense, thereby contradicting the coercer's intent—so the act remains attributed to the coerced, not the coercer. For instance, if A compels B to commit an offense in a specific place, and B does it elsewhere, then B is acting willingly, not under coercion. The second sub-category: where changing the locus would change the essence of the act itself, such as when A compels B to sell and deliver an item. If delivery were attributed to A (the coercer), it would become usurpation (ghaṣb), because A has no ownership. Therefore, delivery is attributed to B (the coerced), completing the contract, and the buyer acquires defective ownership (milk fāsid). The passage then applies this to a specific case: if only the seller is coerced, not the buyer, and the sold item perishes in the buyer's hand, the buyer must guarantee its value. The buyer may choose to hold either the coercer or the seller (as original owner) liable. If he holds the coercer, the coercer can then recover from the buyer. The footnotes clarify that the receipt of the price (qabḍ al-thaman) is not treated like delivery because it does not involve changing the locus of an offense. The guarantee is by value because the sale is defective (fāsid). The buyer's recourse and the effect on subsequent sales are also explained.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Compelling a person to do something against his will, with the threat of harm.
الفاعل (al-fāʿil): The person who performs the coerced act (the direct doer).
الحامل (al-ḥāmil): The one who compels or causes the coercion (the coercer).
آلة (ālah): Instrument: treating the coerced person as a mere tool of the coercer, so the act is attributed to the coercer.
محل الجناية (maḥall al-jināyah): The specific object or place of the offense.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification in this passage, what is the ruling when a person is coerced into selling a specific item, and the buyer receives it?
A) The sale is completely void (bāṭil) and ownership never transfers.
B) The sale is defective (fāsid) and the buyer gets defective ownership (milk fāsid).
C) The sale is valid and effective because the coerced is an instrument of the coercer.
D) The sale is suspended until the coerced person ratifies it.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a coerced sale is fāsid (defective) not bāṭil, applying the instrument classification to attribute delivery to the coerced person while preserving the coercer's potential liability for destruction.

Coerced Release (Ibra') is Invalid
Ruling with proof: Coerced ibra' is invalid by qiyas to acknowledgement, which cannot be done jokingly.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that coerced ibra' is invalid and explain the legal reasoning based on its nature as an acknowledgement.

Core Idea
Coercion invalidates ibra' because it is analogous to an acknowledgement requiring full consent.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from later Hanafi fatwa works: a coerced release (ibra') of a debtor or guarantor is invalid. The passage from Radd al-Muhtar explains that ibra' is akin to an acknowledgement of discharge, which cannot be done jokingly (hazl), and coercion likewise vitiates the act. Understanding this ruling clarifies the Hanafi emphasis on consent in gratuitous legal acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Locate the ruling: coerced ibra' of a debtor or guarantor is invalid (la yasihh). The passage explicitly states 'لا يصح مع الإكراه إبراؤه مديونه أو إبراؤه كفيله'.
2
Understand the legal analogy (qiyas)
The reason given: ibra' is not valid with hazl (jest) because it is an acknowledgement (iqrar) of discharge. Coercion affects it similarly. The implicit analogy: like acknowledgement, ibra' requires full consent.
Analogy: acknowledgement is an admission of liability; ibra' is an admission of discharge. Both require a deliberate, voluntary statement.
3
Apply the ruling to a scenario
If a creditor is compelled to release a debtor, the release is void. The debt remains owed. Conversely, a voluntary release is valid and extinguishes the debt.
This differs from sales, where coercion might render the contract fasid (defective) but not necessarily batil.
4
Note the evidentiary weight
This ruling is from fatawa mutakhkhirin (later fatwa collections) like Radd al-Muhtar. It is the mufta bihi (operative position) in the Hanafi school.
Zahir al-Riwayah may not explicitly cover this scenario, but later jurists settled on this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا إبراؤه مديونه أو كفيله

[٣] قوله : لا إبراؤه مديونه أو كفيله ; أي [لا يصح مع الإكراه إبراؤه مديونه أو إبراؤه كفيله بنفس أو مال ; لأن البراءة لا تصح مع الهزل ; لأنها إقرار لفراغ الذمة، فيؤثر فيها الإكراه.

وكذا لو أكره الشفيع أن يسكت عن طلب الشفعة فسكت لا تبطل شفعته إذا زال الإكراه فطلب، وإلا فبطلت وكذا لو أكره على تسليمها بعد طلبها لا تبطل. هكذا في رد المحتار
Not his release of his debtor or his guarantor. [3] His statement: 'not his release of his debtor or his guarantor' meaning [it is not valid under coercion] his release of his debtor or his release of his guarantor concerning a person or property, because release is not valid with jest because it is an acknowledgement of discharge of liability, so coercion affects it. Likewise, if the pre-emptor is coerced into remaining silent about claiming pre-emption and he remains silent, his pre-emption right is not nullified if he claims after the coercion is removed, otherwise it is nullified. And likewise, if he is coerced into surrendering it after claiming, it is not nullified. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage addresses two rulings about coercion. First, the coerced release (ibra') of a debtor or guarantor is invalid because ibra' is akin to an acknowledgement that the liability has been discharged; it cannot be done jokingly (hazl). Since coercion removes consent, it invalidates ibra'. Second, regarding pre-emption (shuf'a): if a pre-emptor is coerced into remaining silent about claiming his right, his right is not lost as long as he claims after coercion ends; if he remains silent without coercion, his right is nullified. Similarly, if he is coerced into surrendering the property after claiming, his right is not nullified. These rulings are from Radd al-Muhtar, a late Hanafi fatwa collection.

Key Terms
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Release or discharge of a debtor from liability, a unilateral act of relinquishment.
الهزل (al-hazl): Jest or joking; acts that are not meant seriously.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi ruling on a coerced release (ibra') of a debtor?
A) Valid, because the creditor's statement alone suffices.
B) Invalid, because ibra' requires free consent like an acknowledgement.
C) Valid only if the debtor agrees.
D) Invalid only if the coercion is physical.

Key Takeaway
Coerced ibra' is batil (void) because it requires free consent, analogous to an acknowledgement.

Coerced Silence on Shuf'a Does Not Nullify the Right
Ruling with conditions: Coerced silence does not nullify shuf'a; coercion must be the cause of silence, and the right must be claimed after coercion ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a pre-emptor's right is not nullified if he remains silent under coercion, and apply the condition that he must claim after coercion ends.

Core Idea
Coerced silence on claiming shuf'a does not forfeit the right; it is preserved until coercion lifts.

This lesson addresses a practical scenario in pre-emption (shuf'a): what if a co-owner is forced to stay silent about his right? The passage from Radd al-Muhtar clarifies that such coercion does not nullify the right, provided the pre-emptor claims it promptly after the coercion ends. This ruling protects the pre-emptor from duress and ensures that rights are only lost through deliberate waiver.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and ruling
The passage states: if a pre-emptor is coerced into remaining silent about claiming shuf'a, and he remains silent, his right is not nullified if he claims after the coercion is removed. If he remains silent without coercion, it is nullified.
Key distinction: absence of coercion vs. presence of coercion.
2
Understand the condition for preservation
The right is preserved only if the pre-emptor claims shuf'a after the coercion ends. If he remains silent even after coercion (i.e., he acquiesces), the right is nullified.
The coercion must be the cause of the silence; once free, he must act.
3
Parallel rule: coerced surrender after claiming
If a pre-emptor is coerced into surrendering the property after having already claimed his right, the surrender does not nullify his right. The claim was validly made.
This protects the pre-emptor's right even if he is forced to relinquish later.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Example: A co-owner is threatened to not claim shuf'a. He obeys. After the threat ends, he immediately files a claim. His right is valid. If he waits without coercion for a long time, he loses it.

Source Text

Likewise, if the pre-emptor is coerced into remaining silent about claiming pre-emption and he remains silent, his pre-emption right is not nullified if he claims after the coercion is removed, otherwise it is nullified. This passage addresses two rulings about coercion. First, the coerced release (ibra') of a debtor or guarantor is invalid because ibra' is akin to an acknowledgement that the liability has been discharged; it cannot be done jokingly (hazl). Since coercion removes consent, it invalidates ibra'. Second, regarding pre-emption (shuf'a): if a pre-emptor is coerced into remaining silent about claiming his right, his right is not lost as long as he claims after coercion ends; if he remains silent without coercion, his right is nullified. Similarly, if he is coerced into surrendering the property after claiming, his right is not nullified. These rulings are from Radd al-Muhtar, a late Hanafi fatwa collection.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'a): Pre-emption right of a co-owner to purchase a sold share before outsiders.
الإكراه (al-ikrah): Coercion that forces someone to act or refrain against their will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Immediately upon silence.
B) If he does not claim after the coercion ends.
C) Only if the coercion is severe.
D) Never.

Key Takeaway
Coerced silence does not forfeit shuf'a; the right is restored if claimed after coercion lifts.

Contracts of Restricted Persons: The Hanafi Doctrine of Mawquf
Ruling on contracts made by restricted individuals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on contracts made by a minor, slave, or insane person who understands the contract, and distinguish between the guardian's role for slaves versus minors and insane individuals.

Core Idea
A contract by a restricted person with understanding is valid but suspended—ratification makes it effective, rejection nullifies it.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all contracts are immediately binding. The capacity of the contracting party matters. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah discusses contracts made by minors, slaves, and the insane. It establishes that if they understand the nature of the contract, the contract is valid but suspended (mawquf) pending ratification by their guardian or master. However, the guardian's discretion differs based on whose right is affected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their capacity
The passage covers three parties: minor, slave, and insane. They all have partial or restricted legal capacity, which affects the validity of their contracts.
2
Check the condition of understanding
For the contract to be valid in principle, the person must understand the nature of the contract (benefit and harm). Without this, the contract is void.
The Arabic phrase 'ya'qilu' means understanding the benefit and harm.
3
Classify the contract type
If understanding is present, the contract is concluded as mawquf (suspended). It is not immediately effective, but it is not void.
Mawquf is a distinct category in Hanafi fiqh.
4
Analyze the guardian's role
For the slave, the master has free choice because the suspension is for his right. For the minor and insane, the guardian must act in their best interest because the suspension is for their welfare.
Ratification (ijazah) relates back to the time of contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن عقد منهم وهو يعقله أجاز وليه أو ردّ. قوله: منهم يرجع إلى الصبي والعبد والمجنون. قوله: وهو يعقله؛ أي يعقل نفع العقد ومضرته، يعني يعرف أن البيع جالب للثمن، سالب للمبيع، والشراء بالعكس مثلاً. قوله: أجاز وليه إن شاء؛ أورد إن شاء؛ لأنَّ التوقف في العبد كان لحق المولى، فيتخير المولى فيه، وفي الصبي والمجنون كان نظراً لهما، فليتحرى مصلحتهما، وفيه لا بد أن يعقل العقد؛ ليوجد ركن العقد، فينعقد موقوفاً على الإجازة.
And whoever among them makes a contract while he understands it, his guardian may ratify it or reject it. His saying: 'among them' refers to the minor, slave, and insane person. His saying: 'while he understands it' means he understands the benefit and harm of the contract, i.e., he knows that sale brings the price and removes the sold item, and purchase is the opposite, for example. His saying: 'his guardian may ratify if he wishes' – he added 'if he wishes' because the suspension in the case of the slave is due to the right of the master, so the master may choose; in the case of the minor and insane, it is for their own welfare, so the guardian must seek their best interest. In this, it is necessary that he understand the contract so that the pillar of the contract exists, and it is concluded as suspended pending ratification.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling for contracts ('uqood) made by a minor (sabiyy), a slave ('abd), and an insane person (majnun). The key condition is that the person must have intellectual understanding (ya'qilu) of the contract's nature. If they do, the contract is valid but suspended (mawquf) on the ratification of the guardian or master. The guardian's role differs: for the slave, it is to protect the master's right, so the master may freely choose; for the minor and insane, it is to protect their own welfare, so the guardian must consider their best interest. This is because the minor and insane lack full legal capacity, but they may have partial understanding (like the ma'tuh who can act as an agent). The ruling excludes gifts (hibah), divorce (talaq), and manumission (itq) because these are either pure benefit or pure harm, and thus have different rules.

Key Terms
عقد موقوف (`aqd mawquf): A contract that is valid in itself but depends on external ratification
أجاز وليه (ajaza waliyyuhu): The guardian ratifies the contract, making it effective from the time of contracting
يعقله (ya'qiluhu): He understands it – specifically understanding the benefit and harm of the contract

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A 15-year-old boy who understands trade sells his book for 10 dirhams. The sale is:
A) Void (batil) because he is a minor
B) Valid and binding immediately
C) Suspended on his guardian's ratification
D) Valid only if his father sold it

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, contracts by minors, slaves, and the insane who understand the transaction are valid but suspended (mawquf) pending ratification by the guardian or master.

No Interdiction for Foolishness or Debt According to Abu Hanifa
Ruling that a free adult cannot be interdicted for certain reasons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Abu Hanifa's position on hijr for safah, fisq, and dayn and explain why the mufta bihi of the Hanafi school follows the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa: no hijr for safah, fisq, or dayn. Mufta bihi: hijr for safah is allowed (Sahibayn).

This lesson examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute over legal interdiction (hijr). Abu Hanifa held that a free adult with full legal capacity cannot be restricted in handling his property, even if he is foolish or sinful. The Sahibayn disagreed, allowing a judge to impose hijr to protect the individual's wealth. The relied-upon position in the school is that of the Sahibayn.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The passage states that a free mukallaf is not placed under hijr for safah, fisq, or dayn. This is the ruling of Abu Hanifa.
The ruling is negative: no hijr.
2
Locate the dispute within the Hanafi school
The phrase 'this is according to Abu Hanifa' signals that the Sahibayn hold a different view. Identify their position: they allow hijr for safah (not for fisq or dayn alone).
3
Follow each imam's proof
Abu Hanifa relies on the principle that an adult has full legal capacity (ahliyyah) and no explicit text restricts him. The Sahibayn analogize to the hijr of minors (qiyas) and emphasize protection of property.
The proofs are in the commentary.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Abu Hanifa, a spendthrift adult cannot be stopped; under the Sahibayn, a judge may appoint a guardian over his property.
5
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position in fatwa is that of the Sahibayn: hijr for safah is permissible. This is the operative ruling.
Tarjih is by later Hanafi masters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يُحْجَرُ حَرٌ مكلّفٌ بِسَفِهِ وفسق ودَيْن، هذا عند أبي حنيفة
And a free, legally responsible person is not placed under interdiction due to his foolishness, immorality, and debt. This is according to Abu Hanifa.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents Abu Hanifa's position that a free, adult, sane person (hurr mukallaf) cannot be subjected to legal interdiction (hijr) on grounds of foolishness (safah), immorality (fisq), or debt (dayn). The term 'safah' here specifically means reckless squandering of wealth, such as paying singers or throwing money into the sea. Abu Hanifa argues that such a person has full legal capacity (ahliyyat al-wujub and ahliyyat al-ada'), and restriction would violate personal freedom. The commentary notes that safah in legal terminology refers to wasting money contrary to shar'i and rational norms. This is reported as the view of Abu Hanifa; the phrase 'this is according to Abu Hanifa' implies that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) may differ. Indeed, in the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that a person can be interdicted for foolishness under certain conditions, as per the Sahibayn. Thus, this passage records an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
سفه (safah): Foolishness in spending, i.e., wasting money contrary to reason and shari'ah
فسق (fisq): Immorality, sinfulness
دين (dayn): Debt

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) He cannot be interdicted because he is legally responsible
C) He can be interdicted only if he is a minor
D) He is interdicted automatically because of safah

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa's strict view on non-interdiction for safah is not the final school position; the mufta bihi permits hijr for foolishness to protect wealth.

Hajr on the Fasiq and the Three Special Categories
Correcting a textual error and clarifying Hanafi hajr on three special categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that hajr (financial interdiction) is not permitted on a fasiq, identify the three special categories who may be interdicted, and explain why that interdiction is sensory rather than legal.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa prohibits hajr on a fasiq; only three harmful persons can be physically prevented, not legally interdicted.

This lesson addresses a common textual corruption found in some manuscripts of Sharh al-Wiqaya, which falsely implies that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad permitted hajr on a sinful person. We will correct this error, establish the authentic Hanafi position, and delve into the unique ruling regarding three specific individuals whose harm to society justifies preventive action by the ruler.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Textual Error
In most copies of the text, a phrase states that 'according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, he may place interdiction on a sinful person.' The author of the hashya points out that this is a corruption, as the two disciples actually do not differ with Abu Hanifa on this point. Verify the correct transmission: the two disciples permit hajr except for sinfulness, while al-Shafi'i permits it entirely.
The error arose from a scribal misplacement of the conjunction.
2
State the Correct Hanafi Position
Abu Hanifa's ruling: No hajr on a free, adult, sane person due to foolishness, debt, sin, or negligence. The two disciples also do not permit it for sinfulness. Thus, the Hanafi school unanimously does not allow hajr on a fasiq. The judge may only interdict a bankrupt at the creditors' request.
This is confirmed by Mufti al-Thaqalayn and later authorities.
3
List the Three Special Categories
The text then states: 'بل مفت ماجن' (rather, an immoral mufti). Abu Hanifa permits interdiction – actually sensory prevention – on three types: (1) al-mufti al-mājin – a mufti who teaches false legal tricks, (2) al-ṭabīb al-jāhil – an ignorant doctor who gives lethal medicines, and (3) al-mukārī al-muflis – a bankrupt carrier who takes hire but has no animals. Understand the description of each from the passage.
These three cause widespread harm and their prevention is part of commanding good and forbidding evil.
4
Differentiate Sensory Prevention from Legal Hajr
The passage from al-Badā'i' clarifies that 'hajr' here is not the legal meaning that invalidates transactions. For example, if the mufti gives a fatwa after being prevented, if correct it is valid; if the doctor sells medicines, his sale is valid. This shows it is only physical restraint (man' hissi) – the ruler may stop them from practising their harmful trade, but their financial acts remain legally effective.
This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding the scope of the ruler's intervention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إذا طلب غرماء المفلس الحجر عليه حَجَرَه القاضي ومنعه من البيع والإقرار، وعندهما وعند الشافعي له يحجر على الفاسق زجراً له، بل مفت ماجن. قال في الحاشية: ثم نقول وقع بعد قوله والإقرار في أكثر النسخ بل في كلها: وعندهما وعند الشافعي له يحجر على الفاسق، وفي أقلها عندهما بلا عطف، وعلى التقديرين لا ريبة في فساده؛ لأنه صريح في وقوع الخلاف منهما في الفاسق. وقد صرّح مفتي الثقلين بنفيه، حيث قال: اعلم أن الحجر عند أبي حنيفة على الحر البالغ العاقل لا يجوز بسبب السفه والدين والفسق والغفلة، وعنهما يجوز بغير الفسق، وعند الشافعي يجوز بالكل. انتهى. قوله: بل مفت ماجن... الخ؛ أي لا يحجر حر مكلف، بل يحجر هؤلاء الثلاث. اعلم أن الماجن: هو الذي لا يبالي ما صنع وما قيل له، ومصدره المجون، والمجانة اسم منه، والفعل من باب طلب. وفي الذخيرة: المفتي الماجن: هو الذي يعلم الناس الحيل الباطلة، بأن يعلّم المرأة حتى ترتد فتبين من الزوج، ويعلم الرجل أن يرتد فيسقط عنه الزكاة، ثم يُسلم، ولا يبالي أن تحرم حلالاً أو يحل حراماً، فضرره متعد إلى العامة. [ص102] وطبيب جاهل ومكار مفلس فإن بلغ غير رشيد لم يُسَلّم إليه ماله حتى يبلغ خمسا وعشرين سنة. اعلم أن أبا حنيفة يرى الحجر على هؤلاء الثلاثة دفعاً لضررهم عن الناس، فالمفتي الماجن: هو الذي يعلم الناس الحيل، والمكاري المفلس: هو الذي يُكاري الدابة ويأخذ الكراء فإذا جاء أوان السفر لا دابة له فانقطع المكتري عن الرفقة. والطبيب الجاهل: فإنّه يسقي الناس في أمراضهم دواء مهلكاً، وهو يعلم ذلك، أو لم يعلم. والمكاري المفلس: وهو الذي يتقبل الكراء، ويؤاجر الإبل، وليس له إبل ولا غيرها يحمل عليه، ولا مال يشتري به الدواب. والناس يعتمدون عليه ويدفعون الكراء إليه، ويتصرف هو ما أخذ منهم في حاجته، وإذا جاء أوان الخروج يخفي هو نفسه، فيذهب أموال الناس، وربما يصير ذلك سبباً لتقاعدهم عن الخروج إلى الحج والغزو، وفساد هذا الشخص متعد أيضاً. هكذا في الكفاية. قال في البدائع: ليس المراد بالحجر هاهنا حقيقة الحجر، وهو المعنى الشرعي الذي يمنع نفوذ التصرف، ألا ترى أن المفتي لو أفتى بعد الحجر وأصاب في الفتوى جاز، ولو أفتى قبل الحجر وأخطأ لم يجز. وكذا الطبيب لو باع الأدوية بعد الحجر نفذ بيعه، فدل هذا على أنه ما أراد به الحجر حقيقةً، وإنما أراد به المنع الحسي: أي يمنع هؤلاء الثلاثة عن عملهم حساً؛ لأنّ المنع عن ذلك من باب الأمر بالمعروف والنهي عن المنكر. كذا قال الأستاذ في غرره. انتهى. كذا في حاشية الجلبي. قوله: خمساً وعشرين سنة يسلّم إليه ماله وإن لم يؤنس منه الرشد.
And also, if the creditors of a bankrupt person request interdiction against him, the judge places him under interdiction and prevents him from selling and acknowledging. And according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and according to al-Shafi'i, he may place interdiction on a sinful person as a deterrent. Rather, [the mufta bihi is] an immoral mufti. He said in the marginalia: Then we say: after his statement 'and acknowledging' in most copies, rather in all of them, it occurs: 'and according to them and according to al-Shafi'i he may place interdiction on a sinful person', and in fewer copies: 'according to them' without conjunction. In both cases, there is no doubt about its corruption, because it is explicit in the occurrence of disagreement from them (the two) concerning the sinful person. And the Mufti of the Two Worlds has explicitly denied it, where he said: Know that interdiction according to Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, is not permissible for a free, adult, sane person due to foolishness, debt, sinfulness, or negligence; according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is permissible except for sinfulness; and according to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, it is permissible entirely. End quote. His statement: 'Rather, an immoral mufti...' etc., means: He does not place interdiction on a legally responsible free person, rather he places interdiction on these three. Know that al-mājin is one who does not care what he does or what is said to him; its verbal noun is al-majūn, and al-majāna is a noun from it, the verb from the paradigm of ṭalaba. And in al-Dhakhīra: The immoral mufti is one who teaches people false legal stratagems, such that he teaches a woman to apostatize so that she separates from her husband, and teaches a man to apostatize so that zakat is dropped from him, then he returns to Islam, and he does not care if he makes permissible what is forbidden or forbids what is permissible; his harm extends to the public. [Page 102] And an ignorant doctor and a bankrupt carrier. And if [a minor] reaches [maturity] and is not prudent, his wealth is not handed over to him until he reaches twenty-five years. Know that Abu Hanifa holds interdiction on these three to ward off their harm from people. The immoral mufti is the one who teaches people stratagems. The bankrupt carrier is one who rents out an animal and takes the hire, then when the time for travel comes, he has no animal, so the hirer is stranded from the caravan. And the ignorant doctor is one who gives people a deadly potion for their illnesses, whether he knows it or not. And the bankrupt carrier is one who undertakes carriage and hires out camels, but he has no camels nor anything else to carry on, nor money to buy animals. People rely on him and pay him the hire, and he spends what he took from them on his needs. When the time of departure comes, he hides himself, so people's money is lost, and perhaps that becomes a reason for their failure to go out for Hajj and jihad, and the corruption of this person is also widespread. Thus in al-Kifāya. He said in al-Badā'i': What is meant by interdiction here is not the true interdiction, which is the legal meaning that prevents the validity of transactions. Do you not see that if the mufti gives a fatwa after interdiction and hits the correct ruling, it is permissible; but if he gave a fatwa before interdiction and made a mistake, it is not permissible. Similarly, if the doctor sold medicines after interdiction, his sale is valid. This indicates that he did not intend true interdiction; rather he intended sensory prevention: i.e., these three are prevented from their work physically, because preventing that is of the type of commanding good and forbidding evil. Thus said the professor in his Ghurar. End quote. Thus in Hāshiyat al-Jalabī. His statement: 'twenty-five years' means: his wealth is handed over to him even if no prudence is observed from him.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement from a super-commentary on Sharh al-Wiqaya, addressing a textual corruption and clarifying the Hanafi position on financial interdiction (hajr). The author first notes a scribal error: in many copies, the text states that according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and al-Shafi'i, the judge may place interdiction on a sinful person (fasiq) as a deterrent. However, this is incorrect because it implies that the two disciples disagreed with Abu Hanifa on this point. The Mufti of the Two Worlds (a reference to a later authority) clarifies that Abu Hanifa does not permit interdiction on a free adult sane person due to foolishness, debt, sinfulness, or negligence. The two disciples permit it except for sinfulness; al-Shafi'i permits it entirely. Thus, the correct position is that the Hanafi school does not allow interdiction on a fasiq. Instead, the text says 'بل مفت ماجن' (rather, an immoral mufti), indicating that Abu Hanifa only permits interdiction on three specific types of people: an immoral mufti (who teaches false legal tricks), an ignorant doctor (who gives lethal medicines), and a bankrupt carrier (who takes hire but has no animals). These three cause widespread harm, and the ruler may physically prevent them from practicing, but this is not the legal hajr that nullifies transactions; rather it is a sensory/administrative prevention (man' hissi) as part of commanding good and forbidding evil. The passage also includes the rule that a person who reaches puberty but is not yet prudent (non-rashid) should not be given his wealth until he reaches twenty-five years according to Abu Hanifa, and then it is handed over even if no prudence is seen. This is the last sentence of the lesson.

Key Terms
الحجر (al-hajr): Financial interdiction restricting a person's right to dispose of property.
المفتي الماجن (al-muftī al-mājin): A mufti who teaches people false legal stratagems without caring about halal and haram, causing widespread harm.
المكاري المفلس (al-mukārī al-muflis): A carrier who hires out animals for transport, takes hire, but has no animals or money, defrauding people.
الطبيب الجاهل (al-ṭabīb al-jāhil): A doctor who administers lethal medicines, whether knowingly or not, causing harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the corrected Hanafi position in this passage, what type of prevention is applied to the three special categories (immoral mufti, ignorant doctor, bankrupt carrier)?
A) Legal interdiction (hajr shar'i) that invalidates all their transactions.
B) Sensory prevention (man' hissī) that physically stops them from working but does not affect their legal capacity.
C) Financial penalty only.
D) Imprisonment and nullification of all past contracts.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, hajr on a fasiq is not permitted; only three harmful persons can be physically prevented from their harmful activities, and this prevention is sensory, not legal interdiction.

Imprisonment of a Free Debtor: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on interdiction of a free debtor

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on imprisoning a free debtor for debt and distinguish between the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding interdiction (hajr).

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa refused interdiction for debt, allowing imprisonment only; the Sahibayn saw hijr as necessary to protect creditors.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a judge may impose interdiction (hajr) on a free debtor at the creditors' request. Abu Hanifa holds that hijr nullifies legal capacity and is not justified by a specific harm; instead, the judge may imprison the debtor to force him to sell his own property. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow hijr to prevent the debtor from selling below market value or alienating property. The passage also discusses the permissibility of selling one currency for another by istihsan and the argument over talji'a (asset alienation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Legal Issue
The core question: may a judge, at the request of creditors, impose interdiction (hajr) on a free debtor to prevent him from disposing of his property?
The passage treats this as an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
2
State Abu Hanifa's Position and Proof
Abu Hanifa: no hijr in debt. His proof: hijr nullifies legal capacity (ahliyyah), which is not permissible to repel a specific harm. He cites Quran 4:29 prohibiting trade without mutual consent. The judge may only imprison the debtor to force him to sell his own property.
Abu Hanifa sees imprisonment as sufficient to compel payment.
3
State the Sahibayn's Position and Proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: hijr may be imposed upon creditors' request to prevent the debtor from selling below market price or making a talji'a (alienation). They analogize to hijr of a safīh (spendthrift), but here for creditors' benefit.
The Sahibayn focus on protecting creditors from fraudulent transfers.
4
Analyze Abu Hanifa's Response
Abu Hanifa argues that the fear of talji'a is imaginary (mawhūma). The legitimate goal is payment of debt, which can be achieved by imprisonment without hijr. Sale is not a specific way; the debtor may choose any lawful means.
The response rejects the analogy to safīh because the purpose differs.
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under Sahibayn, the judge restricts the debtor's transactions; under Abu Hanifa, the debtor remains free to sell but is imprisoned until payment. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's, as recorded in al-Hidayah.
This difference affects the judge's authority over the debtor's property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣]قوله : وحبس القاضي المديون...الخ ؛ قال أبو حنيفة : لا حجر في الدين، ليبيع ماله لدينه، وقضى دراهم دينه من دراهمه، وباع دنانيره لدراهم دينه ، وبالعكس استحساناً)، اعلم أنَّ القياس أن لا يبيع الدراهم لأجل دنانير الدين ، ولا الدنانير لأجل دراهم الدين فإذا وجبت ديون على حرّ، وطلب غرماؤه حبسه والحجر عليه، لا يحجر عليه ؛ لأنّ في الحجر إهدار أهليته ، فلا يجوز لدفع ضرر خاص.
فإنّ له مال لم يتصرّف القاضي فيه ؛ لأنه نوع حجر، ولأنه تجارة لا عن تراض، فيكون حراماً بالنص، وهو قوله عة: ﴿ يَتَأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا لَا تَأْكُلُوا أَمْوَالَكُم كم ) : أي بالحرام شرعاً بالغصب والربا إِلَّا أَن تَكُونَ تِجَرَةٌ عَن تَرَاضِ ) ، ولكن يحبسه القاضي ليبيع ماله لدينه... الخ.

ومعنى قولهما : منعه من البيع ؛ أن يكون البيع بأقل من ثمن المثل، أما البيع بثمن
منه ،
المثل لا يبطل حق الغرماء، والمنع لحقهم، فلا يمنع وباع ماله إن امتنع المفلس من بيعه، وقسمه بين غرمائه بالحصص عندهما ؛ لأنّ البيع مستحق لإيفاء ديونه، حتى يحبس لأجله، فإذا امتنع ناب القاضي منابه ، كما في الحب والعُنَّه.

[3] His saying: And the judge imprisons the debtor... etc.; Abu Hanifa said: There is no interdiction in debt, rather he (the debtor) should sell his property for his debt, and he pays his debt dirhams from his dirhams, and he sells his dinars for his debt dirhams, and vice versa by istihsan. Know that by qiyas he should not sell dirhams for dinar debt, nor dinars for dirham debt. So when debts become due on a free man, and his creditors request his imprisonment and interdiction upon him, he is not interdicted because in interdiction there is nullification of his legal capacity, so it is not permitted to repel a specific harm. For he has property; the judge does not dispose of it because it is a type of interdiction, and because it is trade without mutual consent, so it is haram by the text, which is His saying: 'O you who believe, do not consume your wealth among yourselves unjustly except it be a trade by mutual consent' (4:29). But the judge imprisons him so that he sells his property for his debt... etc.
[1] His saying: so that he sells his property; i.e., he imprisons him forever until he sells his property himself to pay his debt. And the two said: When his creditors request interdiction upon him, the judge imposes interdiction on him, and prevents him from selling, disposing, and confessing, so that it does not harm the creditors, because the interdiction on the safih is only permitted as a consideration for him, and this interdiction is a consideration for the creditors, because he might alienate his property and defeat their right. The meaning of their saying: preventing him from selling is that the sale is for less than the fair price; as for selling at the fair price, it does not nullify the creditors' right, and the prevention is for their right, so it does not prevent. And he sells his property if the insolvent refuses to sell it, and distributes it among his creditors proportionally according to them, because the sale is owed to fulfill his debts, for which he is imprisoned. So if he refuses, the judge stands in his place, as in the case of impotence (hubb) and unna. We said: Alienation is imaginary, and what is due is the payment of debt, and sale is not a specific way for that, unlike impotence and unna. And imprisonment for payment of debt, using whichever means he chooses, how? Even if the sale were valid, the imprisonment would be harmful to both, by delaying the creditor's right and torturing the debtor, so it is not legislated. This is stated in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on imprisoning a free debtor (al-madyūn al-ḥurr) by the judge. Abu Hanifa holds that the debtor should not be interdicted (ḥajr) for debt, because interdiction nullifies his legal capacity (ahliyyah) and is not permissible merely to repel a specific harm. Instead, the judge may imprison the debtor to force him to sell his own property to pay the debt. The two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) differ: they allow the judge to impose interdiction at the creditors' request, preventing the debtor from selling below market price or confessing, to protect the creditors from possible asset alienation (talji'a). They argue that interdiction here is for the benefit of the creditors, analogous to the interdiction of a safīh, but with a different purpose. Abu Hanifa responds that the fear of alienation is imaginary, and the legitimate goal is payment of debt, which can be achieved by imprisonment without full interdiction. The passage also discusses the specific case of selling currency (dinar for dirham debt and vice versa) by istihsan, against the qiyas that would prohibit it. The preferred position (from al-Hidayah) is Abu Hanifa's: imprisonment without hijr, and the debtor sells his own property.

Key Terms
حجر في الدين (hajr fī al-dayn): Interdiction imposed due to debt, preventing the debtor from disposing of his property
إستحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference over qiyas; here, allowing sale of currency types for different debts
تلجئة (talji'a): A lawful form of asset alienation (gift with hidden condition) to defeat creditors

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what action may the judge take against a free debtor who refuses to pay?
A) Impose hijr (interdiction) and sell his property
B) Imprison him to force him to sell his own property
C) Directly sell his property without imprisonment
D) Leave him free without any action

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi mufta bihi is that a judge may imprison a free debtor but may not impose interdiction; the debtor must sell his property voluntarily.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

Istihsan Overrides Qiyas in Currency Debt Sales
Intra-Hanafi ruling on selling dirhams for dinars debt via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school permits (via istihsan) the sale of dirhams to discharge a dinars debt, and identify the thamarah al-khilaf between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Istihsan permits the sale of one currency for another debt because both share monetary value, overriding qiyas.

This lesson explores a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: Abu Hanifa rules by qiyas that selling dirhams to pay a dinars debt is invalid, while his disciples Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani adopt istihsan and permit it. The key lies in how they weigh the unity of monetary value against the difference in form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
First, note that Abu Hanifa prohibits the sale via qiyas, while the Sahibayn permit it via istihsan. This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The disagreement is between the founder and his main students, not between madhhabs.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas says: since dirhams and dinars are different types, exchanging one for the other to discharge a debt is akin to an invalid sale of debt for non-currency.
Abu Hanifa focuses on the difference in genus (coinage).
3
Understand the istihsan argument
The Sahibayn argue that dirhams and dinars are united in thumaniyya (monetary value) and wealth, as evidenced by their joining in zakat. This unity allows the sale.
Istihsan here considers the underlying economic reality over formal difference.
4
See the practical consequence
Under the Sahibayn's view, the judge can sell the debtor's dirhams to pay a dinars debt, facilitating debt recovery. Under Abu Hanifa's view, such a sale would be void.
This thamarah al-khilaf affects how courts handle overlapping currency debts.
5
Know the relied-upon position
The mufta bihi (operative ruling) is the Sahibayn's istihsan, as it is more practical and has been adopted by later Hanafi authorities.
This is a standard example of Hanafi methodology: istihsan can override a clear qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أنَّ القياس أن لا يبيع الدراهم لأجل دنانير الدين، ولا الدنانير لأجل دراهم الدين، لأنهما مختلفان، لكن في الاستحسان أن يباع كل واحد لأجل الآخر؛ لأنهما متحدان في الثمنية. وجه الاستحسان: إنّ الدراهم والدنانير متحدان في الثمنية والمالية، ألا ترى أنه يضم أحدهما في الآخر في الزكاة، مختلفان في الصورة، فبالنظر إلى اتحادهما يثبت للقاضي ولاية التصرف، وبالنظر إلى اختلافهما يسلب عن الدائن ولاية الأخذ عملاً بالشبهين.
Know that analogy is that one should not sell dirhams for dinars debt, nor dinars for dirhams debt, because they are different. But in istihsan, each may be sold for the other because they are united in monetary value. The basis of istihsan is that dirhams and dinars are united in monetary value and wealth; do you not see that one is joined with the other in zakat? They differ in form, so considering their unity, the judge has authority to transact, and considering their difference, the creditor is deprived of the right to take, acting on both aspects.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage addresses a dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding selling dirhams to pay a debt owed in dinars, and vice versa. Abu Hanifa holds that such a sale is not allowed on the basis of qiyas (analogy) because dirhams and dinars are different types of currency, so exchanging one for the other to discharge a debt of a different type is invalid. However, the disciples adopt istihsan (juristic preference) and permit the sale because both are unified in being monetary media (thumaniyya) and have the same underlying value. The istihsan reasoning is that while they differ in form, they are considered jointly in zakat; the judge may exercise authority to transact on behalf of the debtor when necessary, but the creditor cannot forcibly take one for the other. This is a classic example of Hanafi juristic methodology where istihsan overrides qiyas.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning that prohibits selling dirhams for dinars debt due to difference in type.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that permits the sale due to unity of monetary value.
الثمنية (al-thumaniyya): The quality of being a monetary medium; both dirhams and dinars serve as money.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why is it permitted to sell dirhams to pay a debt in dinars?
A) Because the debtor has no other assets
B) Because dirhams and dinars are united in monetary value
C) Because the creditor has a right to force the sale
D) Because qiyas is not applicable here

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits, via istihsan, selling one currency to discharge a debt in another due to their shared monetary value, overriding qiyas.

Judge's Authority to Sell Bankrupt's Goods
Ruling on judge's sale of bankrupt's movable and immovable property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the judge's sale of the bankrupt's 'urud and 'aqar, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the mufta bihi ruling.

Core Idea
The judge cannot sell the bankrupt's movable goods; only currency—because purpose attaches to the specific good.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the author explains an intra-Hanafi dispute: can the judge force the sale of the bankrupt debtor's movable goods and real estate? Abu Hanifa restricts the judge to liquid assets, while the two disciples (Sahibayn) allow the sale of all assets. The reasoning hinges on whether the purpose (gharad) attaches to the specific identity of the item or to its monetary value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties involved
The scenario involves a bankrupt debtor (muflis) who has movable goods ('urud) and real estate ('aqar) but refuses to sell them to pay debts. The judge (qadi) wants to sell them. Who can force the sale?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa says the judge cannot sell the bankrupt's 'urud and 'aqar. He may only sell dirhams and dinars. Why? Because the purpose (gharad) is attached to the specific forms and entities (suwar wa a'yan) of these goods. Forced sale would violate the owner's right over specific items.
3
State the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say that if the debtor refuses, the judge may sell these assets and distribute proceeds to creditors proportionally. They see the purpose as ultimately financial, not attached to the specific item.
4
Analyze the reasoning (ta'lil)
The passage distinguishes currency (nuqud) as a means (wasila) – its form is irrelevant, so the judge can transact it. Movable goods and real estate are ends (maqsad) – their specific identity matters, so the judge cannot force their sale. This is the key ta'lil.
This reasoning is used by Abu Hanifa to limit the judge's authority.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
The relied-upon position for fatwa (mufta bihi) in later Hanafi practice is that of the Sahibayn, allowing the judge to sell the bankrupt's 'urud and 'aqar. The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa, creditors cannot force sale of these assets; under Sahibayn, they can.
Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah preferred the Sahibayn's view for necessity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا عرضه وعقاره)، خلافاً لهما، فإنَّ المفلس إذا امتنع من بيع العرض والعقار للدين، فالقاضي يبيعهما ويقضي دينه بالحصص. بخلاف العروض؛ لأنّ الغرض يتعلّق بصورها وأعيانها، فلا يبيعها القاضي، أمّا النقود فوسائل، فلا يتعلّق الغرض بصورها، فيتصرفُ القاضي فيها، فافترقا. قوله : من أفلس؛ يقال : أفلس الرجل إذا لم يبق في يده مال، كأن دراهمه صارت فلوساً، كما يقال أخبت الرجل : إذا صار أصحابه خبتاً، وأقطف إذا صارت دابته قطوفاً. قوله : ومعه عرض؛ أي متاعٌ لرجل بعينه اشتراه منه، ولفظ : معه؛ صريح في أن وضع المسألة في أنّ المبتاع باق في يده.
Not his movable goods and real estate, contrary to them (the two disciples). Indeed, when the bankrupt refrains from selling the movable goods and real estate for the debt, the judge sells them and pays his debt proportionally. Unlike movable goods, because the purpose is attached to their forms and entities, so the judge does not sell them. As for currency, they are means, so the purpose is not attached to their forms; thus the judge transacts in them. So they differ. His statement: 'from afalsa'—it is said: a man becomes bankrupt when no money remains in his hand, as if his dirhams became fulus (copper coins). His statement: 'and with him is a commodity'—i.e., a specific man's merchandise that he bought from him, and the word 'with him' indicates that the issue is that the buyer still has it in his hand.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the judge's power to sell the bankrupt debtor's movable goods ('urud) and real estate ('aqar). According to Abu Hanifa, the judge cannot sell these assets; he can only sell dirhams and dinars. The two disciples argue that if the debtor refuses to sell, the judge may sell them and distribute the proceeds among creditors proportionally. The reasoning is that the purpose in movable goods and real estate is attached to their specific identities (forms and entities), so selling them would be like forcing a transaction that the owner dislikes. Currency, however, is a means to an end, not valued for its form, so the judge can transact in it. The passage also provides a linguistic definition of 'iflas' (bankruptcy) and 'ard' (commodity). The term 'mufaflis' refers to one whose dirhams have become worthless like fulus (copper coins). This clarifies the context of the ruling.

Key Terms
العرض (al-'arad): Movable goods or merchandise, as opposed to cash (dirhams/dinars).
أفلس (aflasa): To become bankrupt, having no dirhams or dinars left; according to the text, it implies his money becomes worthless like fulus.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because movable goods are not valuable
B) Because the purpose is attached to their specific forms and entities
C) Because the debtor has the right to refuse
D) Because the judge lacks authority over movable goods

Key Takeaway
The judge's authority to sell bankrupt's assets depends on whether the purpose attaches to the specific identity of the asset; the mufta bihi view allows sale of all assets.
