Seller's Right in Bankruptcy: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on seller's rescission right in bankruptcy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the seller's right when the buyer is bankrupt, explain the Hanafi proof, and compare it with the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Hanafi: The seller is an ordinary creditor, not entitled to rescind solely due to buyer's bankruptcy.

This passage examines a fundamental disagreement between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools on whether a seller can reclaim goods from a bankrupt buyer. The Hanafi position, rooted in a distinction between contractual duties and liability for debt, holds that the seller must share the proceeds with other creditors. Understanding this dispute reveals how each school's conceptual framework shapes its rulings on financial insolvency.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position from the matn
Identify the explicit ruling: the seller is 'uswah li al-ghurama' (equal with creditors) and has no special right to rescind.
The matn says 'fa bai'ahu uswah li al-ghurama'.
2
Identify the opposing school and their position
Recognize that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that upon the buyer's bankruptcy, the seller has the option to rescind (khiyar al-faskh) after the judge places interdiction.
The passage says 'wa qala al-Shafi'i: yahjuru al-qadi 'ala al-mushtari... thumma li al-ba'i khiyar al-faskh'.
3
Follow the opposing proof
Understand the Shafi'i analogy: just as inability to deliver the sold item gives the buyer a right to rescind, inability to pay the price should give the seller a right to rescind.
This is based on the commutative nature of the contract.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi reply distinguishes between delivery of the specific commodity (a contractual duty) and payment of price (a liability in the dhimmah). Insolvency affects only payment, not the commodity, so rescission is not warranted.
The exception is salam where substitution is impossible.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Under Hanafi law, the seller shares proceeds with creditors; under Shafi'i law, the seller can reclaim the specific goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا عرضه وعقاره، ومن أفلس ومعه عرض شراه فبائعه أسوة للغرماء. خلافاً لهما، فإنَّ المفلس إذا امتنع من بيع العرض والعقار للدين، فالقاضي يبيعهما ويقضي دينه بالحصص. وقال الشافعي: يحجر القاضي على المشتري بطلبه، ثم للبائع خيار الفسخ. ولنا: إنَّ الإفلاس موجبُ العجز عن تسليم المنقودة من الدراهم والدنانير، وهو غير مستحق بالعقد، فلا يثبت حق الفسخ باعتباره، وإنّما المستحق وصف في الذمة: أعني الدين، وبقبض العين تتحقق منهما مبادلة هذا الحقيقة، فيجب اعتبارها إلا في موضع التعذر، كالسلم؛ لأنّ الاستبدال ممتنع، فأعطى للعين حكم الدين.
Not his commodities and real estate. And whoever becomes bankrupt and has with him a commodity that he purchased, then his seller is equal with the creditors. Contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). For the bankrupt if he refrains from selling the commodity and real estate for the debt, the judge sells them and satisfies his debt in shares. Al-Shafi'i said: The judge places interdiction on the purchaser at his request, then the seller has the option of rescission. Our proof: Insolvency causes inability to deliver the currency of dirhams and dinars, and it is not a right owed by the contract, so the right of rescission is not established on its basis. Rather, what is owed is a description in the liability, i.e., the debt. With possession of the specific thing, the exchange of this reality is realized from them, so it must be considered except in a case of difficulty, like salam, because substitution is impossible, so the specific thing is given the ruling of the debt.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage presents an inter-madhhab dispute concerning the rights of a seller when the buyer becomes insolvent (muflis) while still in possession of the purchased commodity ('ard). The Hanafi position, attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and supported by the commentator, is that the seller is merely an unsecured creditor (uswah li al-ghurama'), meaning he must share the proceeds with other creditors proportionally. The commentator notes that this is contrary to the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), who hold that the judge can sell even commodities and real estate to satisfy debts. However, the main dispute is with Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that the seller can request the judge to place the buyer under legal interdiction (hajr), after which the seller has the option to rescind the sale (khiyar al-faskh). The Hanafi reply (lana) argues that insolvency only creates inability to deliver currency, which is not a contractual right, so the option to rescind should not arise. Instead, the debt remains in the liability (dhimmah), and the specific goods should be treated as property unless substitution is impossible (as in salam sales). This reasoning relies on conceptual analysis and analogy (qiyas), hence the argument type is dalil aqli.

Key Terms
عرض ('ard): A commodity or movable property other than money (dirhams/dinars) that the buyer purchased from the seller.
أسوة (uswah): Equality; the seller is treated equally with other creditors, sharing the proceeds proportionally.
خيار الفسخ (khiyar al-faskh): The option to rescind a contract, here claimed by the seller due to the buyer's insolvency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of the seller when the buyer is bankrupt and still has the purchased commodity?
A) The seller can rescind the sale and reclaim the commodity.
B) The seller is treated as an ordinary creditor and shares the proceeds with other creditors.
C) The seller can demand the judge to interdict the buyer.
D) The seller can take back the commodity without court order.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school treats the seller as an ordinary creditor in bankruptcy, based on the distinction between contractual duties and debt liability.

Seller's Status When Buyer Becomes Insolvent
Explains that the seller is an ordinary creditor in insolvency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the seller's status when the buyer becomes insolvent and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position, evaluating the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
When a buyer becomes insolvent, the seller is merely an ordinary creditor with no right to rescind the sale.

This passage examines a critical legal dispute: does a seller have the right to reclaim his goods when the buyer becomes insolvent? The Hanafi position, upheld in al-Hidayah, treats the seller as an ordinary creditor, while Imam al-Shafi'i grants a right to rescind. Understanding the reasoning behind each view illuminates the Hanafi emphasis on the completed nature of the sale contract.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: 'sharah fa-ba'i'uhu uswah lil-ghurama' — the seller is equal to other creditors.
This applies when the buyer has the commodity but hasn't paid the price.
2
Identify the opposing school and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the seller can request the judge to declare interdiction (hajr) and then has the option to rescind (khiyar al-faskh).
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Shafi'i argument is based on analogy (qiyas): the buyer's inability to pay is like the seller's inability to deliver the sold item, and commutative contracts require equality, similar to salam.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi rebuttal: insolvency causes inability to deliver currency, but this obligation is not part of the contract; what is owed is a debt in the dhimmah. The sale is already complete with possession, so it stands except in cases of impossibility like salam where substitution is impossible.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Under Hanafi law, the seller cannot reclaim the goods and must queue with creditors. Under Shafi'i law, the seller can annul the sale and take back his goods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شراه فبائعه أسوة للغرماء) : أي أفلس ومعه عرض شراه، ولم يؤد الثمن، فبائعُهُ أسوة للغرماء، وقال الشافعي له : يحجرُ القاضي على المشتري بطلبه ، ثم للبائع خيار الفسخ ". [١] قوله : وأسوة ؛ وهي - بضم الهمزة وكسرها - لغتان فيما أسى به : أي اقتدى به : أي صار بائعُ هذا العرض مقتدياً بغرماء المديون مساوياً معهم. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي [٢] قوله : ثم للبائع خيار الفسخ ؛ لأنه عجز المشتري عن أداء الثمن، فيوجبُ ذلك العجز حق الفسخ ، كعجز البائع عن تسليم المبيع ؛ وهذا لأنه عقد معاوضة، وقضيته المساواة، وصار كالسلم. ولنا : إنّ الإفلاس موجبُ العجز عن تسليم المنقودة من الدراهم والدنانير، وهو غير مستحق بالعقد، فلا يثبت حق الفسخ باعتباره، وإنّما المستحق وصف في الذمة : أعني الدين، وبقبض العين تتحقق منهما مبادلة هذا الحقيقة، فيجب اعتبارها إلا في موضع التعدر، كالسلم؛ لأنّ الاستبدال ممتنع ، فأعطى للعين حكم الدين. كذا في «الهداية).
He bought it, so his seller is equal to the creditors: That is, he became insolvent and with him is the commodity he bought, and he did not pay the price, so his seller is equal to the creditors. Al-Shafi'i said: The judge imposes interdiction on the buyer at his request, then the seller has the option to rescind. [1] His saying: 'And equal' (uswah) — it is with dammah and kasrah of the hamzah, two dialects for what is consoled by it: meaning he followed, i.e., the seller of this commodity became a follower of the debtor's creditors, equal to them. As in Hashiyat al-Jalabi. [2] His saying: 'Then the seller has the option to rescind'; because the buyer's inability to pay the price necessitates the right to rescind, like the seller's inability to deliver the sold item. This is because it is a commutative contract and its requirement is equality, and it becomes like salam (advance payment). Our evidence: Insolvency causes inability to deliver the currency (dirhams and dinars), and it is not owed by the contract, so the right to rescind is not established on its basis. Rather, what is owed is a description in the dhimmah, namely the debt, and by taking possession of the item, the exchange of this reality is realized between them, so it must be considered except in cases of difficulty, like salam, because substitution is impossible, so the item is given the ruling of the debt. As in al-Hidayah.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the rights of a seller when the buyer becomes insolvent after purchasing a commodity on credit. The base text (matn) states that the seller is merely an ordinary creditor (uswah), meaning he shares equally with other creditors and has no preferential right. The commentator explains this: the buyer has the commodity but failed to pay the price, so the seller is like any other creditor. Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the judge may declare the buyer interdicted (hajr) at the seller's request, and then the seller has an option to rescind (khiyar al-faskh) the sale. The Shafi'i argument is that the buyer's inability to pay is analogous to the seller's inability to deliver the sold item, and since it is a commutative contract, equality is required, and it becomes like a salam contract where the buyer fails to deliver the advance payment. The Hanafi response (wa lana) is that insolvency causes inability to deliver the currency (dirhams/dinars), but this inability is not an obligation under the contract; what is owed is a debt in the dhimmah (a liability), not the actual delivery of the specific coins. By taking possession of the commodity, the exchange (mubadalah) of the item for the debt is realized, so the validity of the contract must be maintained except where it is impossible, as in salam, where substitution is impossible and hence the item is given the ruling of the debt. Thus, in a normal sale, no rescission is granted due to insolvency alone. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position is that the seller has no special right to rescind; he is simply an ordinary creditor.

Key Terms
أسوة (uswah): Equality or being a peer; here it means the seller is equal to other creditors in claiming from the insolvent buyer.
خيار الفسخ (khiyar al-faskh): The option to rescind a contract; in this context, the seller's right to annul the sale due to the buyer's insolvency.
إفلاس (iflas): Insolvency; the state of a debtor who has no assets to pay debts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when a buyer becomes insolvent after purchasing a commodity on credit, what is the seller's status?
A) He has the right to rescind the contract.
B) He is like other creditors.
C) He can demand immediate payment from the buyer.
D) The sale is void.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the seller is an ordinary creditor with no right to rescind the sale solely because the buyer becomes insolvent.

The Legal Definition of Idhn: Lifting Interdiction, Not Agency
Defines idhn as lifting interdiction and clarifies the slave's capacity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of idhn (permission for a slave to trade) and distinguish it from agency (tawkil) by explaining that the permitted slave acts for himself and bears personal liability.

Core Idea
Permission lifts the master's right, not creating agency; the slave transacts for himself.

This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya opens the chapter on the Permitted Slave (al-Ma'dhun). It defines idhn as the lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and clarifies that the permitted slave acts with his own capacity, not as an agent. The Hanafi position is contrasted with Imam al-Shafi'i's view that idhn is tawkil. Understanding this distinction is essential for determining liability in commercial transactions of slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its legal context
Locate the keyword 'al-Ma'dhun' (المأذون) and note that it is a verbal noun meaning 'permission', not an adjective. The passage discusses the legal meaning of idhn in the context of a slave who was previously interdicted.
The placement of this chapter after Interdiction is logical: permission presupposes a prior restriction.
2
Extract the Hanafi definition
The passage states: 'الإذن فك الحجر' (Permission is the lifting of interdiction). Note that this is the legal meaning, supported by linguistic analysis (idhn = release, opposite of prevention).
The author refutes al-Zayla'i's interpretation that idhn means notification.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i view
The passage notes that according to al-Shafi'i, idhn is tawkil (agency) and inabah (delegation). In Hanafi, it is not; the slave acts for himself (li nafsihi).
This difference leads to different liability rules.
4
Analyze the legal effect
The passage explains: 'ثم يتصرّفُ العبد لنفسه بأهليته، فلم يرجع بالعهدة على سيده' (then the slave acts for himself by his legal capacity, so he does not have recourse on his master). This is the key practical consequence.
Recourse (al-uhda) means the right to demand payment or other obligations.
5
Apply to a scenario
Consider the hypothetical: a permitted slave buys goods on credit. Under Hanafi law, the slave is personally liable; the merchant cannot sue the master. This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).
Shafi'i would hold the master liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون
قوله : كتاب المأذون ؛ إيرادُ كتاب المأذون بعد كتاب الحجر ظاهر المناسبة، إذ الأذن يقتضي سبق الحجر ، فلما ترتبا وجوداً ترتبا أيضاً ذكراً. كذا في «نتائج الأفكار». 
قوله : المأذون ؛ قال في ردّ المحتار : أي الإذن، فهو مصدر كمَعْسُور، وإن كان الظاهر أنه صفة، ولكنّه يحتاجُ لحذف المضاف أو الصلة، في «الكِرْمَانِي»: يقال مأذون له أو لها ، وترك الصلة ليس من كلام العرب، وأقره القُهُسْتانِي، وتقدير المضاف: إذن المأذون ؛ لأنَّ البحث عن الأفعال لا عن الذوات. وفي المصباح : إنّ الفقهاء يحذفون الصلة لفهم المعنى.
قوله : الإذن فك الحجر ؛ هذا معناه الشرعي، قال صاحب (رد المحتار : قال الطوري: قال شيخ الإسلام في مبسوطه: الإذنُ هو الإطلاق لغةً ؛ لأنه ضدّ الحجر، وهو المنع، فكان إطلاقاً عن شيء إلى شيء. اهـ. وفي «النهاية»: الإذن في الشيء رفع المانع لمن هو محجور عنه، وإعلام بإطلاقه فيما حجر عليه، من أذن له في الشيء إذناً ، وأبعد الإمامُ الزَّيْلَعِي حيث قال : إنّه الإعلام، ومنه الأذان، وهو الإعلام ؛ لأنّ الإذن من أذن في كذا : إذا أباحه، والأذان من أذن بكذا : إذا أعلم ... الخ ، وفي حاشية أبي السعود: وقال قاضي زاده له في «التكملة»: لم أرقط في كتب اللغة مجيء الإذن بمعنى الإعلام.
ثم يتصرّفُ العبد لنفسه بأهليته، فلم يرجع بالعهدة على سيده
فإذا عرض له الرِّق وتعلّق به حق المولى صارَ مانعاً ، لكونه مالكاً للتصرُّف ، فإذا أسقط المولى حقه المانع عن التصرُّفِ وأزالَ حجْرَه : أي منعه عن التصرُّف فهو الإذن، هذا عندنا ، وعندَ الشَّافِعِي له هو توكيل وإنابة.
(ثم يتصرف العبد لنفسه بأهليته ، فإنَّهُ ليس بتوكيل، والوكيل هو الذي يتصرف لغيره ، فقوله : ثم يتصرفُ عطف على محذوف ، فإنَّ قولَه : الإذن فك الحجر معناه : إذا أذن المولى ينفك العبد عن الحجر، فعطف على قوله : ينفك. قولُه : ثم يتصرف. (فلم يرجع بالعهدة على سيده) ، هذا تفريع على أنَّه يتصرفُ لنفسه ، فإِنَّه إذا اشترى شيئاً لا يطلبُ الثَّمن من المولى لكونه مشترياً لنفسه، بخلاف الوكيل فإنَّهُ يطلبُ الثَّمن من الموكل ؛ لأنه اشترى للموكل.
[١] قوله : صار بالغا... الخ ؛ لأنه بعد الرق وإن بقي أهلاً للتصرف بلسانه الناطق ، وعقله المميز، لكن لَمَّا كان تصرُّفه يوجب تعلق الدين برقبته أو كسبه، وذلك حقُّ المولى، ثبت الحجرُ عنه فلا بُدَّ من إذنه ؛ لئلا يبطل حقه من غير رضاه، فقوله : وإسقاط الحجر كالتفسير لقوله : فك الحجر. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي" [٢] قوله : ينفك العبد عن الحجر ؛ فلا يتوقتُ بوقت، ولا يتخصص بنوع : أي بمكان ، قال القُهُسْتانِيُّ : وفي «التاتارخانية» هذا إذا صادف الإذنُ عبداً محجوراً، أما إذا صادف عبداً مأذوناً يتخصص ، فلو أذن له في التجارة ثم دفع إليه مالاً وقال : اشتر لي به الطعام، فاشترى العبد الرقيق يصير مشترياً لنفسه ، نص عليه محمد . كذا في «رد المحتان [٣]قوله : فلم يرجع بالعهدة ؛ أي بحق التصرُّف كطلب الثمن وغيره، والعهدة فعْلَة بمعنى مفعول من عهده لقيه. «قُهستاني». كذا في «ردّ المحتار».
The Book of the Permitted Slave. His statement: 'The Book of the Permitted Slave'; introducing the Book of the Permitted Slave after the Book of Interdiction is clearly appropriate, because permission requires prior interdiction. Since they are sequential in existence, they are also sequential in mention. Thus in 'Nata'ij al-Afkar'. His statement: 'al-Ma'dhun'; said in Radd al-Muhtar: it means permission (idhn), so it is a verbal noun like ma'sur, even though it appears to be an adjective, but it requires omission of the complement or the relative clause. In al-Karmani: it is said ma'dhun lahu or laha, and omitting the relative clause is not from the speech of the Arabs, and al-Quhustani agreed, and the implied complement is: idhn al-ma'dhun (permission of the permitted slave), because the discussion is about actions not essences. In al-Misbah: the jurists omit the relative clause for understanding the meaning. His statement: 'Permission is the lifting of interdiction and removal of a right'; this is its legal meaning. Said the author of Radd al-Muhtar: al-Turi said: Shaykh al-Islam said in his Mabsut: Permission linguistically is release because it is the opposite of interdiction, which is prevention, so it is a release from something to something. End. In al-Nihaya: Permission for something is the removal of an obstacle from one who is interdicted from it, and an announcement of his release regarding what he was prevented from; from 'adhina lahu fi shay'in idhnan'. And Imam al-Zayla'i went far when he said it is notification, from which comes adhan, which is notification, because idhn comes from 'adhina fi kadha' meaning he permitted it, and adhan from 'adhina bi kadha' meaning he informed. Etc. In the Hashiya of Abu al-Su'ud: Qadi Zada said in al-Takmila: I have not seen in any book of language that idhn means notification. Then the slave acts for himself by his legal capacity, so he does not have recourse on his master. So when slavery occurs and the master's right attaches, it becomes an obstacle, because he is the owner of the disposition. When the master drops his right that prevents disposition and removes his interdiction, i.e., his prevention from disposition, that is permission. This is with us, and with al-Shafi'i it is delegation and agency. (Then the slave acts for himself by his legal capacity, for it is not agency. The agent is one who acts for another. So his statement: 'then acts' is conjuncted to an omitted clause, for his statement: 'permission is lifting interdiction' means: when the master gives permission, the slave is freed from interdiction, so 'then acts' is conjuncted to his saying: 'is freed'. His statement: 'so he does not have recourse on his master' is a branching from the fact that he acts for himself. For if he buys something, he does not seek the price from the master because he is a buyer for himself, unlike the agent who seeks the price from the principal because he bought for the principal. [1] His statement: 'he became legally competent...' etc.; because after slavery, even though he remains legally competent for disposition by his speaking tongue and discerning intellect, but since his disposition would cause debt to attach to his person or his earnings, and that is the master's right, interdiction is established against him, so his permission is necessary so that his right is not voided without his consent. So his statement: 'and removal of interdiction' is an interpretation of his statement: 'lifting of interdiction'. Thus in 'Hashiyat al-Jalabi'. [2] His statement: 'the slave is freed from interdiction'; so it is not restricted by time, nor specified by type, i.e., place. Al-Quhustani said: In al-Tatarkhaniyya, this is when permission encounters an interdicted slave. But if it encounters an already permitted slave, it becomes specified. So if he permits him to trade then gives him money and says: buy food for me with it, and the slave buys, the slave becomes a buyer for himself, as stated by Muhammad. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar. [3] His statement: 'so he does not have recourse on the master'; i.e., with respect to right of disposition like demanding the price etc. Al-uhda is a verbal noun meaning object of encounter from 'ahidahu laqiyahu'. Quhustani. Thus in Radd al-Muhtar.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqaya begins the chapter on the Permitted Slave (al-Ma'dhun). The author first explains the placement of this chapter after the chapter on Interdiction (al-Hajr), noting the logical sequence: permission presupposes interdiction, so the order in the text mirrors the order in reality. He then clarifies the term 'al-Ma'dhun' itself. While it appears to be an adjective, it is actually a verbal noun meaning 'permission', with an implied complement—literally 'permission of the permitted slave'—because the focus is on actions, not persons. The jurists commonly omit the relative clause for brevity, as noted in al-Misbah. The core definition is given: permission (idhn) is the lifting of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) and the removal of the master's right that prevents the slave from transacting. Linguistically, idhn means release, the opposite of prevention. Some, like al-Zayla'i, equated idhn with notification (i'lam), but this is refuted by Qadi Zada, who found no lexical basis for that meaning. The legal effect of this definition is crucial: when a master gives permission, the slave acts for himself (li nafsihi) by his own legal capacity (ahliyyah). This is not agency (tawkil). An agent acts for another, so the principal bears liability. Here, the slave is the principal in his own transactions; he buys and sells for himself, and the master has no recourse on him for debts. The passage includes a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who holds that permission is a form of delegation (tawkil wa inabah). The Hanafi response is that the slave's capacity is inherent—slavery only prevents its exercise due to the master's right. Permission simply removes that obstacle, allowing the slave to act on his own behalf. Further comments clarify that the lifting of interdiction is unconditional: it is not restricted by time or place unless the slave was already permitted. A scenario from Muhammad (al-Shaybani) illustrates: if a master gives a permitted slave money to buy food, the slave buys for himself, not as an agent. The term 'al-uhda' (recourse) is explained as the right to demand payment or other obligations.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): Permission given by the master to a slave to engage in transactions, legally defined as lifting interdiction (fakk al-hajr).
المأذون (al-ma'dhun): The permitted slave; a slave who has been granted permission by his master to trade. The term itself is a verbal noun meaning 'permission'.
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Lifting of interdiction; the removal of the legal restriction that prevented the slave from transacting independently.
توكيل (tawkil): Agency; a contract whereby one person appoints another to act on his behalf, making the principal liable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view presented in this passage, what is the legal effect of a master giving permission to his slave to trade?
A) The slave becomes an agent of the master, and the master is liable for debts.
B) The slave's transactions are valid but the master must pay the price.
C) The slave acts for himself and has no recourse on the master.
D) The slave's transactions are void unless the master ratifies them.

Key Takeaway
Idhn in Hanafi fiqh is fakk al-hajr, not tawkil, so the permitted slave transacts for himself and bears personal liability.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
advanced
12 min
Nature of Permission (idhn) for a Slave to Trade
Inter-madhhab dispute on legal nature of idhn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that idhn is fakk al-hajr (removal of interdiction) rather than tawkīl (agency), and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Permission to trade removes the master's interdiction, making the slave liable for his own contracts.

This passage addresses a fundamental legal question: when a master permits his slave to engage in trade, does that permission constitute an agency (the slave acts on behalf of the master) or the removal of a legal barrier (the slave regains his natural capacity to contract for himself)? The Hanafi school, represented here, adopts the latter view. Understanding this distinction is crucial because it determines who bears the liability for debts incurred by the slave and the scope and duration of the permission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position on idhn
The passage states that idhn is fakk al-hajr: the master drops his right that prevents the slave from transacting. The slave then acts for himself by his own legal capacity (ahliyyah).
This is the core legal characterization; all subsequent rulings branch from it.
2
Trace the consequences of fakk al-hajr
Because the slave acts for himself, he alone is liable for debts (he does not 'return the liability' (al-‘uhda) to the master). Also, the permission is not time-bound (not even a stated time limits it) and is not specialized by type (permission in one trade covers all trades).
These consequences are derived by qiyas to other forms of isqāt (dropping of rights) like divorce and manumission.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i considers idhn to be tawkīl (agency), meaning the slave acts on behalf of the master, so the master is liable. The passage rebuts this by noting that agency is time-bound and specialized, whereas idhn in the Hanafi view is not.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is in who bears the debt and the scope of the permission.
4
Distinguish idhn from specific command (isti'māl)
The passage clarifies that a specific order (e.g., 'buy this specific item') is isti'māl, not idhn. Such an order does not grant the slave general trading capacity; it merely directs a single act. If it were treated as idhn, every minor directive would open the door to unlimited liability.
This boundary prevents abuse and preserves the distinction between a servant following orders and a permitted trader.

(1) قوله : فإنّ الإسقاط لا يتوقت ؛ كالطلاق والعتاق وتأجيل الدين وتأخير المطالبة إذ الاسقاطات يتلاشى. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» [٢]قوله : فإن أذن في نوع ؛ سواء سكت عن غيره أو نهى بطريق الصريح نحو أن يأذن في شراء البرّ، وقال: لا تشتر غيره. كذا في رد المحتار). Book of the Permitted Slave: Its Rulings. Then the slave acts for himself by his own capacity, so he does not return the liability upon his master. When slavery occurs and the master's right attaches, it becomes a preventer, because he (the master) is the owner of the right to dispose. So when the master drops his right that prevents disposition and removes his interdiction — i.e., his prohibition from disposing — then that is the permission (idhn). This is our position. According to al-Shafi'i, it is a delegation (tawkīl) and appointment as agent (ināba). (Then the slave acts for himself by his own capacity, for it is not a delegation. The agent is one who acts for another. So his statement 'then he acts' is a conjunction to an omitted phrase, for his statement 'permission is the removal of interdiction' means: when the master permits, the slave is released from interdiction. So (then he acts) is conjoined to 'is released'. (So he does not return the liability upon his master) is a branching on that he acts for himself. For if he buys something, he does not demand the price from the master because he is a buyer for himself, unlike the agent who demands the price from the principal because he bought for the principal. (And it is not time-bound) is a branching on that it is a dropping of a right, not a delegation. For dropping is not time-bound, and delegation is time-bound. (Thus a slave permitted for a day is permitted until he is interdicted, and it is not specialized by type. For if he permits in a type, his permission covers all types. This is a branching on that it is removal of interdiction, not delegation. For removal of interdiction is release from a restriction, so it is not specialized by a transaction. There is a disagreement with al-Shafi'i. The meaning is that if he permits in a type of trade, his permission covers all types. Similarly, if he says: sit as a dyer, then it is permission to buy what is necessary for this work, so it covers. Similarly, if he says: bring me the rent every month such-and-such. Contrary to if he is permitted to buy a specific thing, for that is a specific command (isti'māl), not permission. (1) His statement: 'for dropping is not time-bound' — like divorce, manumission, postponement of debt, and delaying (the demand). For droppings are effaced. (2) His statement: 'for if he permits in a type' — whether he remains silent about other types or prohibits explicitly, like if he permits buying wheat and says: do not buy other than it. (3) His statement: if he permits in a type of trade — like wheat for example. Yes, his permission in bread, dyeing and other types of crafts. (4) His statement: 'contrary to if he permits...' etc. It cannot be said: this contradicts his statement later: 'but if he restricts, according to us it covers all trades because the first (restriction) explicitly restricts and does not benefit permission, while the second (general permission) benefits it.' For we say: the restricted (cases) are two types: 1. A type that cannot be permission: it is the command for a personal transaction, like the command to sell a specific item of his own from foods, drinks, garments, etc. This is specific command (isti'māl). For if this were made permission, the door of specific command would be closed, because it would lead to: whoever is commanded to buy a load for two fils would be a permitted slave whose acknowledgment of debts that consume his value is valid and he is immediately held liable, so no one would venture that.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school on the nature of permission (idhn) given by a master to his slave to engage in trade. It is an inter-madhhab dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: idhn is the removal of interdiction (fakk al-hajr). When a master permits his slave, he drops his right that prevented the slave from transacting. Consequently, the slave acts for himself, not as an agent for the master. Therefore, if the slave buys something, the seller cannot demand the price from the master; the slave is personally liable. This differs from agency (wakāla), where the agent acts for the principal and the principal bears liability. The author then elaborates that idhn is not time-bound because it is a dropping of a right, analogous to divorce or manumission, which are not limited by time. A slave permitted for a day remains permitted until the master revokes the permission. Similarly, idhn is not specialized by type of trade: permission in one type (e.g., trading in wheat) covers all types of trade. However, if the master gives a specific command (isti'māl) like 'buy this specific item,' that is not idhn but a personal directive, and the slave acts as a servant, not a permitted trader. The Shafi'i school holds that idhn is a form of agency (tawkīl), leading to different rulings on liability and scope. The Hanafi response relies on the nature of idhn as a removal of an existing impediment (the master's right), not a creation of a new capacity. The practical consequence (thamara) is that in the Hanafi school, the permitted slave's contracts are for his own account, and he is solely responsible for the obligations, whereas according to Shafi'i, the master would be liable. The text concludes by distinguishing between general permission and specific commands: a specific command does not constitute idhn because it would open the door to abuse, as every minor instruction would make the slave a permitted trader with full capacity to incur debt.

Key Terms
الإذن (al-idhn): The master's permission to the slave to engage in trade; legally, it is the removal of interdiction (fakk al-hajr) enabling the slave to transact for himself.
العهدة (al-‘uhda): The liability or responsibility for a debt or obligation arising from a contract. The slave does not return the 'uhda to the master.
الاستخدام (al-isti‘māl): A specific command from the master to the slave to perform a particular transaction (e.g., buy this specific item). This is not idhn; it is a personal directive that does not make the slave a permitted trader.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The cloth trade is invalid because it violates the restriction.
B) The cloth trade is valid and the slave is liable; the permission covers all trades.
C) The cloth trade is valid but the master is liable because he did not revoke permission.
D) The cloth trade is invalid and the slave is not liable.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a master's permission to his slave to trade is the removal of interdiction (fakk al-hajr), not agency, so the slave contracts for himself and bears his own liability.

Permission by Indication and the Scope of the Ma'dhun's Trade
Explaining how a slave becomes ma'dhun through the master's silent consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how a slave becomes ma'dhun by indication (idhn dalalah) and identify the types of transactions a ma'dhun may perform, including the internal Hanafi dispute on excessive loss.

Core Idea
Silence can be permission: a ma'dhun's authority is not a delegation but an abandonment of the master's right to restrict.

This lesson examines how a slave's permission to trade can be established implicitly through the master's silence. The Hanafi school, unlike al-Shafi'i and Zufar, recognizes 'idhn dalalah' to prevent deception. The passage also outlines the broad scope of a ma'dhun's trade, including selling even at a loss, and notes disagreements among the Hanafi masters.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on ma'dhun by silence
Identify that the Hanafi school holds that a slave becomes ma'dhun when his master sees him trading and remains silent, establishing permission by indication (idhn dalalah). This is based on preventing deception (daf'an lil-ghurar) due to the common practice of slaves purchasing small items.
This is the ruling from the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing school and their objection
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that permission is a form of deputyship (tawkil) requiring explicit verbal specification. Without it, the slave cannot be considered ma'dhun.
The opposing school requires sarih permission.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response distinguishing permission from agency
The Hanafi response emphasizes that permission is an abandonment of the master's right to restrict (isqat al-haqq wa fakk al-hajr), not a delegation of authority. Thus the slave's ownership becomes effective, and his authority is not limited to specific types of goods, unlike a wakil.
This distinction explains why the scope is broader for the ma'dhun.
4
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In Hanafi law, a slave can become ma'dhun by mere silence, while in Shafi'i law explicit permission is required. This affects the validity of the slave's transactions—e.g., if a master sees his slave trading and objects later, the transaction is still valid in Hanafi but void in Shafi'i.
Also note the internal dispute on ghabn fahish and muzara'ah.
5
Scope of transactions and intra-Hanafi disputes
With explicit permission (idhn sarih), the ma'dhun can engage in all trade by consensus. With implicit permission, he can also engage in all trades, but there is dispute on whether selling at excessive loss is valid (Abu Hanifa allows; Sahibayn disallow). Similarly, muzara'ah is fasid according to Abu Hanifa but valid according to Sahibayn, and fatwa is given by Sahibayn.
This shows the layered nature of Hanafi rulings.

كتاب المأذون أحكامه
ويثبت : دلالة، فعبد راه سيده يبيع ويشترى وسكت مأذون ( ويثبت : دلالة، فعبد راه سيده يبيع ويشترى وسكت مأذون ) ، هذا عندنا خلافاً لزفر والشافعي (١) ، وإنما يكون مأذونا دفعاً للغرور استخدام عبده فيما اشتدّ إليه حاجته ؛ لأنَّ غالب استعمال العبد في شراء الأشياء الحقيرة.
وقسم يجب أن يكون إذناً ، وهو الأمر بالتصرُّفِ النوعي : كالأمر ببيع نوع معين من أ، أنواع الأعيان المذكورة، وهذا الفرق الذي مداره النوعية والشخصية هو المفهوم من كلام صاحب «الهداية». صرح به الأكمل. كذا في «حاشية جلبي).

(٢) قوله : وسكت مأذون ؛ قال في «الهداية (4) : لا فرق بين أن يكون عيناً مملوكاً للمولى أو لأجنبي. [٣] قوله : خلافاً لزفر والشافعي الله لهما : إنّ الإذن توكيل وإنابة من المولى ؛ لأنه يستفيد الولاية من جهته، ويثبت الحكم وهو الملك له دون العبد ؛ ؛ ولهذا يملك حجره، فيتخصص بما خصه كالمضارب. ولنا: إنه إسقاط الحق، وفك الحجر على ما مرّ، وعند ذلك تظهر ملكية العبد ، فلا يتخصص بنوع دون نوع، بخلاف الوكيل ؛ لأنه يتصرف في مال غيره، فتثبت له الولاية من جهته، وحكم التصرف وهو الملك واقع للعبد ، حتى كان له أن يصرفه إلى قضاء الدين والنفقة، وما استغنى عنه يخلفه المالك فيه. كذا في «الهداية»).

(٢) قوله : لأنه تبرّع ؛ أي بمنزلة التبرع ؛ لأنّ البيع بالغبن الفاحش خلاف المقصود، إذ المقصود بالبيع الاسترباح دون الإتلاف، فكان بمنزلة التبرع ؛ ولهذا اعتبر من المريض من الثلث، وما هو خلاف المقصود لا ينتظمه الإذن بالمقصود. كذا في «العناية»(۲). [٤] قوله : ويوكل بهما ؛ أي بالبيع والشراء ؛ لأنه قد لا يتفرغ بنفسه، فجازَ الاستعانة بغيره. [0]قوله : ويرهن ويرتهن ؛ لأنهما من توابع التجارة، فإنهما إيفاء واستيفاء. [٦] قوله مزارعة ؛ وهي . عقد على الزرع ببعض الخارج من الأرض، وهي فاسدة عند الإمام الله ، وعندهما : جائزة، وبقولهما يفتى لتعامل الناس.
(1) ينظر: «ذخيرة العقبى (ص٥٤٧). (۲) «العناية» (۹ : ۲۸۷). (۳) ((الهداية (٤ : ٤ ) .
Chapter of the Ma'dhun: Its Rulings. And it is established: by indication, so a slave whom his master sees selling and buying and remains silent is [considered] ma'dhun. This is according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. He is only considered ma'dhun to prevent deception, due to the master's need for his slave's service in what he urgently needs, because the common use of a slave is in purchasing low-value items. And there is a type that must be explicit permission: that is the command for specific transactions, like the command to sell a specific type from the mentioned types of goods. This distinction, which revolves around type and individual, is understood from the words of the author of Al-Hidayah. Al-Akmal explicitly stated it. Likewise in Hashiyat Chalabi. [1] His statement: 'And it is established by indication' means permission is established by indication. In Al-Haqaiq: The master's silence is only considered permission if nothing preceded that negates permission during the silence, such as if he said: 'If you see my slave trading and I remain silent, then he has no permission to trade' — then later he sees him trading and remains silent, he does not become ma'dhun by consensus. Similarly in Radd al-Muhtar. (2) His statement: 'and remains silent' — he is ma'dhun. He said in Al-Hidayah: There is no difference whether the item is owned by the master or by a third party. [3] His statement: 'contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i' — they said: Permission is delegation and deputyship from the master, because the slave benefits from authority from his side, and the ruling (i.e., ownership) is established for the master, not for the slave; therefore, the master retains the right to restrict him, so it is limited to what he specified, like a mudarib. Our proof: It is an abandonment of the right and removal of restriction as previously mentioned; upon that, the slave's ownership becomes apparent, so it is not limited to one type over another, unlike the agent (wakil) because he acts in the property of another, so authority is established for him from that side, and the ruling of the transaction (i.e., ownership) occurs for the slave, so that he may use it to pay debts and maintenance, and what remains is succeeded by the owner. Likewise in Al-Hidayah. (References...) And explicitly: if he permitted absolutely, every trade of his is valid by consensus. So he may sell and buy, even with excessive loss, and he may appoint agents for them, and pawn, and take as pawn, and accept land on lease and take it on sharecropping. And explicitly: if he permitted absolutely, every trade of his is valid by consensus. For specifying something in the narrations, if it indicates negation of the ruling from what is other than it, then generalization of trade by consensus is specific to when he is absolute. But if he restricted it, then according to us it generalizes all trades, contrary to al-Shafi'i. (He may sell and buy, even with excessive loss) — but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not valid with excessive loss because it is gratuitous. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof: It is from the category of trade. (And he may appoint agents for them, and pawn, and take as pawn, and accept land on lease) meaning he takes it on lease and sharecropping (and takes it on sharecropping). [1] His statement: 'explicitly': by saying to him: 'I permit you to trade', and 'trade' is a general name encompassing all types, so he may sell and buy whatever types of goods occur to him because it is the essence of trade. This is by consensus, unlike permission by indication, which has the disagreement of Zufar and al-Shafi'i, as has passed. (2) His statement: 'because it is gratuitous': meaning it is like a gift; because selling at an excessive loss is contrary to the purpose, since the purpose of selling is profit, not loss; so it is like a gift; for this reason, it is considered from the third of the sick person's wealth; and what is contrary to the purpose is not included in permission given for the purpose. Likewise in Al-Inayah. [3] His statement: 'and his proof: it is from the category of trade' — and the slave acts with his own legal capacity, so he becomes like a free person, and likewise the permitted minor. Likewise in Al-Hidayah. [4] His statement: 'and he may appoint agents for them' meaning for sale and purchase; because he may not be able to attend personally, so it is permissible to seek help from others. [5] His statement: 'and pawn and take as pawn' because they are subsidiaries of trade, as they are fulfillment and receipt. [6] His statement: 'sharecropping'; it is a contract on cultivation for a portion of the produce from the land. It is void (fasid) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, but according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is permissible, and fatwa is given according to their view due to people's practice.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the establishment of a slave's permission to trade (idhn li al-tijarah) by indication (dalalah). The Hanafi school holds that if a master sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, this constitutes implicit permission, making the slave a ma'dhun. The rationale is to prevent deception (ghurur), as slaves are commonly employed to purchase low-value items, and restricting them to explicit permission would cause hardship. Zufar and al-Shafi'i object, arguing that permission is a form of deputyship (tawkil) requiring explicit authorization, similar to an agent (wakil). The Hanafi response, as presented in Al-Hidayah, distinguishes permission from deputyship: permission is an abandonment of the master's right to restrict (isqat al-haqq wa fakk al-hajr), not a delegation of authority. Therefore, the slave's ownership becomes effective, and his trading authority is not limited to specific types of goods. The passage then discusses explicit permission (sarih), which by consensus covers all trades, and contrasts it with implicit permission, which is disputed. Within the Hanafi school, there is an intra-madhhab dispute regarding selling with excessive loss (ghabn fahish): Imam Abu Hanifa allows it as it falls under trade, but Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) consider it invalid because it resembles a gratuitous transfer (tabarru'). The passage also covers the slave's authority to appoint agents, engage in pawn transactions, and accept land for sharecropping (muzara'ah), noting that the Sahibayn consider muzara'ah valid while Imam Abu Hanifa deems it void, but fatwa is given according to their view due to customary practice.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been granted permission by his master to engage in trade.
إذن دلالة (idhn dalalah): Permission established by implication, such as the master's silent acquiescence to the slave's trading.
غبن فاحش (ghabn fahish): Excessive loss or discrepancy in price in a sale, such that it is considered outside normal market practice.
إسقاط الحق (isqat al-haqq): Abandonment of a right; here the master's right to restrict the slave's trade.
فك الحجر (fakk al-hajr): Removal of restriction; the master lifts the legal constraint on the slave's trading ability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how does a slave become ma'dhun if the master sees him trading and remains silent?
A) He does not become ma'dhun; explicit permission is required.
B) He becomes ma'dhun by indication (dalalah) to prevent deception.
C) He becomes ma'dhun only if the master verbally says, 'I permit you.'
D) He becomes ma'dhun only for the specific items the master saw him trade.

Key Takeaway
Silence can constitute permission in Hanafi fiqh to prevent deception, and the ma'dhun's authority is broader than that of a wakil, covering all trade unless explicitly restricted.

Scope of Absolute Permission for the Authorized Slave
Defines the scope of absolute permission for a slave to trade by consensus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of absolute permission for an authorized slave and explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement on sale with gross loss.

Core Idea
Absolute permission makes all trades valid by consensus; but gross loss and sharecropping reveal nuanced ikhtilaf.

The authorized slave (ma'dhūn) is a key figure in Hanafi commercial law. This lesson examines the scope of absolute permission (idhn mutlaq) from the master, establishing that it covers all trade transactions by consensus. However, when transactions involve gross loss (ghaban fahish) or sharecropping (muzara'ah), the Hanafi school has internal disagreements that affect validity. We'll trace the proofs and practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
Read the opening statement: absolute permission renders 'every trade' valid by consensus. Identify what 'every trade' includes and the condition of unrestricted permission.
The consensus (ijma') is among Hanafis, not all schools.
2
Locate the dalil
The text cites consensus (إجماعاً) as proof. Note that this is a qat'i dalil in Hanafi usul.
Consensus is a primary source of law.
3
Analyze the dispute on ghaban fahish
Abu Hanifa holds that a sale with gross loss is valid because it falls under trade; Abu Yusuf and Muhammad consider it a donation (tabarru'), thus invalid. Compare their proofs.
The same difference applies to an authorized minor (sabi ma'dhun).
4
Examine additional transactions
The passage lists other acts: agency, pledge, leasing, sharecropping. Note that for sharecropping, the Imam holds it invalid while the two consider it valid; fatwa is given by their view.
Fatwa follows the Sahibayn due to customary practice (ta'amul al-nas).
5
Note cross-madhhab difference
If permission is restricted (muqayyad), Hanafis hold it covers all trades, contrary to al-Shafi'i who restricts to the specified trade.
This shows Hanafi reliance on general words and usul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصريحاً، فلو أذن مطلقاً صح كل تجارة منه إجماعاً، فإنَّ تخصيص الشيء بالذكر في الرواياتِ إِن دل على نفي الحكم عما عداه فتعميم التجارة إجماعاً يختص بما إذا أطلق، أما إذا قيدَ فعندنا يَعُمُ التَّجَارَاتِ خلافاً للشافعي. (فيبيع ويشتري، ولو بغبن فاحش)، ولا يصح عندهما بغبن فاحش؛ لأنَّه تبرع. وله: إِنَّهُ من باب التّجارة، والعبدُ متصرّف بأهلية نفسه، فصار كالحرّ، وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبي المأذون. (ويوكل بهما، ويرهن، ويرتهن ويتقبل الأرض) أي يأخذها قبالة بالاستئجار والمساقاة ويأخذها مزارعة. وهي عقد على الزرع ببعض الخارج من الأرض، وهي فاسدة عند الإمام، وعندهما: جائزة، وبقولهما يفتى لتعامل الناس.
And expressly: if he gives absolute permission, every trade from him is valid by consensus. For specifying something by mention in narrations, if it indicates negation of the ruling from what is other than it, then the generalization of trade by consensus is specific to when he is unrestricted. But if restricted, then with us it includes all trades, contrary to al-Shafi'i. So he sells and buys, even with excessive loss, but it is not valid according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) with excessive loss, because it is a donation. And his (Abu Hanifa's) proof: it is from the category of trade, and the slave acts with his own capacity, so he is like a free person. On this difference is the authorized minor. And he appoints agents for them, and gives and takes pledge, and accepts land, i.e., takes it by lease and sharecropping, and takes it for sharecropping. And it is a contract for cultivation with a portion of the output from the land, and it is invalid according to the Imam, but according to them it is valid, and by their saying fatwa is given due to people's practice.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage discusses the scope of absolute permission (idhn mutlaq) for a slave (ma'dhūn) to engage in commerce. The text opens by stating that with absolute permission, every type of trade is valid by consensus (ijma'), but this is only when the master gives unrestricted permission. If the permission is restricted (e.g., to a specific trade), then there is a difference: the Hanafis hold that general words still include all trades, while al-Shafi'i disagrees. The author then lists specific transactions. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding sale with gross loss (ghaban fahish). Abu Hanifa permits it because it falls under trade, while the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) consider it invalid as it is akin to a donation. The same difference applies to an authorized minor. Further, the slave may appoint agents, give and take pledges, and accept land on lease or sharecropping. The sharecropping contract (muzara'ah) is considered invalid by Abu Hanifa but valid by his two companions, and fatwa is given according to their view due to prevalent practice.

Key Terms
المأذون (al-ma'dhūn): A slave authorized by his master to engage in trade with general or specific permission.
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghaban al-fāḥish): Excessive loss in a sale where the price is far below market value.
الإذن المطلق (al-idhn al-muṭlaq): Unrestricted permission given to a slave to engage in all types of trade.
المزارعة (al-muzāra'ah): A contract of sharecropping where the landowner gives land to another to cultivate in exchange for a portion of the crop.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the consensus mentioned in the passage, what is the effect of absolute permission (idhn mutlaq) for a slave?
A) Only profit-making sales are valid.
B) Every type of trade is valid by consensus.
C) Only transactions without gross loss are valid.
D) The slave can only trade in specified goods.

Key Takeaway
Absolute permission for an authorized slave makes all trades valid by Hanafi consensus, but gross loss and sharecropping involve valid intra-school disagreements.

Permissible Transactions for a Ma'dhun Slave
Lists permissive rulings for a slave permitted to trade

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the range of permissible transactions for a ma'dhun slave and explain why each is considered part of trade.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun's permission covers all acts necessary for trade, including partnership, hiring, and acknowledging debts.

This lesson explores the core commercial acts a ma'dhun may perform. The passage lists these acts and provides reasoning that ties each to the purpose of trade. Understanding this scope is essential to grasp the legal personality of the ma'dhun as an agent of his master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the list of permissible acts
Read the initial section and enumerate the nine acts: buying seeds, entering shirkat 'inan, mudaraba, hiring property, hiring himself, acknowledging deposits/loans, giving small food gifts, hosting, reducing price for defect.
2
Classify each act as a trade necessity
For each act, note the commentary's reason: it either generates profit (buying seeds, partnerships, hiring) or facilitates trade (acknowledgments, gifts to attract customers, price reduction as custom).
3
Distinguish between 'inan and mufawada
The commentary explains that shirkat 'inan is allowed because it is based on agency, while mufawada requires suretyship which is outside permission. Understand that the ma'dhun can only act as agent, not as surety.
4
Apply the rationale for hiring himself
The Hanafi view is that his self is capital; hiring himself does not restrict his ability to trade, unlike selling or pawning. This contrasts with Shafi'i.
This step touches on an inter-madhhab difference that will be covered in Lesson 2.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكامه
ويشتري بذراً يزرعُه، ويشارك عناناً ويدفع المال ويأخذه مضاربة، ويستأجر ويؤجر نفسه. ويقر بوديعة وغصب ودين ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً، ويضيف مَن يطعمه، ويحط من الثمن بعيب قدراً عهدا. ولا يتزوج ولا يزوج رقيقه.
أما شرح المشاركة: إنما قال عناناً احترازاً عن المفاوضة. وشركة العنان هي شركة في كل تجارة أو في نوع من أنواع التجارة، وتصح ببعض مال أحد الشريكين، ومع فضل مال أحدهما، ومع تساوي ماليهما، مع تفاوت الربح بينهما ومع تفاوت ماليهما بتساوي الربح بينهما، ومع كون أحد المالين دراهم والآخر دنانير، والخلط فيه ليس بشرط. وشركة المفاوضة هي شركة متساويين من جهة المال، ومن جهة الحرية، ومن جهة الدين، وتتضمن الكفالة والوكالة. فله أن يشارك شركة العنان، وليس له أن يشارك شركة المفاوضة لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعل ذلك كانت عناناً لأن في المفاوضة عناناً وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يملك المأذون، وهو الوكالة.
وقوله ويؤجر نفسه هذا عندنا خلافاً للشافعي. وهو يقول: إن العبد لا يملك العقد على نفسه: كبيع نفسه وهبتها ورهنها، فكذا على منافعه لأنها تابعة لها. ولنا: إن نفسه رأس ماله، فيملك التصرف فيها، إلا إذا كان يتضمن إبطال الإذن كالبيع لأنه يحجر به، والرهن لأنه يحبس به، فلا يحصل مقصود المولى، أما الإجارة لا يحجر به، ويحصل به المقصود، وهو الربح ويملك.
وقوله ويقر بوديعة وغصب ودين لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد.
وقوله ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً: فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز إهداء غير المأكولات أصلاً، والإهداء اليسير راجع إلى الضيافة اليسيرة، وهي معتبرة بمال تجارته. والقياس أن لا يصح شيء من ذلك لأنه تبرع، لكن تركناه في اليسير لأنه من ضرورات التجارة استجلاباً لقلوب المعاملين.
وقوله ويحط من الثمن بعيب قدراً عهداً: أي مثلاً ما يحط التجار لأنه من صنيعهم، إذ قد يكون أخذ المعيب به من الحط، بخلاف الحط من غير عيب، والحط أكثر من العادة لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام القصد، وليس من صنيع التجار فلا ضرورة إليه.
وقوله ولا يزوج رقيقه: أي لا يكفي في تزوجه الإذن المفهوم من الإذن العام للتجارة، بل يجب أن يأذن له قصداً على حدة. وعند أبي يوسف له يزوج الأمة لأنه تحصيل المال، لهما إنه ليس من التجارة.
The Book of the Permitted Slave: Its Rulings. And he buys seeds to plant, and enters into a limited partnership (shirkat 'inan), and gives and takes capital for mudaraba, and hires (something) and hires himself out. And he acknowledges deposit, usurpation, and debt, and gives a small gift of food, and hosts someone he feeds, and reduces the price for a defect by an amount customary. And he does not marry nor marry his slaves. As for the explanation of partnership: He only said 'inan to exclude mufawada. Shirkat 'inan is a partnership in every trade or in a type of trade, and it is valid with part of the capital of one partner, with excess capital of one, with equality of their capital, with difference in profit between them, with difference in capital with equality of profit, and with one capital being dirhams and the other dinars, and mixing is not a condition. Shirkat mufawada is a partnership of equals in capital, freedom, and religion, and includes suretyship and agency. So he may enter into shirkat 'inan, but not shirkat mufawada because it is based on agency and suretyship, and suretyship is not included in the permission; if he does so, it is 'inan because mufawada contains 'inan and more, so it is valid to the extent the ma'dhun owns, which is agency. And his statement 'and hires himself out' is according to us, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He says the slave does not own the contract on himself: like selling himself, gifting him, pawning him, so too on his benefits because they are subsidiary. For us: his self is his capital, so he owns disposal over it, except if it entails invalidating the permission like sale because it restricts, and pawn because it detains, so the master's purpose is not achieved; as for hiring, it does not restrict and achieves the purpose, which is profit and ownership. And his statement 'and acknowledges deposit, usurpation, and debt' because acknowledgment is a consequence of trade; for if his acknowledgment were not valid, no one would deal with him. And his statement 'and gives a small gift of food': it indicates that gifting non-foods is not permissible at all, and small gift refers to modest hospitality, which is measured by the capital of his trade. The analogy is that none of this should be valid because it is gratuitous, but we left analogy in small amounts because it is a necessity of trade to attract the hearts of those dealing with the ma'dhun. And his statement 'and reduces the price for a defect by an amount customary': i.e., for example, what merchants reduce because it is their practice, since taking the defective item with reduction is allowed, unlike reduction without defect, and reduction more than customary is pure gratuity after completion of the purpose, not the practice of merchants, so there is no necessity. And his statement 'and does not marry his slaves': i.e., the permission understood from the general permission for trade is not sufficient for his marriage, rather he must intend permission separately. According to Abu Yusuf, he may marry the slave-girl because it is acquisition of wealth; for the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it is not from trade.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the Book of the Ma'dhun (a slave permitted by his master to trade). It lists the permissible and impermissible acts for such a slave. The commentary expands each ruling with legal reasoning and occasional intra- and inter-madhhab disputes. The passage is from Sharh al-Wiqaya with supercommentary 'Umdat al-Ri'aya. The main rulings: The ma'dhun may buy seeds, enter into shirkat 'inan (a form of partnership where partners contribute capital in a specific trade), give and take mudaraba capital, hire property or persons, and hire himself out. He may also acknowledge debts and liabilities, give small gifts of food, reduce the price for a defect as customary. He may not marry or arrange marriage for his slaves. The commentary clarifies that 'inan partnership is permissible but 'inan contains elements of agency and suretyship; since the ma'dhun has no authority for suretyship, if he enters into mufawada it is treated as 'inan. The Shafi'i view that a slave cannot hire himself out is rejected because the slave's self is his capital and hiring does not invalidate the master's permission. Acknowledgment is necessary for trade dealings. Gift of food is an exception to the rule against gifts because it attracts customers. Price reduction for defect is allowed if customary, as it is a trade practice. Marriage is not trade, so separate permission is needed; Abu Yusuf's view that a slave may marry a slave-girl is overruled because marriage is not a trade transaction.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given general permission by his master to engage in trade.
شركة عنان (shirkat 'inan): A partnership in a specific trade or type of trade where partners contribute capital, may be unequal, and profit may be shared unevenly; mixing is not required.
مفاوضة (mufawada): A partnership of equals in capital, freedom, and religion, which includes mutual agency and suretyship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because his self is his capital and hiring does not invalidate the master's permission.
B) Because all slaves have the right to contract.
C) Because the master's permission explicitly includes hiring.
D) Because hiring is a form of sale.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun's permissible acts are bounded by trade necessity; any act that does not serve trade or invalidates permission requires separate explicit authorization.

Prohibitions and Disputes: Marriage, Gifts, and the Scope of Trade
Lists permissive rulings for a slave permitted to trade

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the acts a ma'dhun may not perform without separate permission and evaluate the differences between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf on marrying a slave-girl.

Core Idea
Marriage and non-customary gratuitous transfers are outside the scope of general trade permission and require explicit authorization.

While the ma'dhun enjoys wide latitude in trade, certain acts like marriage and excessive gifts are not included in general permission. This lesson examines these boundaries and the scholarly disputes surrounding them, including the Shafi'i view on hiring oneself and the intra-Hanafi disagreement on marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify prohibited acts
From the passage, identify the acts the ma'dhun may not do: marriage for himself and his slaves, and by implication, non-customary gifts and price reductions beyond customary amount.
2
Analyze the rationale for prohibition
For each prohibited act, note the reason: marriage is not trade; excessive gifts are pure gratuity not necessary for trade. Understand the principle that only trade necessity justifies departure from the general rule against gratuitous acts.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage
According to Abu Yusuf, a ma'dhun may marry a slave-girl because it acquires wealth (the offspring). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad disagree. Identify the basis of each view and the mufta bihi.
4
Understand the Shafi'i objection to hiring oneself
The Shafi'i school holds that a slave cannot contract over his own person, so hiring himself out is invalid. The Hanafi response argues that his self is capital and hiring does not compromise the master's permission.
This dispute shows how differing views on slave-ownership affect commercial capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المأذون أحكامه
ويشتري بذراً يزرعُه، ويشارك عناناً ويدفع المال ويأخذه مضاربة، ويستأجر ويؤجر نفسه. ويقر بوديعة وغصب ودين ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً، ويضيف مَن يطعمه، ويحط من الثمن بعيب قدراً عهدا. ولا يتزوج ولا يزوج رقيقه.
أما شرح المشاركة: إنما قال عناناً احترازاً عن المفاوضة. وشركة العنان هي شركة في كل تجارة أو في نوع من أنواع التجارة، وتصح ببعض مال أحد الشريكين، ومع فضل مال أحدهما، ومع تساوي ماليهما، مع تفاوت الربح بينهما ومع تفاوت ماليهما بتساوي الربح بينهما، ومع كون أحد المالين دراهم والآخر دنانير، والخلط فيه ليس بشرط. وشركة المفاوضة هي شركة متساويين من جهة المال، ومن جهة الحرية، ومن جهة الدين، وتتضمن الكفالة والوكالة. فله أن يشارك شركة العنان، وليس له أن يشارك شركة المفاوضة لأنها تنعقد على الوكالة والكفالة، والكفالة لا تدخل تحت الإذن، فلو فعل ذلك كانت عناناً لأن في المفاوضة عناناً وزيادة، فصحت بقدر ما يملك المأذون، وهو الوكالة.
وقوله ويؤجر نفسه هذا عندنا خلافاً للشافعي. وهو يقول: إن العبد لا يملك العقد على نفسه: كبيع نفسه وهبتها ورهنها، فكذا على منافعه لأنها تابعة لها. ولنا: إن نفسه رأس ماله، فيملك التصرف فيها، إلا إذا كان يتضمن إبطال الإذن كالبيع لأنه يحجر به، والرهن لأنه يحبس به، فلا يحصل مقصود المولى، أما الإجارة لا يحجر به، ويحصل به المقصود، وهو الربح ويملك.
وقوله ويقر بوديعة وغصب ودين لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد.
وقوله ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً: فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز إهداء غير المأكولات أصلاً، والإهداء اليسير راجع إلى الضيافة اليسيرة، وهي معتبرة بمال تجارته. والقياس أن لا يصح شيء من ذلك لأنه تبرع، لكن تركناه في اليسير لأنه من ضرورات التجارة استجلاباً لقلوب المعاملين.
وقوله ويحط من الثمن بعيب قدراً عهداً: أي مثلاً ما يحط التجار لأنه من صنيعهم، إذ قد يكون أخذ المعيب به من الحط، بخلاف الحط من غير عيب، والحط أكثر من العادة لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام القصد، وليس من صنيع التجار فلا ضرورة إليه.
وقوله ولا يزوج رقيقه: أي لا يكفي في تزوجه الإذن المفهوم من الإذن العام للتجارة، بل يجب أن يأذن له قصداً على حدة. وعند أبي يوسف له يزوج الأمة لأنه تحصيل المال، لهما إنه ليس من التجارة.

Permissible Gratuitous Acts of the Ma'dhun
Commentary listing permissible gratuitous acts for the authorized slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the permissible gratuitous acts for a ma'dhun and explain the Hanafi rationale of istihsan based on trade necessity.

Core Idea
Small gifts and customary reductions are exceptions to the ban on gratuitous acts for the ma'dhun.

In this lesson, we examine the specific gratuitous acts that a ma'dhun (a slave authorized to trade) may perform according to the Hanafi school. The general rule prohibits such acts as charity, but the school makes exceptions for small items and customary practices that facilitate commerce. The passage explains the rationale of istihsan (juristic preference) over qiyas (analogy).

Guided Path
1
List the permissible acts
Identify the four acts mentioned: acknowledgment (iqrar) regarding trust, usurpation, and debt; gifting a small amount of food; hosting someone with food; and reducing the price for a defect as is customary.
These are exceptions to the general prohibition of tabarru' (gratuitous acts) for the ma'dhun.
2
Understand the conditions
Note the conditions for each act: the gift must be of food and small relative to trade capital; hospitality is treated similarly; reduction must be for a defect and follow customary practice.
The text emphasizes that non-foods are not permitted for gifting.
3
Analyze the proof
Recognize that the qiyas (analogy) would forbid all gratuitous acts because they are pure charity (tabarru'). However, the school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) to permit small amounts and customary reductions due to trade necessity (darurat al-tijarah).
The key phrase: 'لكن تركناه في اليسير؛ لأنه من ضرورات التجارة'.
4
Apply the rationale
Consider how the permissibility varies: what is 'small' depends on the trade capital; what is 'customary' depends on merchant practice. The underlying illa (effective cause) is the need to attract customers and facilitate trade.
This is a classic example of istihsan in mu'amalat.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقر بوديعة وغصب ودين ويهدي طعاماً يسيراً، ويضيف مَن يطعمه، ويحط من الثمن بعيب قدراً عهدا.
قوله : ويقر بوديعة ... الخ؛ لأن الإقرار من توابع التجارة؛ لأنه لو لم يصح إقراره لم يعامله أحد. كذا في «الزيلعي».
قوله : طعاماً يسيراً؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يجوز إهداء غير المأكولات أصلاً، والإهداء اليسير راجع إلى الضيافة اليسيرة، وهي معتبرة بمال تجارته، فقيل: إن كان مال تجارته مثلاً عشرة آلاف درهم، واتخذ ضيافة بمقدار عشرة، كان يسيراً، وإن كان مال تجارته عشرة مثلاً فاتخذ ضيافة بمقدار دانق فذاك يكون كثيراً عرفاً، والهدية بالمأكول كالضيافة به. والقياس أن لا يصح شيء من ذلك؛ لأنه تبرع، لكن تركناه في اليسير؛ لأنه من ضرورات التجارة استجلاباً لقلوب المعاملين مع المأذون له في التجارة. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي» ناقلاً عن «البيانية».
قوله : قدراً عهدا؛ أي مثلاً ما يحط التجار؛ لأنه من صنيعهم، إذ قد يكون أخذ المعيب به من الحط، بخلاف الحط من غير عيب، والحط أكثر من العادة؛ لأنه تبرع محض بعد تمام القصد، وليس من صنيع التجار فلا ضرورة إليه. كذا في «التبيين».
And he acknowledges regarding a trust, usurpation, and debt; and he gifts a small amount of food; and he hosts the one whom he feeds; and he reduces from the price due to a defect as is customary. The author says: 'And he acknowledges...' because acknowledgment is among the necessities of trade; for if his acknowledgment were not valid, no one would deal with him. Likewise in al-Zayla'i. The author says: 'a small amount of food'; therein is an indication that it is not permissible to gift non-edibles at all, and the small gift refers to small hospitality, and it is measured by the capital of his trade. It is said: If his trade capital is, for example, ten thousand dirhams, and he hosts to the extent of ten, it is small; and if his trade capital is ten, for example, and he hosts to the extent of a daniq, then that would be considered large customarily. And the gift of food is like hospitality concerning it. The analogy is that none of this should be valid because it is a gratuitous act, but we left analogy in the case of a small amount because it is among the necessities of trade to attract the hearts of those dealing with the authorized trader. Likewise in Hashiyat al-Jalabi, quoting from al-Bayaniyyah. The author says: 'as is customary'; meaning, for example, what merchants reduce, because it is from their practice, as sometimes taking a defective item involves a reduction, unlike reduction without a defect, and reduction more than usual, because it is a pure gratuitous act after the completion of the transaction, and it is not from the practice of merchants, so there is no need for it. Likewise in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof, explaining the permissible gratuitous acts that a ma'dhun (a slave authorized to trade) may perform. The base ruling states that the ma'dhun may acknowledge (iqrar) regarding a trust, usurpation, or debt, gift a small amount of food, host someone, and reduce the price for a defect according to custom. The commentary clarifies that acknowledgment is necessary for trade because people would not transact with him otherwise. For the gift of food, the text specifies it must be small and measured relative to his trade capital; what is considered small varies with wealth. The underlying analogy (qiyas) would prohibit all gratuitous acts as they are pure charity, but the Hanafi school departs from analogy via istihsan for small amounts because they are necessary for trade to attract customers. Similarly, reduction for defect is permitted because it is customary among merchants and akin to taking the defective item with a reduction, whereas reducing without defect or excessively is a pure gift with no trade necessity.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave whom the master has permitted to engage in trade, thus authorized to perform acts necessary for commerce.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment of a right or liability, here regarding deposits, usurpation, or debts.
ضيافة (diyafah): Hospitality, specifically hosting a person with food, considered a small gratuitous act.
حط (hatt): Reduction in price due to a defect in the sold item, permissible if customary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the defect makes the sale invalid otherwise.
D) Because the ma'dhun owns the merchandise.

Key Takeaway
The ma'dhun may perform small gratuitous acts and customary reductions to facilitate trade, departing from qiyas via istihsan.
