Prohibited Acts for the Authorized Slave
Rulings on acts forbidden to the ma'dhun and the scope of permissible trade debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the acts prohibited for the ma'dhun and explain the Hanafi rationale using the 'a fortiori' (awlawi) argument.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun cannot perform acts beyond trade, even if compensated, because his authorization is limited to trade.

This lesson examines the boundaries of the authorized slave's capacity. The Hanafi school specifies that contracts like kitabah, emancipation, loans, and gifts are outside the scope of trade authorization, even when they involve compensation. The reasoning rests on the principle that what is not trade cannot be performed by one whose sole permission is for trade.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prohibited acts
List the four transactions that the ma'dhun is not permitted to perform: kitabah (manumission contract), emancipation (itq), loan (qard), and gift (hibah) even if with compensation.
These are not trade transactions.
2
Understand the rationale for each prohibition
Kitabah is not trade because it is exchange for freeing the slave, not for property. Emancipation without compensation is a pure gift, which he cannot do. Emancipation with compensation is even greater than kitabah. Loan and gift are also not trade.
The illa is that these acts are outside the definition of trade.
3
Analyze the awlawi argument for compensated emancipation
The commentator says: 'the one who does not own the lesser (kitabah), it is more so that he does not own the greater (compensated emancipation).' This is an a fortiori argument (qiyas awlawi).
This reasoning is based on the hierarchy of transactions.
4
Recognize the scope of permissible debts
The ma'dhun can incur debts that arise directly from trade or its equivalents, such as sale, purchase, lease, and hire. These are within his authorization.
This clarifies the boundary of his capacity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكاتبه، ولا يعتق أصلاً، ولا يقرض، ولا يهب ولو بعوض. وقالوا: لا بأس للمرأة أن تتصدق بشيء يسير من بيت زوجها، كالرغيف مثلا، من بيت زوجها. هذه المسألة ليست من هذا الباب لكنها ذكرت للمناسبة، فإن المرأة مأذونة عادة بهذا. وكل دين وجب بتجارته، أو بما هو في معناها؛ كبيع وشراء، وإجارة واستئجار.
قوله: ولا يكاتبه؛ أي لا يجوز للعبد المأذون أن يكاتب عبده؛ لأنه ليس بتجارة، إذ هي مبادلة المال بالمال والبدل في الكتابة مقابل لفك الحجر، فلم يكن تجارة إلا أن يجيزه المولى، ولا دين عليه؛ لأن المولى قد ملكه، ويصير العبد نائباً عنه. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله: ولا يعتق أصلاً؛ أي لا بمال ولا بغيره؛ لأن الثاني تبرع محض، وهو لا يملكه، والأول تصرف يفوق الكتابة، فمن لا يملك الأدنى، فأولى أن لا يملك الأعلى.
قوله: فإن المرأة; وإن كان مسوقاً لبيان مناسبته ذكر هاهنا، لكنه في الحقيقة تعليل لقول المصنف: ولا بأس ... الخ. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».
قوله: كبيع وشراء... الخ; نظير قوله: دين وجب بتجارته, [قوله]: إجارة واستئجاره، نظير قوله: أو بما هو في معناه, وصورة وجوب الدين بالبيع: هو أن يبيع.
And he does not enter into a kitabah contract with him, nor emancipate at all, nor lend, nor gift even with compensation. And they said: There is no harm for a woman to give charity of a small thing from her husband's house, like a loaf of bread, from her husband's house. This issue is not from this chapter but it is mentioned for appropriateness, for the woman is customarily authorized in this. And every debt that arises from his trade, or from what is like it, such as sale and purchase, lease and hire.
The author says: 'Nor does he enter into kitabah'; i.e., it is not permissible for the authorized slave to contract kitabah with his slave; because it is not trade, as trade is exchange of property for property, and the consideration in kitabah is in exchange for removing the impediment of slavery, so it is not trade unless the master permits it, and there is no debt upon him; because the master owns him, and the slave becomes his agent. Likewise in al-Hidayah.
The author says: 'Nor emancipate at all'; i.e., neither with compensation nor without; because the latter is a pure gratuitous act, and he does not own the act, and the former is a transaction that surpasses kitabah; so the one who does not own the lesser, it is more so that he does not own the greater.
The author says: 'For the woman'; although it is set forth to explain its appropriateness mentioned here, it is in fact a rationale for the author's statement: 'There is no harm...' Likewise in Hashiyat al-Jalabi.
The author says: 'Such as sale and purchase...' etc.; it is parallel to his statement: 'debt that arises from his trade', [the author says]: 'lease and hire', parallel to his statement: 'or from what is like it', and the scenario of debt arising from sale is: that he sells.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage enumerates acts that the ma'dhun (authorized slave) is prohibited from performing, followed by a side ruling about a woman's charity. The ma'dhun cannot: (1) enter into a kitabah (manumission contract) with his own slave, because it is not a trade transaction; (2) emancipate a slave at all, either for compensation or gratuitously—gratuitous emancipation is a pure gift which he cannot do, and compensated emancipation is even greater than kitabah, so it is forbidden; (3) lend money; (4) make a gift, even if it is with compensation (like a gift in exchange for something). The commentary explains that these acts are beyond the scope of trade authorization. Then the text adds that a woman may give a small charity (like a loaf of bread) from her husband's house. This is not directly about the ma'dhun, but it is mentioned because women are customarily authorized in such small household charities. Finally, it clarifies that debts arising from the ma'dhun's trade or similar transactions (e.g., sale, purchase, lease) are valid and binding. The commentary elaborates that the ma'dhun acts as an agent for the master in these debts.

Key Terms
مكاتبة (mukatabah): A contract of manumission where the slave pays the master in installments for freedom.
عتق (itq): Emancipation of a slave.
قرض (qard): Loan; giving something with the obligation to return a like amount.
هبة (hibah): Gift; a gratuitous transfer of property.
مأذون (ma'dhun): Slave who is given permission by his master to engage in trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following acts is the ma'dhun permitted to perform without explicit permission from his master?
A) Emancipating a slave for a sum of money.
B) Gifting a small item to a customer to encourage business.
C) Selling goods from the shop at a profit.
D) Entering into a kitabah contract with another slave.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun's authorization is strictly limited to trade; non-trade transactions like loans, gifts, kitabah, and emancipation are invalid unless the master permits them.

Woman's Small Charity from Husband's House
Rulings on a woman's small charity from her husband's house based on customary authorization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a woman giving small charity from her husband's house and explain the role of custom (urf) in establishing permissibility.

Core Idea
Custom permits a woman to give small household items in charity without explicit husband permission.

This lesson addresses a common household scenario: a woman wanting to give a small item like a loaf of bread from her husband's house to charity. The Hanafi school, citing customary practice, permits this despite the general rule that a wife does not have unlimited authority over her husband's property. The ruling is based on 'urf (custom) and the husband's implicit consent.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its context
The text states: 'There is no harm for a woman to give charity of a small thing from her husband's house, like a loaf of bread.' This is a permission (ibaha).
The ruling is an exception to the general principle that the wife cannot dispose of her husband's property without permission.
2
Understand the basis of the permission
The commentary explains that women are customarily (urf) authorized in such small gestures. Customary practice is recognized as a legal proof in Hanafi fiqh.
The husband's silence or general tolerance is taken as implied permission.
3
Identify the condition of 'small amount'
The permission is restricted to 'something small' (shay' yasir). Large amounts would require explicit permission.
Smallness is determined by custom (urf) and the local context.
4
Note the scope and limitations
This ruling is specific to charity from the husband's house; it does not extend to other dispositions like gifts, loans, or waste.
The text explicitly says this issue is not from the chapter on the ma'dhun but is mentioned for appropriateness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يكاتبه، ولا يعتق أصلاً، ولا يقرض، ولا يهب ولو بعوض. وقالوا: لا بأس للمرأة أن تتصدق بشيء يسير من بيت زوجها، كالرغيف مثلا، من بيت زوجها. هذه المسألة ليست من هذا الباب لكنها ذكرت للمناسبة، فإن المرأة مأذونة عادة بهذا. وكل دين وجب بتجارته، أو بما هو في معناها؛ كبيع وشراء، وإجارة واستئجار.
قوله: ولا يكاتبه؛ أي لا يجوز للعبد المأذون أن يكاتب عبده؛ لأنه ليس بتجارة، إذ هي مبادلة المال بالمال والبدل في الكتابة مقابل لفك الحجر، فلم يكن تجارة إلا أن يجيزه المولى، ولا دين عليه؛ لأن المولى قد ملكه، ويصير العبد نائباً عنه. كذا في «الهداية».
قوله: ولا يعتق أصلاً؛ أي لا بمال ولا بغيره؛ لأن الثاني تبرع محض، وهو لا يملكه، والأول تصرف يفوق الكتابة، فمن لا يملك الأدنى، فأولى أن لا يملك الأعلى.
قوله: فإن المرأة; وإن كان مسوقاً لبيان مناسبته ذكر هاهنا، لكنه في الحقيقة تعليل لقول المصنف: ولا بأس ... الخ. كذا في «حاشية الجلبي».

Restrictions on the Ma'dhun's Transactions
Listing prohibited acts for the ma'dhun with a side ruling on women's charity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four transactions a ma'dhun may not perform and explain the legal principle limiting his authority to trade.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun's permission is for trade only—he cannot manumit, lend, gift, or make kitabah.

In Hanafi fiqh, a slave authorized to trade (ma'dhun) operates under strict limits. The classical text enumerates four prohibited acts that fall outside commercial activity, reinforcing that his permission extends only to trade-related transactions. The passage also includes a side ruling on a woman's small charity from her husband's house, likened to customary authorization.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four prohibited acts
From the text, list the four actions the ma'dhun is forbidden from performing: kitabah, manumission, lending, and gifting (even with compensation).
These are explicitly stated with 'لا' (not).
2
Understand the underlying principle
All four acts are non-commercial; they involve donation or emancipation. The ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade (buying and selling).
The text mentions 'بما هو في معناها' (what is in the meaning of trade) like sale.
3
Recognize the exception for trade debts
Debts arising from trade or similar transactions are binding on the master. This is implied by 'وكل دين وجب بتجارته'.
This confirms that trade-related acts are valid.
4
Examine the side ruling on women's charity
The text adds that a woman may give small charity from her husband's house, like a loaf of bread, based on customary authorization. The commentator notes this is not from the chapter but mentioned for appropriateness.
This shows how custom can create limited authorization.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The book of the authorized slave: its rulings. He may not make a kitabah contract with his slave, nor manumit at all, nor lend, nor gift even for compensation. Every debt that becomes obligatory through his trade or what is in its meaning, like sale and purchase. This issue is not from this chapter, but it was mentioned for appropriateness, for the woman is customarily authorized in this.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter on the authorized slave (al-ma'dhun). It enumerates four actions the ma'dhun is absolutely prohibited from doing: (1) writing a kitabah contract with his own slave, (2) manumitting any slave, (3) lending money, and (4) gifting anything, even if it is with compensation. These are all non-commercial acts; the ma'dhun's permission is limited to trade. The commentary then adds a side ruling that a woman may give small charity from her husband's house, like a loaf of bread, and notes this is placed here due to customary authorization, not because it belongs to the chapter.

Key Terms
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
يكاتبه (yukatibuhu): To make a kitabah contract with his own slave, promising freedom in exchange for payments.
عتق (itq): Manumission of a slave, whether for compensation or not.
قرض (qard): Lending money or goods, which is a benevolent contract.
هبة (hibah): Gifting, which is a transfer of ownership without compensation.

Key Takeaway

Debts of the Ma'dhun Attach to His Person
Inter-madhhab dispute on the liability of the ma'dhun's person for trade debts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the liability of the ma'dhun for trade debts and explain the dispute with Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The ma'dhun can be sold for trade debts because the debt attaches to his person, not just his earnings.

In Hanafi fiqh, an authorized slave (ma'dhun) can engage in trade with his master's permission. A critical question arises: if the slave incurs debts in the course of trade, can the creditors seize the slave himself to recover their money? This passage presents the Hanafi ruling that such debts attach to the slave's person (raqaba), allowing his sale, and contrasts it with the positions of Zufar and al-Shafi'i who restrict recovery to the slave's earnings.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Hanafi ruling
State clearly that according to the Hanafi school, all debts from trade (and similar transactions) attach to the ma'dhun's person, enabling his sale.
The debt attaches to the slave's raqaba.
2
2. Recognize the scope of debts
List the types of debts included: sale, purchase, lease, liability for deposit, usurpation, denied trust, and dower from intercourse with a purchased slave.
These are all considered 'in the meaning of trade'.
3
3. Contrast with the opposing view
Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that only the slave's earnings can be sold, not the slave himself. Their reasoning: the master's purpose is to gain new wealth, not lose existing property.
The opponent's dalil is based on the master's intention.
4
4. Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The Hanafi response is that the debt arises for the master's benefit (since trade profits go to the master), so it is fair to attach the debt to the slave's person to prevent harm to creditors.
دفعاً للضرر عن الناس – to prevent harm to people.
5
5. Determine the practical difference
Under Hanafi, the slave can be sold and the price distributed; under Shafi'i/Zufar, only earnings (and gifts) are taken, and the slave remains with the master.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكل دين وجب بتجارته، أو بما هو في معناها؛ كبيع وشراء وإجارة واستئجار، وغرم وديعة وغصب، وأمانة جحدها، وعقر وجب بوطء مشرية بعد الاستحقاق يتعلق برقبته يباع فيه، ويقسم ثمنه بالحصص، وبكسبه حصل قبل الدين أو بعده وبما انهب هذا عندنا، وقال زفر والشافعي: لا يباع هو في الدين لكن يباع كسبه؛ لأن غرض المولى تحصيل مال لم يكن لا فوت ما قد كان ولنا: أن الدين ظهر في حق المولى، فيتعلق برقبته دفعاً للضرر عن الناس.
And every debt that becomes obligatory through his trade, or what is in its meaning, like sale, purchase, lease, hire, liability for a deposit, usurpation, a trust that is denied, and dower that becomes obligatory through intercourse with a purchased slave after a claim of entitlement, attaches to his person (raqaba) such that he is sold for it, and his price is distributed proportionally, and also attaches to his earnings whether acquired before or after the debt, and to what is gifted to him. This is according to us. And Zufar and al-Shafi'i said: He himself is not sold for the debt, but his earnings are sold, because the master's purpose is to acquire wealth that was not there, not to lose what he already had. And our evidence: that the debt materializes in relation to the master, so it attaches to his person to prevent harm to people.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute. The Hanafi position is that all debts arising from the ma'dhun's trade or similar transactions (e.g., sale, lease, liability for lost deposit, usurpation, denied trust, or dower from intercourse with a purchased slave) attach to the ma'dhun's physical person (raqaba). This means the ma'dhun can be sold to satisfy the debt, and his sale price is distributed among creditors proportionally. Additionally, his earnings and gifts are also subject to seizure. This is the position of Abu Hanifa and his school ('indana). In opposition, Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the ma'dhun himself cannot be sold for the debt; only his earnings can be sold. Their reasoning is that the master's objective in giving permission is to acquire new wealth, not to lose his existing property (the slave). The Hanafi response (lana) is that since the debt arises in the interest of the master (as the trade benefits him), it attaches to the slave's person to prevent harm to creditors. This tarjih aims to protect the public interest.

Key Terms
رقبة (raqabah): The physical person of the slave, as an asset that can be sold to cover debts.
كسب (kasb): Earnings acquired by the ma'dhun through his trade.
غرم (ghurm): Liability or compensation for loss, e.g., from a deposit that is lost.
عقر (aqr): Dower (mahr) owed due to intercourse with a purchased slave under a claim of ownership.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, can the authorized slave (ma'dhun) himself be sold to pay off debts from his trade?
A) No, only his earnings can be sold
B) Yes, he can be sold
C) Only if the master agrees
D) Only for debts of dower

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that trade debts of an authorized slave attach to his person, allowing his sale, to protect creditors.
